This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited Pakistan in May 1994. It comprised 14
members representing IDA, the British Overseas Development Administration and the United Nations Population Fund.
Representatives of the Population Council worked closely with the mission. Separate population missions from the
German Kreditanstalt fuir Wiederaufbau and the Asian Development Bank were associated with the mission's work.
The IDA team comprised Messrs./Mesdames C. Walker (Mission Leader), Bashir ul Haq (Health Advisor, Resident
Mission, Pakistan), Siraj ul Haq (Health Planner, Consultant), J. Herm (Management Specialist, Consultant), J.
Hohnen (Public Health Specialist), T. Merrick (Senior Population Advisor), I. Morris (Human Resources Specialist),
A. Quisumbing (Economist), Z. Sathar (Population Specialist, Consultant), and L. Vassiliou (Economist, Consultant).
In addition, Messrs./Mesdames A. Duncan (Senior Economist), R. Osborn (Population Specialist), S. van der Tak
(Economist) and D. Thomas (Population Specialist) contributed substantially during project preparation. Project
processing was supervised by Mr. P. Isenman (Director, SAl) and Ms. B. Herz (Manager, SAlPH). The Peer
Reviewers for the project were Messrs. T. Merrick (Senior Population Advisor, PHN), 0. Pannenborg (Manager,
AFIPH) and S. Sudhakar (Senior Population Specialist, SAIPH).



ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
Program and Credit Summary
Borrower:                  Islamic Republic of Pakistan
Beneficiaries:             Province of Balochistan
Province of North-West Frontier
Province of Punjab
Province of Sindh
Azad Janunu & Kashmir
Federally administered areas & territories
Federal Ministry of Population Welfare
Amount:                    SDR 45.0 million (US$65.1 million equivalent)
Terms:                     Standard, with 35 years maturity
Program Description
Pakistan's population growth of about 3 percent annually - faster than any other large Asian country
- undermines its efforts to relieve poverty and achieve sustainable development. High fertility is part
of a web of poor social indicators in Pakistan, tied to slow progress in the social sectors as well as
poverty. The best way to slow population growth will be to build demand for smaller families through
improvements in education and economic opportunity, especially for women, and in children's survival
prospects, while supplying better family planning information and services so that couples can more easily
limit fertility. Family planning will then become a more appealing option. Pakistan's Social Action
Program, which covers primary education, primary health care, population, and rural water supply, will
address both "demand" and "supply" aspects of family planning. This proposed population project will
contribute mainly by strengthening the supply of family planning information and services, to meet the
demand that already exists. It will thus complement other SAP programs and projects in education and
health as well as broader efforts to promote economic opportunity that will generate further demand. The
project would cover a three-year segment (1995/96-1997/98) of the Population Welfare Program of the
Eighth Five-Year Plan (1993/94-1997/98) and continue into the first year (1998/99) of the Ninth Plan.
IDA has no population project in Pakistan today, and therefore the Association is only presently assisting
population activities through the Social Action Program Project (SAPP).' The proposed project would
help the Government achieve its population policy objective of reducing fertility, thereby slowing
population growth and improving the health of women and children. More immediately, the proposed
project would help increase contraceptive prevalence by improving the quality and availability of family
planning services and information to meet and reinforce existing demand. The Government has set
ambitious targets for the Eighth Plan Period -- to expand service coverage to 100 percent in urban areas
and 70 percent in rural areas, to nearly double contraceptive use, and to reduce fertility by 10 percent.
The proposed project would support the entire national population program through financing that is
' Pakistan Social Action Program Project, Credit No. 2593-PAK, approved by the Board on March 31, 1994.



(ii)
linked to a sequenced policy and implementation reform program agreed between the Government and
IDA. The intention is to help the GOP implement its population program as a whole, by addressing
programmatic implementation problems and facilitating the policy environment. The project will help
Pakistan to: (a) plan and implement policy reforms to improve the effectiveness of population activities;
(b) boost the quality and availability of family planning services, which are now very scarce and usually
not of good quality; (c) strengthen existing weak population information, education, and communications;
(d) expand the training of personnel involved in family planning; (e) improve the management and
supervision capacity of the federal Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW) and provincial Population
Welfare Departments (PWDs); (f) strengthen links between population and other sectors including health,
education, and women's development; and (g) involve the private sector (including NGOs) more in family
planning. The project would reinforce a "client perspective" by emphasizing the need to provide choice
and quality and to involve communities and users in the provision of family planning. The principal
innovation in this project is an emphasis on community-based outreach in rural areas through Village
Family Planning Workers, drawn from the communities they serve. These workers will discuss and
deliver family planning services and information to women in their homes and communicate the clients'
needs and priorities to program managers. The project will be reviewed annually to check progress on
the agreed policy and implementation reform measures and to ensure appropriate development expenditure
consistent with implementation capacity and with available financing. It will be mainly implemented by
the MPW and PWDs.
Benefits and Risks
The project will contribute to a major expansion in family planning services to increase Pakistan's low
contraceptive prevalence rate and decrease its very high fertility and population growth rates. Services
will be of a higher quality, more available, and of increased effectiveness. The project will also improve
support of population objectives by other line departments and the private sector through the increased
number of health and other agencies' facilities offering family planning services, increased provision of
population education in schools, expanded social marketing of contraceptives, and a doubling of NGO
activities. The government will benefit from the various institutional development components,
particularly the management, planning and technical skill enhancement activities. Lastly, through lower
fertility and population growth, the project will facilitate the achievement of human development and
poverty alleviation, improve the health and reduce the mortality of mothers and children, improve
women's status, and reduce environmental pressures.
The main risk is that government support for the population program will fade because of political or
religious pressures or because results are slow in coming. Political support for the program is currently
high, and the inclusion of population prominently in the SAPP has helped strengthen political resolution.
The enhanced communications program will also help address this risk. But this will continue to be a
difficult area. The second risk is that the implementation agencies lack capacity to execute the program
and that the program approach might tax this limited capacity even further. This is being addressed by
management training, and sustained technical support, by decentralization and increased participation, by
detailed development work, and by a continuing program of operations research. The joint monitoring
of progress, program development workshops, and annual agreements will also make important
contributions. Lack of accountability from the provinces, the health authorities and other agencies
concerned for the success of the program, is the third risk. This will be reduced by the progress in
decentralization already achieved, and the increased efforts to improve interdepartmental coordination
under the SAPP. Finally, there is also the risk that the private sector might be inadequately involved.
Completing the establishment of an independent Trust to channel support to NGOs would greatly reduce



(iii)
this risk; in future, commercial companies involved in the program will have increased autonomy over
trading decisions. Although the project is risky, the risks seem eminently worth taking in view of the
potential for raising per capita living standards, improving the status of women and producing substantial
macroeconomic benefits.
Poverty Category: Program of targeted interventions
The Project supports the entire national population program, which plans to increase dramatically the
coverage and quality of family planning services throughout the country, but especially in rural and poor
urban areas. Poor women and their children will benefit most - particularly through new outreach
programs in rural areas.

ISLAMIC REPUBLIC OF PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. SECTOR BACKGROUND, ISSUES, CONSTRAINTS
1.    The Government of Pakistan has asked the International Development Association (IDA) to help
finance its national Population Welfare Program (PWP) as part of Pakistan's Eighth Five-Year Plan
(1993/94 to 1997/98). Designed to reduce fertility, the PWP should also improve the health of Pakistan's
women and children.
2.    Pakistan's population is growing at a rate of about 3 percent a year - higher than that of any
other large Asian country. This rapid growth undermines its efforts to relieve poverty and achieve
sustainable development. Part of a web of poor social indicators in Pakistan, high fertility is tied to slow
progress in improving the social sectors and to persistent and extensive poverty.
3.    To slow population growth, the Government proposes to build demand for smaller families by
improving educational and economic opportunities, especially for women. Through better health care,
it will improve children's survival prospects, which will also encourage parents to opt for smaller
families. Finally, it will offer better family planning information and services to enable couples to limit
fertility more easily. The objective of the PWP is, therefore, to increase contraceptive prevalence by
improving the quality and availability of family planning services, reducing the distance - cultural as
well as physical - that women need to cross to obtain services, and increasing the support of
communities (especially men) for family planning.
4.    The PWP is a crucial part of Pakistan's broader Social Action Program (SAP), currently being
implemented by the Government and supported by another IDA project, the Social Action Program
Project (SAPP).' Pakistan's Social Action Program (which covers primary education, primary health
care, population, and rural water supply) addresses in a general way both the demand and supply aspects
of family planning. The proposed Population Welfare Program Project (PWPP), which would begin after
two years of SAPP support to population, would contribute to this effort mainly by strengthening the
supply of family planning information and services to meet existing demand. By concentrating on these
few areas, it would complement other SAP programs and broader efforts to promote economic
opportunity. The PWPP would be more comprehensive and detailed than is possible under SAPP. There
is currently no IDA-supported project in population. It is fully consistent with IDA's Country Assistance
Strategy for Pakistan.2
5.    The PWP's main goal is to promote the idea of the small family as the norm and to provide
couples with the means to achieve this norm. It proposes to reduce fertility by emphasizing the link
' Pakistan Social Action Program Project, Credit No. 2593-PAK, approved by the Board on March 31, 1994.
2 Discussed in the Board on March 31, 1994.



2
between family planning and maternal and child health, by helping to focus on the underlying factors that
presently inhibit demand for family planning in Pakistan. These include the low status of women; their
low levels of education and participation in the labor force, which combine to make the opportunity cost
of their time abnormally low; the pervasive cultural bias toward universal marriage and large families;
lack of husband and community support for family planning; and limited access to social services.
6.    The SAP and supporting SAPP both seek to strengthen performance in basic social sectors during
the Eighth Plan by improving implementation (through better budgeting, staffing, administrative
decentralization, and beneficiary participation); by improving project design (to expand access and
improve quality); and by increasing the level of effort (especially Government funding). The population
component of the SAP includes a commitment to higher funding levels and better budgeting procedures.
These budgetary commitments are matched by policy, program, and administrative reforms in critical
areas, such as a substantial new outreach program, an improved information and education campaign
directed toward men as well as women, stronger links with the provincial Departments of Health, more
decentralization of the program to the provinces, better staffing and training, and a more reliable
contraceptive supply system.
7.    IDA's proposed Population Welfare Program Project (PWPP) -  a Sector Investment and
Maintenance Credit - would build on, strengthen, and be strengthened by these SAPP reforms. It would
provide support for four years - the last three years of the five-year PWP for the Eighth Five Year Plan
(the first two years of which are being partly financed through the SAPP) plus the first year of the Ninth
Five Year Plan. In this way, IDA would be supporting the entire national population program within an
overall framework agreed upon by the Government and IDA. Financing would be linked to a sequenced
program of policy and implementation reforms tied to annual reviews.
8.    The proposed project would be an integral part of the total population program. It would focus
particularly on institution-building (including better systems, staffing, human resource development, and
monitoring). The project would cover:
*     Formulating and implementing policy reforms to improve the effectiveness of population
activities,
*     Improving the quality and availability of family planning services by emphasizing client
orientation and beneficiary participation,
*     Expanding outreach and improving the range and availability of contraceptives,
*     Strengthening information, education, and communication about population  and family
planning,
*     Expanding the training of personnel involved in family planning,
*     Increasing  the  involvement of the private sector (including  nongovernmental
organizations) in family planning,
*     Strengthening links between population and other sectors (such as health and education)
and efforts (such as women's development),
*     Strengthening the management and supervisory capacity of the Federal Ministry of
Population Welfare (MPW) and provincial Population Welfare Departments (PWDs).
9.    It is estimated that the Government of Pakistan's five-year Population Welfare Program (1993/94
to 1997/98) would cost US$ 251.4 million equivalent, and the four-year program (1995/96 to 1998/99)



3
would cost US$287.6 million equivalent.3  The proposed project would finance US$65.1 million
equivalent of the four-year time slice. Other donors (mainly through their ongoing projects) would finance
about US$61.5 million equivalent during this period, and the Government will finance the balance, some
US$161.0 million equivalent.
A. Background
10.    With a population of over 120 million, Pakistan is the eighth most populous country in the
world.4 Poverty is widespread, with the poorest 40 percent of households spending US$100 per month
or less. Although there has been some improvement since Independence, life expectancy remains low,
maternal and child mortality remains high, and infectious and communicable diseases are common.5
Although fertility rates have declined somewhat, the decline has been slow, especially among the poor,
relative to that in other South Asian countries.
11.    A slow but steady decline in mortality and little change in fertility have resulted in an estimated
population growth rate of about 3 percent a year - the highest of any large-population country in Asia
(Annex IA, Table 1). At this rate, Pakistan's population will double every twenty three years. (Indeed,
much of the growth in the next decade or so is now unavoidable, given that the next cohorts of mothers
have already been born). While neighboring countries - such as Bangladesh, India, and Iran - have
had some success in lowering fertility rates, Pakistan has not. If this trend is to be reversed in the near
future, it is urgent to increase women's education and improve family planning service delivery now.
12.    Pakistan's high population growth rate imposes a major constraint on economic and social
development. Although real GDP growth has averaged around 6 percent a year for more than a decade,
because of the growth in population, per capita income growth has been only half that. Efforts to reduce
poverty and improve standards of living are also substantially stymied by ever-increasing numbers.
13.    Massive growth in population also has serious macroeconomic consequences. Pakistan will have
to contend with higher public expenditures, or shortfalls in government services, or both. In the near
term, growing unemployment, underemployment, and low wages are almost inevitable. In the longer
run, it will be far easier to improve per capita living standards if population growth slows.  A
predominantly agricultural country dependent on scarce land and water resources, Pakistan needs to
become less dependent on agriculture by diversifying its economy. Diversification will require greater
investment and more education, particularly if labor productivity and wages are to rise. But demographic
pressures on public education and health push services to lower levels, perpetuating low labor
productivity. The resulting reduction in aggregate household savings constrains economic growth still
further. There are two options for the future: a large population with a lower standard of living or a
smaller population with a higher standard. Through its commitment to the SAP and PWP, the current
government (which assumed office in October 1993), has opted for the latter course.
3The cost for the four-year program (1995/96 to 1998/99) is higher than that for the five year program due to the rapid
expansion of the program.
4World Development Report 1994.
' See section on Health and Population Sector Status for more detailed statistics.



4
14.    Despite the high and sustained growth of around 6 percent a year, the economy has shown a
number of structural weaknesses. Since the late 1980s, moreover, it has been burdened by relatively high
fiscal deficits.
15.    In reaction to these imbalances, Pakistan has been implementing a medium-term  adjustment
program. During the adjustment period, Pakistan's economic growth and export performance have been
strong, and it has instituted structural reforms to encourage private activity. Since 1987/88, GDP growth
(at factor cost) has averaged some 5.5 percent a year, and real per capita GDP growth has risen by over
10 percent. Based on an expanded reform program put in place in August 1993 - and endorsed and
implemented by the newly elected Government beginning mid-October 1993 - Pakistan's economic
performance during the first six months of 1993/94 has shown a remarkable improvement, compared to
its slowdown in 1992/93.6 The fiscal deficit has been significantly reduced from 8 percent of GDP in
1992/93 to 5.8 percent in 1993/94. However, while the fiscal deficit was within its programmed level,
federal reserves were below target, reflecting lower than projected customs duties and sales taxes.
Resource mobilization will have to improve in order to meet competing demands on budgetary resources
for nondevelopment expenditures (such as interest and defense expenditures), which have limited the
Government's ability to alleviate infrastructure constraints, improve social conditions, and reduce poverty.
Nevertheless, progress in the implementation of the SAP, which IDA is supporting through the SAPP,
has been encouraging: Government has budgeted an amount for SAP in 1994/95 that will allow
expenditures to rise by more than 0. I percent of GDP over the budgeted level in 1993/94.
B. Health and Population Sector Status
16.    Despite some progress in urban areas, health indicators throughout the country remain poor. The
crude death rate, which fell by 40 percent over the past twenty-five years, stands now at 10 per 1,000.
Health problems are concentrated mainly in young children and their mothers, and child and maternal
mortality rates are high. The under-five mortality rate for girls in 1992 was 129 and for boys 142--
compared with averages of 102 and 114 in all low-income countries and 108 and 104 in neighboring
India.7 Male life expectancy in Pakistan is about 59 years (as compared to an average of 61 years for
all low-income countries); Pakistan's female life expectancy stands at 59 years (as compared to the low-
income country average of 63). This is a substantial improvement over the 1970s, when female life
expectancy in Pakistan was 47 years and men's 49 years. Yet, Pakistan's female life expectancy remains
one of the lowest in the world; for high-income economies, women generally outlive men by five or six
years.
17.    Pakistan's total fertility rate is estimated to be 5.6 births per woman - one of the highest in Asia,
and marriage is almost universal. Although the total fertility rate has started to decline in urban areas
6 See also Pakistan: Public Sector Adiustment Loan (Ln. 3645-PAK: Cr. 2542-PAK). Release of the Second Tranche.
Memorandum from the President, December 15, 1994; Pakistan: Economic Update: Recent Developments and Medium-Term
Policy Aeenda, February 28, 1994; Pakistan: Country Economic Memorandum FY93, Progress under the Adiustment Proeram
March 23, 1993 (Report No. 11590-PAK); and Rapid Population Growth in Pakistan: Concerns and Consequences, March 17,
1989 (Report No. 7522-PAK).
' World Development Report, 1994.



5
(where women are more educated), it remains at about 5.6 in the rural areas where 67 percent of
Pakistanis live.' The crude birth rate is about 41 per thousand population. The burden that this high
fertility places on mothers is reflected in a maternal mortality rate of roughly 500 per 100,000 live
births.9 By comparison, the maternal mortality rate is less than 10 in most industrial countries and less
than 100 in low-income countries with good primary health care systems (such as Sri Lanka and
Zimbabwe).'0 The toll on child survival taken by closely spaced births, poor nutrition, and poor public
services, shows in Pakistan's under-five child mortality rates, which are about 138 per 1,000 live births
- again, among the highest in the region (Annex 1A, Table 2).
18.    Family planning--promoting the use of contraceptives, breast feeding, and child-spacing--is the
most direct and practical way to lower fertility rates in the near term. In Pakistan it is clear that
substantial unmet demand for family planning already exists. In 1991, around 40 percent of married
women either wanted no more children (27.3 percent) or wanted to delay their next birth (12.5 percent).
(Annex IA, Table 3)." Among these women, only 11.8 percent were currently using contraceptives--
2.1 percent for spacing, and 9.8 percent for limiting. Thus, 28 percent of married women had an unmet
need for family planning, and of the total demand for family planning, only 30 percent was satisfied.
The fact that abortions still take place (even though they are strictly illegal and typically occur in
primitive and highly risky conditions) is further evidence of unmet demand for family planning.
Program and Policy Development
19.    Although Pakistan was one of the first developing countries to have a national population policy
and program, successive population programs have had limited impact on fertility (See Annex 3 for a
history of population programs in Pakistan and lessons learned from these programs). Demographic
targets were set but not met, in large part because political commitment was low, under-funding serious,
management weak, and changes in leadership and management frequent.
20.    Population policy in Pakistan has frequently changed direction. Between 1965 and 1973, the
program relied mainly on the use of traditional midwives (dais) to motivate the population, distribute
contraceptives, and refer clients to clinics for IUD insertion and sterilization. Between 1973 and 1977,
the emphasis was on a "continuous motivation system", implemented by (what were supposed to be) well-
trained couples rather than dais. The program languished from 1977 to 1980, when it was transferred
from the Ministry of Health and Social Affairs to the Ministry of Planning and given an infrastructure
completely separate from that for health. This change was effected at the urging of donors, including
IDA, who believed that it would lead to greater emphasis on population. Yet the program continued to
function at a very low level. Public health facilities generally failed to offer family planning services,
even though Government policy (since at least 1985) mandated that they do so.
£ See Table A2-1, Annex 2, which presents the wide range of TFR estimates from various surveys.
9 UNICEF, The Progress of Nations, 1993.
10 World Development Report, 1994.
" 1991 Pakistan Demographic & Health Survey (DHS).



6
21.   In 1990, the population program was strengthened somewhat when the Ministry of Population
Welfare (MPW) was created and given responsibility for all population matters. During 1991, the
Government undertook an Accelerated Program, and political support for family planning was more open.
The Government program included steps to expand service delivery and improve efficiency. Family
planning services were provided at an increasing number of health facilities. The number of fixed and
mobile sterilization units was expanded. Population education programs were enhanced and targeted
better. Field supervision was strengthened, and community involvement was sought.
22.   The population program of the Eighth Plan builds on and substantially strengthens initiatives taken
since 1990. The first two years of the Plan are supported through the SAPP. The new political
commitment is reflected in funding levels three times those in the Seventh Plan. A major new outreach
program is being developed to reach rural women who have so far been the group most under-served.
Greater efforts will be made to deliver family planning services from health outlets. Greater emphasis
is being placed on serving more of the urban population through the private sector (including NGOs,
registered medical practitioners, and the social marketing of contraceptives). A strengthened information,
education, and communications program should help to inform both men and women, and direct one-to-
one personal contact will be used to stimulate demand. A stronger management information system and
monitoring and evaluation program are being put in place at all levels, and service quality, reliability,
choice, and the user's perspective are being emphasized.
Organization and Management
23.    The public sector. Pakistan's national population program is federally funded and administered
by the Ministry of Population Welfare. Field programs are implemented by provincial Population
Welfare Departments (PWDs) for the four Provinces and by MPW for the federally administered areas
(AIK, FANA, ICT).'2 The federal MPW controls the overall program and manages the support
programs, while the four provincial authorities - through their PWDs (including their division, district,
and tehsil officials) manage service delivery. More specifically, the federal Government controls clinical
and nonclinical training, contraceptive procurement and distribution, social marketing, NGO coordination,
information, education, and communication research, logistics, and monitoring and evaluation.
24.   Most family planning service delivery is provincially implemented and includes Family Welfare
Centres and Reproductive Health Service Centres for clinical methods, especially sterilization. Family
Welfare Centres are staffed by a female paramedical and support staff.  Their outreach into the
community so far has been limited. Provincial PWDs' budgets are allocated by the federal Government
as part of the Annual Development Plan. Moves to decentralize programs and funding to the provinces
have recently made significant progress. Family planning services are also becoming available at health
outlets of the Departments of Health and other government institutions.
25.    The private sector. Despite Pakistan's large private medical sector, few private medical doctors
or clinics offer family planning services. The largest private sector provider of family planing services
is the Family Planning Association of Pakistan (FPAP), a nongovernment organization, whose integrated
approach was the model for MPW's current multisectoral strategy. The FPAP relies heavily on
volunteers, and, in 1986, served two-thirds again as many clients as MPW at about one-tenth the cost.
2 The program in FATA is adminiscered by the PWD of North-West Frontier Province.



7
26.    FPAP provides training for Family Welfare Workers and dais and delivers family planning and
maternal and child health services through about 100 Family Welfare Centres, Mobile Service Units and
community-based distribution schemes. It also cooperates with other NGOs, model clinics, hospitals, and
target groups. In addition, it is involved in income-generating projects and in literacy projects for
women.
27.   Other sizable NGOs that offer family planning services include the All Pakistan Women's
Association (APWA), Behbud, Pathfinder, and the Pakistan Voluntary Health and Nutrition Association
(PVHNA). Recent initiatives of MPW are aimed at increasing the involvement of the private commercial
sector - especially private medical practitioners (RMPs) and traditional practitioners (and homeopaths)
- in providing family planning services by giving them pre- and in-service training, and providing
contraceptive supplies.
28.   The Social Marketing of Condoms Project has been successful in reaching urban consumers. By
the end of the Seventh Plan, Sathi condoms were being sold in more than 33,000 outlets--chemists,
general stores and grocery and paan shops--and annual sales were around 45 million. The SMC Project,
through its promotion and marketing of Sathi condoms, has helped to desensitize the issue. Between 1986
and 1992, some 291 million Sathi condoms were sold. The Government is now planning to expand social
marketing principles to other contraceptive methods: oral pills, injectable contraceptives, and IUDs. The
latter methods will involve having private medical practitioners, doctors, and paramedicals provide
services. Even should it continue to supply condoms only, the SMC Project has the potential to supply
an even wider urban market at a relatively low cost.
29.   The NGO Coordinating Council (NGOCC) was established with Government assistance, to
facilitate the population-related work of NGOs. In addition to providing technical and administrative
help, the NGOCC has directly negotiated funding for NGO projects with donors. The NGOCC is
handicapped in its operations, however, by a legal and constitutional framework that undermines its
organizational stability. It also has limited technical and management capacity and no independent
finance.
30.   The NGOCC's instability and inflexibility of the organization has progressively undermined donor
confidence in it as a mechanism for promoting and developing family planning capacity among NGOs.
Efforts to strengthen the management systems through long-term technical assistance made significant
progress, which was then eroded by funding cuts and the relocation of the organization. NGO activity
supported through the NGOCC in 1993/94 fell substantially below performance in the previous year,
largely due to severe cutbacks in donor support.  To alleviate this financial constraint, the Ministry
provided funds in addition to its own regular contribution to make up, at least in part, for the shortfall
from donors. However, releases of government funds have been late and lumpy. While Government
funds have permitted some support to be maintained to static outlets and community-based distribution
projects, funding for surgical centers has been greatly reduced, while that for Family Welfare Centres
is only 34 percent of last year's level. The NGO situation was further complicated by the loss of almost
all experienced NGOCC staff following its transfer from Karachi to Islamabad in late 1993.
31.   Given the systematic weakness of NGOCC, the Government of Pakistan has established a National
Trust for Population Welfare (NATPOW) to replace it. NATPOW is an autonomous NGO, with an
initial Government endowment of Rs 100 million.



8
Service Provision and Utilization
32.    Service delivery.  MPW  has its own clinics and service delivery system, which are largely
independent of Government health facilities. The approach is multisectoral. Core programs include
family planning service delivery and outreach (including sterilization services), population information,
clinical and nonclinical staff training, and contraceptive and supplies logistics. Supporting programs
include those administered with other Government departments, or through nongovernmental
organizations (NGOs) and the Contraceptive Social Marketing Program.
33.    The primary service delivery outlets of the MPW consist of 1,296 Family Welfare Centres whose
primary function is to deliver nonsurgical services (Annex IA, Table 4).' Surgical contraception is
provided through 218 Reproductive Health Service Centres in government and private hospitals, of which
79 are MPW/PWD Reproductive Health Service Centres.
34.    Under the Population Welfare Program a major new outreach program has begun that will
involve the fielding of female village-based family planning workers in all 12,000 villages that have a
population of at least 2,000. Other efforts to improve the delivery of contraceptives to the community
include retraining male Family Welfare Assistants and expanding information, education, and
communication activities to promote increased community support for family planning.
35.    Through the Social Marketing of Contraceptives Project the MPW  also supports a substantial
number of NGO operations and the commercial distribution of contraceptives. Clinical training - mostly
for service-delivery staff - is provided through a network of twelve Regional Training Institutes.
Training in management and the nonclinical aspects of the program - such as information, education,
and communication skills and the development of local community involvement -  is given at three
Population Welfare Training Institutes.
36.    Recent initiatives are underway to coordinate national efforts better with those of provincial line
departments outside the population sector. As of now, 2,619 health outlets of the DOH, 153 facilities
of other provincial line departments, and 200 Target Group Institutions provide family planning services.
There are also recent plans to start deploying a large number of female health outreach workers who
would also provide certain limited family planning services. These workers would be posted in villages
without a Village Family Planning Worker, to avoid overlapping of tasks and catchment areas. Pakistan's
very small HIV/AIDS program is administered through the Ministry of Health, and the population
program's present involvement with it is limited.
37.    Service coverage.  Taken together, all of Pakistan's family planning efforts make services
accessible to less than a quarter of the population. MPW covers about 10 to 12 percent of the population
and other organizations another 13 to 15 percent. Such low coverage is partly responsible for the low
contraceptive prevalence. The number of clients per facility, however, varies widely across and within
provinces (Annex IA, Tables 5 and 6a to 6d) and between urban and rural areas. In urban areas, MPW
estimates that service coverage is about 54 percent. In rural areas, it is only 5 percent. Population-to-
facility ratios throughout Pakistan (except for the Federal Areas) are more than double or even triple the
planning target of 30,000:1. Centers are thinly spread, and about 60 percent of them offer only a very
restricted range of services. Especially for the rural population, moreover, accessibility to these facilities
13 The figures cited above are correct as of June 1994. For a breakdown of service delivery outlets, see Annex IA, Table 4.



9
is limited . The average walking distance to a Family Welfare Centre ranges from 9.3 kilometers in the
Punjab to over 100 kilometers in parts of Balochistan, which means substantial time and travel costs for
most potential clients. Few clients, rural or urban, are willing to travel more than 5 kilometers to a
center, and fewer than 5 percent of actual users travel more than 10 kilometers.'4
38.     Quality of services. The problem of coverage is compounded by the uneven quality of services
available at existing centers. A recent study of Family Welfare Centres throughout Pakistan revealed
that some were, in fact, closed while others operated only intermittently.'5  About one-fifth of these
centers lacked examination facilities, and many did not offer adequate privacy in examination and
counselling  areas.   Family  Welfare Workers, furthermore, often had  misperceptions about the
appropriateness of specific contraceptives, and thus misprescribed them to clients. From one-tenth to one-
fifth of Family Welfare Centers did not carry an adequate mix of contraceptive methods, and some outlets
had run out of supplies altogether.
39.    Poor service quality in government clinics deters new users and discourages the continuing use
of contraceptives. By contrast, NGOs that assure high quality of services have been able to achieve
contraceptive prevalence rates of 25 percent to 45 percent in their catchment areas, against a national
average of 12 percent. These NGOs involve the community. Using doctors, nursing supervisors and
social workers to head groups of five to seven team members, this approach helps to ensure quality by
guaranteeing follow-up, especially when mobile service units are used and field visits are prescheduled.'6
40.    Method mix. Service quality for family planning means providing a wide range of contraceptives
methods and providing clients with enough information to choose the method most appropriate for them.
Women at different reproductive stages would need different family planning methods, and it is important
to provide adequate variety in contraceptive choice for this differentiated clientele.  Government
calculations of births averted, show that the PWP presently relies on IUDs (42 percent of births averted
as of 1992/93), condoms (22 percent), and contraceptive surgery (21 percent). Oral pills and injectables
each account for only 7 percent, with the balance accounted for by foam and other methods (Annex IA,
Table 7).17
41.    A study of Family Welfare Centres indicates that more younger women would opt for family
planning if more information were provided to them about reversible methods used to space births.'8
14 Increased accessibility to family planning outlets - or community-based distribution centers - has been shown to increase
contraceptive use in many countries. In Pakistan, in both rural and urban areas, awareness and use of FWC's declines sharply
with increasing distance from an FWC (World Bank 1989).
"5 The Population Council recently conducted a situation analysis of 100 FWC's chosen to represent all 1,288 FWC's operating
in Pakistan in 1993. See Cemada, Rob, Ahmeen, and Ahmed, "1993-94 Public Family Planning Service Delivery in
Pakistan," Intemational Quarterly of Community Health Education, Vol. 14 (1), pp. 21-52.
16 M. A. Kareem Iqbal and S. Shujaat H. Zaidi, 1992 Diaenostic Study of Maior NGOs in Family Planning, Non-Govenmment
Organization Coordinating Council for Population Welfare, sponsored by the Population Council, Islamabad.
' Data on births averted from PC-I. Population Welfare Proeramme, 1993-98. Federal Activity, Ministry of Population
Welfare, Govermnent of Pakistan, Islamabad.
" Ministry of Population Welfare, Monitoring and Statistics Wing, February 2, 1994. Family Welfare Centers Vicinity Study,
1993, Draft Report.



10
As of now, more than 40 percent of married women aged thirty and above have used contraceptives at
some time, but only 17 percent of married women aged 15 to 19 have done so. Under the present
system, the Government imposes very modest charges for condoms, oral pills, and injectable
contraceptives, IUDs are free, and small compensation payments are made for contraceptive surgery.
42.    Use of services. Although maternal and child health and family planning visits to Family Welfare
Centres have been growing (from one family planning client per day in 1982/83 to an average of 5 per
day in 1992), eight out of ten clinics serve fewer than 150 family planning clients monthly. To make
family planning service delivery more cost-effective, use rates need to increase.'9
43.    Clients often go to Family Welfare Centres for help with general ailments (including problems
of maternal and child health), suggesting that, progranmmatically, family planning is more integrated with
maternal and child health than is implied by the present administrative structure. While maternal and
child health services are valuable in their own right, they could also provide an entry for family planning,
and such field-level integration is useful. As of the 1990-91 Pakistan Demographic and Health Survey
(PDHS), however, Family Welfare Centres accounted for only 13 percent of modern family planning
methods obtained by clients; government hospitals or clinics provided 42 percent, and the private sector
30 percent. Yet, demand for family planning services in Pakistan is high and largely unmet.
44.    Information, education, and communications activities.  To date, Pakistan's information,
education, and communication strategies have had limited success in increasing public awareness of family
planning or demand for small families. Since the 1980s, the PWP has been using national and regional
radio and television broadcasts regularly to promote the concept of a small family norm and to
disseminate information on family planning, maternal and child health, and breastfeeding. These
electronic mass-media activities are supplemented by printed matter, mobile audio-visual vans, and face-
to-face encounters. However, these efforts have succeeded in reaching only one in five married
women.' Information, education, and communication activities are particularly needed to inform people
about family planning outlets within local communities. A recent study found that only slightly more than
half (54 percent) of currently married women in the vicinity of a Family Welfare Centre knew it existed,
and of these, only two-thirds knew its location.2' Moreover, only 44 percent of married women of
reproductive age in the vicinity knew that family planning services were available at the Family Welfare
Centre.
Staffing and Training
45.    Until recently, staffing patterns and work plans for Family Welfare Centres, (the primary service
outlet of the PWP) have not seriously addressed the need for outreach beyond linking with Traditional
Birth Attendants in nearby areas. Some Family Welfare Workers and their assistants, female Family
9 The earlier data are from March 17, 1989 Rapid Population Growth in Pakistan: Concerns and ConseQuences, Report No.
7522-PAK. The more recent data are from Cernada et al., 1994, A Situation Analysis of Public Family Planning Service
Delivery in Pakistan.
20 Pakistan Demographic and Health Survey 1991.
21 Ministry of Population Welfare, Monitoring and Statistics Wing, February 2, 1994 Family Welfare Centers Vicinity Study
1993, Draft Report.



I1
Welfare Assistants, are reluctant to travel alone outside the centers to visit households. But even if more
were willing to travel, the budget for travel and daily allowances is insufficient. Management has only
recently been willing to consider the tradeoff of increasing client contacts by closing centers at certain
times to allow for outreach work. But while the duties of the female Family Welfare Assistants now
include regular home visits in the vicinity of the center, many new centers do not have women Family
Welfare Assistants. In some areas, Mobile Service Units have been set up in association with Family
Welfare Centres to provide outreach. Male Family Welfare Assistants on the Family Welfare Centre staff
are more mobile but are unable to work with women. Recent initiatives that aim to increase their role
in outreach motivation and logistic support base them at the local PWD office or health facility rather
than at the Family Welfare Centre. The scarcity of female clinical workers (doctors or Family Welfare
Counsellors) in supervisory positions contributes to the present ineffectiveness of the supervision of field
personnel. Follow-up is further complicated because staff are split along gender lines.
46.    A network of twelve Regional Training Institutes provides clinical training and three Population
Welfare Training Institutes provide nonclinical training for the PWP. In the Regional Training Institutes,
paramedicals in the PWP (Family Welfare Workers and Assistants), trainers, and NGO paramedicals
receive training in clinical contraception, maternal and child care, and counseling. Population Welfare
Training Institutes train program personnel, employees of other line departments, and community-based
groups in organizational development and communications. (The Regional Training Institutes presently
provide training in family planning to clinical staff of other line departments - particularly of the DOH
and of NGOs).
47.    Apart from basic training, the Regional Training Institutes provide refresher training to Family
Welfare Workers and Assistants and upgrade their training to enable them to work in Mobile Service
Units and as trainers and supervisors of Village Family Planning Workers. This last task, in particular,
would add greatly to the workload of the Regional Training Institutes. Either the capacity of Regional
Training Institutes must be increased or the way refresher courses are provided changed. It is generally
recognized that the Regional Training Institutes are one of the most successful aspects of the PWP,
largely due to their standard setting, and curriculum, and staff development. The Population Welfare
Training Institute system, on the other hand, is seen as a weaker part of the program, that does not meet
provincial needs for management and other nonclinical training.
Expenditure and Financing
48.    Historically, Pakistan's public expenditure on population has been both modest and fluctuating.'
During the 1980s, expenditure on population averaged 0.06 percent of GNP and never exceeded 0.07
percent. As a share of total public expenditure, expenditure on population averaged 0.24 percent during
1983-1992--less than 10 percent of the total expenditure on health. Approximately two-thirds of these
funds were spent on provincial programs.  Chronic underfunding of the population program  has
constrained its prospects for expanding outreach and improving service delivery. Moreover, while
administration and salaries are protected, nonsalary recurrent budgets - for supplies, transport,
information and education - still tend to be inadequate.
49.    The foreign aid component of population expenditures has varied considerably. In the Fifth Plan,
the contribution of foreign assistance was 19 percent; in the Sixth, 53 percent; and in the Seventh, 40
2' See Chapter m and Annex 1B for a more detailed discussion.



12
percent. USAID has been the most important source of foreign assistance, accounting for 65 percent and
87 percent of total assistance in the Sixth and Seventh Plan periods, respectively. After USAID funding
was phased out in the early 1990s, foreign assistance contributions dropped. In 1993, foreign assistance
covered just 25 percent of population program expenditures.
50.    Since 1990/91, the population program  has attracted an increasing share of government
expenditure. During most of the Seventh Plan period, actual expenditures exceeded the plan allocations
- again reflecting the growing priority Pakistan places on population. (The exception was 1992/93,
when the withdrawal of USAID funding resulted in a decline in the total program size. Nevertheless,
Government funding for the program increased substantially during that year.)
51.    The Eighth Five Year Plan places an even stronger emphasis on population, and Government
expenditure is expected to reach 0. 15 percent of GNP by 1995/96 and on through 1997/98 - a figure
more than double the current level. The additional funds will target nonadministrative activities that have
been seriously underfunded heretofore. From 56 percent in 1993, the share of expenditures on program
services is planned to rise to 73 percent by 1998. In contrast, administration's share of total expenditures
will decrease to 15 percent by 1998 from  26 percent in 1993.  Approximately 62 percent of the
expenditures for the Population Welfare Program will take place at the provincial Government level, and
35 percent at the federal level.
Past and Current Assistance in the Sector
52.    The Social Action Program  Project (SAPP).  Though the proposed Eighth Plan Population
Welfare Program is a substantial improvement over earlier plans, serious problems remain - many of
them common to all social sector programs in Pakistan. The Social Action Program Project (SAPP),
which will support a three-year time slice of the Government Social Action Program (SAP), contains a
major initiative to address outstanding intersectoral issues. The SAP covers policy, budgetary and
implementation reform in primary education, rural water supply and sanitation, primary health care, and
population that will link annual financial allocations to performance. It addresses such endemic problems
as the poor design of social services, deficient implementation, and a consistently low level of effort.
Emphasis is given to improving service delivery and increasing community involvement.
53.      The population component of the SAPP and the proposed Population Welfare Program Project
(PWPP) were developed in parallel. The first two years of the PWP would be partly financed through
the population component of the SAPP. The proposed PWPP would then begin in the last year of the
SAPP (1995/96) and continue support for the national Population Welfare Program as a whole. It would
also provide a transition into the first year of the Ninth Plan.
54.    Other IDA projects.  The design of the proposed project draws on the experience of earlier
projects. Although an IDA-assisted population project, completed in December 1989,23 achieved few
of its goals in institutional development or family planning, it demonstrated such lessons as the
importance of sustained Government commitment to the program; of realism about institutional constraints
on implementation (such as difficulties arising from the distribution of program responsibilities between
federal and provincial authorities); of a project being firmly grounded in solid sector work; of the need
2 Pakistan Population Proiect (Credit no, 1350-PAK), with IDA financing of US$18.0 million, approved in April 1983. A
Project Completion Report (Report No.9262-PAK) was circulated in January 1991.



13
to avoid a piece-meal approach (including limiting the number of PC-Is), and of the need for staff
continuity in both the Government and IDA.
55.    These lessons have been taken into account. In the proposed project, information programs for
opinion leaders were designed to enhance a level of political commitment that is already much higher than
it has ever been. Program content and reforms were designed within a realistic implementation schedule.
Local involvement is extensive, and measures have been included to reform the federal-provincial split
of responsibilities. Findings from a variety of Bank and other agency sector studies were incorporated
in the program, including the adoption of a program approach, with only one PC-1 per Government.24
Finally, it was agreed that staff transfers would be limited.
56.    In primary health, two IDA projects presently under implementation' are especially relevant
because they include a greatly enhanced package of maternal health services - including family planning.
Although both of these projects are in relatively early stages of implementation, they have helped show
ways of linking health and family planning services more effectively. Lessons from other social sector
projects in Pakistan incorporated in the PWPP design include the need for advanced preparation, early
Government approval of the project, and the importance of working through the existing implementation
system.
57.    Other donors active in the population sector are UNFPA, ADB, USAID, ODA, and, recently,
the EU and KfW. USAID used to provide a major share of support for the sector but has recently
withdrawn. External support envisaged for the next five years is limited. With the small number of
donors in the sector, it is clear that there will be a funding gap as well as a need for associated technical
assistance.
C. Sector and Program Issues and Constraints
58.    In the past, several major constraints have hampered Pakistan's population efforts. These are now
being addressed under the Eighth Five Year Plan and the Social Action Program.
Lack of Sustained Political Commitment
59.    While Pakistan's political commitment to the population program has oscillated over the years,
until the last two or three years, it has seldom been strong. This has been reflected in low budget
allocations (compounded by a lack of regular status for population expenditure in the budget), slow
releases, weak program  management, and frequent changes in direction.   At its lowest point,
Government's population program was suspended for three years (1977-80), during which time no field
activities or motivational campaigns were undertaken.  Well-designed programs have often been
abandoned before they had time to mature and adjust. Under such circumstances, the ability of the
population program to attract sufficient resources (including high caliber staff), establish a high profile,
24 All development projects in Pakistan must be presented to the approving government authorities in a prescribed detailed
format known as a PC-1. The entire population program is funded from the development budget and is subject to this
requirement.
25 Pakistan Family Health Project (Credit No. 2249-PAK), with IDA financing of US$ 45.0 million, approved in May 1991;
and Pakistan Second Family Health Project (Credit No. 2464-PAK), with IDA financing of US$ 48.0 million, approved in
February 1993.



14
and mobilize intersectoral interest has been frustrated. Strong political commitment is also needed to
allay cultural concerns about family planning, as was shown in Iran, Indonesia and Bangladesh and in
the early days of the Pakistan program.
60.    Over the last few years, a consensus has been growing in Pakistan on the need to address the
population issue seriously. The sector has received much greater public support from Prime Ministers
since 1991, when the Accelerated Program was launched. With the strong support of the present
administration, a special meeting of the Cabinet was called in May 1994 to propose ways that provincial
politicians could more visibly support family planning. Given the broad consensus that has developed,
it is likely that political support for the PWPP would be sustained.
Limited Access and Outreach
61.   In the past, the family planning services provided by the Government have been limited.
Especially in rural areas, only a small proportion of the population lives within reasonable reach of a
service delivery point, so that barely 5 percent of the rural population now has access to family planning
services. Virtually no outreach has been provided for women who may want to use family planning
services, but have to cover too great a cultural and physical distance to obtain them, or couples who need
information on the choices available. Men are moreover not sufficiently aware of the link between family
planning and improved maternal health and child survival. Staffing patterns and resources available at
Family Welfare Centres are also not conducive to outreach. It is clear from the various surveys that there
is substantial unmet demand for family planning services in Pakistan now, but those services and
information must be delivered in a culturally appropriate and convenient way that takes clients' needs and
preferences into account.
Low Levels of Provincial Government Participation
62.    One problem arising from the strong federal control of the population program (including its
funding) is that provincial Governments, dissatisfied with its centralization and lack of responsiveness,
have not become involved in the program. One aspect of this lack of ownership, or commitment, is
persistently poor coordination of family planning activities among the provincial Departments of Health
(DOH), and other provincial departments.
63.    By using the DOH outlets and the proposed new female health outreach field-workers to deliver
family planning services, the PWPP has the potential to expand physical coverage enormously. Despite
a number of policies that have supported such a move, however, it has not yet been successfully
implemented. Recent initiatives to provide family planning as part of maternal and child health services
(including through IDA's two Family Health projects) are presently addressing this problem, but it is not
clear how successful they will be in the absence of greater provincial involvement. One of the key areas
of emphasis of the current project is to enhance the provision of family planning by health outlets (See
Chapter II, paras. 103, 126).
Insufficient Use of Private Sector Potential
64.   In the past, the program has not fully used the potential of the private sector. Although some use
has been made of NGOs, social marketing, private medical practitioners, social marketing, hakeems and
homeopaths, the Government has shown too little awareness of the potential increase in family planning



15
use that could result from a partnership with the private sector. This is an area of great potential,
especially in urban settings, for those clients whose level of demand and knowledge of family planning
is fairly high, but who cannot now obtain contraceptives easily.
Weakness in Demand over the Medium Term
65.   While there is clearly unmet demand by a substantial proportion of the population for family
planning services in the short term, slowing population growth will require increased demand over the
medium and long term. One serious constraint to a successful population program over the longer term
is Pakistan's social and cultural environment, which is not conducive to the forces that bring about the
demographic transition.
66.    Population programs in Pakistan have heretofore operated in a pronatalist environment where
most of the factors associated with high fertility reinforce each other. Literacy, especially of females,
remains very low; infant mortality is high; and a large portion of the population lives in remote villages.
Most significantly, cultural traditions place women largely in a restricted domestic role and inhibit open
discussion of reproduction.  Strong preferences for male children retard the acceptance of family
planning.
67.    For this environment to change, women's status must improve first. Education, better health, and
more participation in the labor-force will provide opportunities for fulfillment in addition to motherhood.
Moreover, a substantial body of literature shows that better educated women tend to have fewer but
healthier children. Families must also be assured that their children will live to adulthood and will
therefore provide continuity for the family and security for the parents in old age. (The SAPP addresses
these problems through its emphasis on improving and expanding social services for women.)
68.    More successful information, education, and communications campaigns must also be developed
to promote demand for family planning, particularly among men and older women (powerful mothers-in-
law). Today, public and private sector ability to provide appropriate information about the benefits of
family planning is limited. Although since 1980 MPW has emphasized an information, education, and
communications strategy that calls for intensive use of face-to-face encounters in villages and Family
Welfare Centres, audiovisual materials and vans in the districts, and a comprehensive mass-media effort,
achievements so far have been limited.
69.   Information, education, and communications programs have been partly constrained by fear of
offending the population with explicit family planning information and creating a backlash, and partly by
a weak information base and a deficient communications strategy development. The result has been
overcentralization of information, education, and communications functions, ineffective training,
inappropriate production modalities, and lack of systematic feedback. Print media and billboards have
limited impact with a largely illiterate, home-based population. Broadcast information, education, and
communications messages - which seldom mention family planning and contraception explicitly - are
ineffective and vague.  In addition, information, education, and communications efforts have not
adequately answered the perceived (or actual) objections of religious leaders and the community to family
planning efforts, and until very recently, men were neglected as a target population. Finally, most
information, education, and communications activity has so far been directed toward attracting new
acceptors of family planning rather than toward maintaining a pool of satisfied users.



16
Management and Organization Issues
70.   There is a need to improve the quality, choice, and reliability of family planning services. But
MPW's efficacy is hampered by the problematic division of labor between the federal and provincial
Governments, lack of regular status in the budget for population activities, and lack of cross-departmental
coordination - particularly with health. The unclear division between federal funding and provincial
implementation has caused financing problems and delayed releases. Although population was placed on
the development budget in order to protect it better, this measure appears to have been counter
productive.
71.    Because the population program continues to be financed entirely from the Government's annual
development budget rather than from the regular budget, PWP employees are not counted in the regular
recurrent budget for staff. Thus, despite their induction into the regular civil service in 1982, PWP jobs
are uncertain, and opportunities for promotion are few. Failure to integrate population staff with the
regular civil service limits opportunities to build commitment toward population goals among the civil
service and discourages the recruitment of well-qualified professionals.
72.     MPW  staff are also subject to constraints that are common throughout Pakistan's public
administration. Decision making is overly centralized. Staff incentives for good performance are limited.
Data are not monitored or well-used. Performance evaluations are based on unrealistic targets rather than
on job descriptions. Based on seniority, promotions offer little incentive for long-term specialist career
development. Finally, the administrative set-up does not make adequate provision for clinical and
nonclinical supervision, which is further complicated by the division of staff along gender lines.
Implementation of Sector Strategy and Policy
73.   While many aspects of the present population program  in Pakistan are well designed,
implementation has been poor. Part of this is due to the problematic division of responsibility between
federal and provincial branches.  With planning and decision making centralized, implementing
institutions have found it difficult to respond adequately to field conditions. The lack of continuity in
management and strategies has meant that the program structure has not evolved through adjustments
based on thorough evaluations. Rather, whenever there was a change in management new programs were
introduced. Field supervision - especially of female staff, has been inadequate. Finally, weak
monitoring and evaluation and program inflexibility have all combined to inhibit the necessary process
of program modification.
II. THE PROJECT
74.    As discussed above, Pakistan's population growth rate of about 3 percent a year seriously
undermines its effort to relieve poverty and achieve sustainable development. Although large families
are still the norm, surveys suggest that about 40 percent of married women would like to have no more
children or to space their next birth. Yet, despite this interest, existing family planning services are
insufficient and of poor quality. To satisfy the unmet demand for family planning services in the short
term, women need to be provided with better information and better and more accessible services. As
women's education and employment possibilities increase over the long term, and desired family size
decreases, services will have to expand to meet additional demands.



17
75.   The overall objective of the Population Welfare Program and Project is to help the Government
achieve its goal of reducing fertility and thereby to slow population growth and improve the health of
women and children. The immediate objectives of both the Program and Project are to improve the
quality and availability of family planning services; to increase demand for these services through
information, education and communication activities; and to strengthen the institutions that manage and
deliver them.
76.   The scope of the Population Welfare Program is the same as that of the National Population
Program as defined in the Activity PC-is approved by the highest Government body, the ECNEC, in
January 1994, plus extra-budgetary support for social marketing and NGOs. The Project, on the other
hand, would finance only part of this total program: the three years from 1995/96 to 1997/98, plus the
additional year 1998/99 which would serve as a transition into the Ninth Plan.
A. The Program Approach
77.   The proposed project's new approach to funding population activities has few precedents, and
none in Pakistan. According to this approach Pakistan's Government must first come to an agreement
with all donors on policy and strategy, including the content and design of the total program, its
financing, goals, and methods of implementation. Then each year, a review session would be held, and
during this meeting, the next year's program components (expenditure program), financing, and program
reforms needed to carry it out would also be defined in detail. IDA and the cooperating donors would
have to approve each annual plan; the program's size, financing and modifications would depend on
progress made during the previous year.
78.   The main difference between this and the traditional project approach is that the Government
would have more flexibility in carrying out the program but would be held to account using measurable
indicators of progress (such as the change in the contraceptive prevalence and total fertility rates). (See
Annex 5 for further details on the methodology of the program.)
79.   Because measurement of impact inevitably lags behind the actual achievement of project goals,
the method of measuring progress would shift gradually from indicators that focus on inputs to include
indicators that emphasize outcome or impact (see Annex 10). The annual review and plans also provide
a mechanism for dialogue between the Government and donors so that the program can be adjusted
incrementally based on need, progress, and experience. Concomitantly, the Government is encouraged
to manage the content of the program, according to how well it is achieving the desired objectives. This
approach, therefore, enables projects to focus on field-based results and to react flexibly, depending on
how well objectives are being achieved. The annual reviews also provide donors a mechanism for
judging the effectiveness of the program as a whole, which should make financing easier and more
efficient, thereby facilitating the Government's management of the project.
80.   Mechanisms for greater stakeholder participation have also been built into the program. One
program initiative is the introduction of village-level outreach workers, whose presence should greatly
facilitate community involvement. In addition, the Government is experimenting with ways to increase
participation and feedback by clients, communities, and field-level workers. To encourage greater
participation at lower Government levels, provincial Population Welfare Departments and the Federal
Ministry of Population Welfare organized a series of three-day, provincial workshops for middle-level
managers, which took place in March and April 1994. These workshops were attended by all program
managers (district managers and above) and by representatives of provincial and national Health, Planning



18
and Development, and Finance Departments; the Accountant General's Office; and Provincial SAP
Coordinators. The workshops oriented program managers to the various components of the Program,
discussed implementation mechanisms, proposed means to improve program management, and reviewed
indicators used to monitor progress. Another series of workshops for tehsil managers and field-worker
representatives began in August 1994.
Program Setting
81.   The proposed four-year Population Welfare Program Project (PWPP) would cover a three-year
segment (1995/96 to 1997/98) of the Population Welfare Program of Pakistan's Eighth Five-Year Plan
(1993/94-1997/98), plus the first year of the next Five-Year Plan. This Project would build on the
achievements of the population component of the Social Action Program Project (SAPP). The proposed
PWPP would begin in the last year of SAPP (1995/96) to support the Population Welfare Program and
would last four years. The population component of the SAPP and of the proposed Population Project
were developed in parallel. (At present, although IDA supports Projects in the other three SAP sectors,
it does not support any projects in the population sector.)
82.   Population policy reforms (mainly institutional) have already begun under the SAPP and reforms
would be added in each year of the PWPP as issues arise. A PC-1 for the population program for the
five years of the Eighth Plan was discussed with the donor community and has already been approved
- making population the only social sector with a detailed program approved for the entire five-year
period.
83.   The first year of the national Population Welfare Program  (1993/94) has already been
implemented and reviewed, and the program for 1994/95 is approved. The 1995/96 program--the first
year of the Population Welfare Program Project--has also been approved by both Government and donors
(although the final details of the PWPP will depend upon the PWP's performance in 1994/95). Directions
of reform for the major policy and program components have also been discussed and approved by both
Government and donors, as have the direction and composition of program financing.
Population Policy Reforn
84.   A series of policy reforms undertaken under SAPP (most of which involve institutional
strengthening, see Annex 8), reflect the Government's heightened commitment to solving social problems.
Under the SAPP, policy matrices are determined for each of the four targeted sectors, which are jointly
approved each year.
85.   For population, the policy matrices cover six key areas:
*     Making adequate financial allocations and releasing them on schedule.
*     Establishing and strengthening strategic and operational planning mechanisms, including
monitoring and evaluation.
*     Increasing the participation of provincial Governments through enhancing their
responsibilities, decentralizing responsibility within and coordinating national activities
with those of provincial line departments.
*     Developing a human resources plan for training population welfare staff.
*     Increasing private sector involvement, including that of NGOs.
*     Ensuring that the supply of contraceptives is adequate.



19
The details of the matrices vary somewhat among the provinces and federal areas, reflecting differences
in their respective situations, constraints, and problems.
86.   During the first two years of the SAPP (1993/94 and 1994/95), the Government has taken these
matters seriously and in most areas has made marked progress. It has particularly addressed problems
of decentralization and cooperation with Health Departments. Human resource development has so far
lagged somewhat behind. Most provinces, except Sindh and the federal areas have made encouraging
progress in extending family planning services geographically. The PWPP, however, would put greater
emphasis on instituting area-based approaches to service delivery, improving management by monitoring
and evaluating activities in the field and by operations research, developing human resources, increasing
private sector participation, and improving the contraceptive supplies and logistics system. These
measures which have already been reviewed and updated, should improve service quality and thereby
overall program performance. (See "future steps" columns of the policy matrices in Annex 8.) An
official letter detailing Government plans for further policy reforms, and actions designed to increase the
accessibility and quality of family planning services, to inform the public and increase demand for
services was submitted and agreed during Negotiations (see Annex 7).
B. Program and Project Objectives, Summary Description, and Policy Actions
87.   The Government's Population Welfare Program operates within the framework of the Eighth
Five-Year Plan. As an integral component of the country's socioeconomic development program, its
main goal is to promote the idea of the small family as the norm and to provide people with the means
to achieve this norm. In official documents, the Program's objectives are stated primarily in demographic
terms as, for example, preventing over 4 million births during the Eighth Plan period, raising the level
of contraceptive use to 24 percent, and thereby lowering fertility by some 10 percent.26 This will be
done by increasing service coverage from 54 percent to 100 percent in urban areas and from 5 percent
to 70 percent in rural areas. A complementary objective is to improve reproductive health services so
that maternal and infant mortality and morbidity are reduced.
88.   These ambitious demographic objectives translate into the following project objectives:
*     To expand family planning service coverage and improve its quality.
*     To increase the demand for family planning services through the information, education,
and communication activities.
*     To strengthen the institutional framework for the delivery of family planning and
reproductive health services through the Ministry and Departments of Population
Welfare, other Government line agencies, the private sector, and nongovernmental
institutions.
89.    Progress toward both Program and Project objectives will be evaluated based on a menu of
indicators, agreed upon by both Government and donors (see Annex 10). (The 1991 Demographic and
Health Survey and existing service statistics collected by the Monitoring and Statistics Wing of the
Ministry of Population Welfare would provide baseline values for quantitative indicators). Progress under
the SAPP and the PWP will be monitored through annual household sample surveys linked to the Pakistan
26 Government of Pakistan, Ministry of Population Welfare, 1993. PC-I. Population Welfare Programme. Federal Activity.
Islamabad. 1993.



20
Integrated Household Survey, a resurvey of the original Demographic and Health Survey cohort, and a
contraceptive prevalence survey. An improved system of monitoring and evaluation will be developed
by both the MPW and Population Welfare Departments, supported by IDA- and donor-funded technical
assistance (Annex 11).
90.   During the recent orientation workshops, participants discussed and modified progress indicators,
to make them more client-oriented. For example, an indicator of client satisfaction which could be
introduced is the number of drop-outs by method. In assessing progress, it was decided that rigid and
unrealistically detailed targets should be avoided. The process outlined above - annual plans, the
introduction of the key policy measures, and the removal of critical implementation constraints - will
expand the absorptive capacity of the agencies concerned. The stringent evaluation process should supply
the information needed to amend or drop unsuccessful activities while enhancing those that work. Key
indicators to be used include: the total fertility rate, the contraceptive prevalence rate (impact indicators),
