number of functioning service delivery points (process indicators), budget allocations and delays in receipt
of funds (financial input indicators) (see Annex 10).
Summary Description
91.   The Population Welfare Program Project supports Pakistan's entire population program. It
includes three major components:
Expanding service coverage and improving service quality. To provide more accessible and better quality
family planning services, the Project would:
Expand services delivered by the MPW and Population Welfare Departments.
Expand and strengthen a recently introduced cadre of community-based, female family
planning workers through the Village Family Planning Worker Program.
Enhance services delivered by public sector health facilities (provincial line departments,
especially provincial Departments of Health) and target group institutions.
3     Increase the private sector's involvement in the delivery of family planning services.
Increasing demand for family planning services. To increase the acceptability and demand for family
planning, the Project would:
*     Strengthen and expand Pakistan's population information, education, and communication
program.
*     Cooperate with provincial line departments (in particular, Departments of Health and
Education).
*     Mobilize the private sector, through such efforts as the Social Marketing of
Contraceptives Program.
*     Increase the support for family planning among Pakistani men and communities.
Strengthening institutional support for family planning. To build on reforms begun under SAPP, the
PWPP would:
*     Make adequate financial allocations and release them on schedule.
*     Establish and strengthen strategic and operational planning mechanisms - including those
for monitoring, re-evaluating and revising strategies,



21
*     Increase the participation of provincial Governments by enhancing their responsibilities,
decentralizing family planning activities within the province, and strengthening
coordination between Project staff and provincial line departments.
*     Develop a human resources plan for improving the quality of population welfare staff.
*     Increase private sector and NGO involvement.
*     Ensure that the supply of contraceptives was of adequate quantity and quality to meet
demand.
Policy Actions
92.   In the context of the SAPP Pakistan has already made major progress on population policy,
administrative, and implementation reforms. Policy Matrices and Implementation Plans (Annex 8) list
federal and provincial Government actions on key issues taken prior to and during 1993/94. These
actions - plus those in progress for 1994/95 - demonstrate the Government's commitment to the
population issue and represent a solid foundation for this project. (Actions already taken and those
planned for the project period are summarized below. For the broad direction of reforms for years two
to five of the Population Welfare Program see Annex 8.)
93.   Reforms begun in 1993/94 and 1994/95 and supported by the SAPP have mostly been
institutional. As such, they prepare the foundation for expanding family planning service coverage,
improving quality, and increasing demand for family planning services. In succeeding years of the PWP,
institutional reform is expected to give way to issues of service delivery. Under the proposed PWPP,
for example, later policy reforms would focus more on instituting area-based approaches to service
delivery and improving the quality of those services. Each major area of reform under the PWPP is
outlined below.
94.   Political commitment andfunding since 1991.  The Governmnent's increased commitment to a
comprehensive population program has been sustained through three changes of administration. This
suggests that a political consensus regarding the national importance of this issue has been reached.
95.   In May 1994, the federal Cabinet reaffirmed the federal Government's commitment to PWP and
enjoined provincial governors and chief ministers to provide public support for the PWP, appoint their
wives as chief patrons of the program in their provinces, and publicly visit family planning clinics in the
provinces as an example. The Cabinet also recommended that the provinces appoint prominent women
and social workers as advisors for the PWP, and that these advisors be given both remuneration and
transport. Other proposed measures included conducting family planning program seminars and briefings
for politicians at provincial headquarters, providing family planning briefing material to all matriculate
graduates, briefing teachers during their training for the Population and Housing Census, and instituting
a World Population Day celebration on July 11, 1994. Finally, the federal Cabinet declared that the PWP
should be reviewed by the Chief Secretary at least once every two months and by the Chief Minister at
least once every quarter.
96.   Greater Government commitment to a population program is also reflected in the Eighth Plan
budget for the PWP. In this Plan, the Government of Pakistan has committed to a level of Rs 9 billion



22
(a three-fold growth over earlier funding levels). The allocation for the PWP in the 1993/94 Annual
Development Plan, moreover, is Rs 1. 1 billion, as compared with Rs 829 million for 1992/93.27
97.    Major progress in streamlining budgetary procedures should also ensure the timely release of
funds, and afford program managers more financial flexibility. In all but one province, the population
program is now listed as a federally funded grant item in the Provincial Annual Development Plan, which
puts it under the control of the Provincial Accountant General. This will allow Population Welfare
Departments to draw funds against the population budget line in the Annual Development Plan without
going through the lengthy process of transferring funds from the federal level.  Procedures for the
delegation of financial authority to district officers and lower officials are also in process, and field
management will be trained regarding governmental financial rules and regulations.
98.    Finally, the federal Government has agreed to exempt the PWP from any federal or provincial
government recruitment bans. To reduce personnel turnover, it has also agreed to assign high-caliber,
senior managers to the program - in particular, federal and provincial Secretaries - who will be
committed to stay in position at least two to three years, barring exceptional circumstances.
99.    Strategic and operational planning mechanisms. To improve strategic and operational planning
capacity, the PWPP would institute the new financial planning procedures described above (para. 97),
strengthen operational planning capacity at the Ministry level, improve the MIS system, and use
information derived from the MIS, monitoring and evaluation system and from planned operations
research studies to refine population services planning and improve their management.
100.  To strengthen management capabilities, the PWPP would conduct a series of orientation
workshops, sponsored jointly by the Provincial Population Welfare Departments and the MPW, for
middle-level managers. (To date, these workshops have been well attended in all the provinces.
Representatives of the Departments of Health, Planning, Finance as well as the SAP coordinators,
Population Welfare Departments, and NGO representatives have attended. Follow-up workshops are
planned, and workshops for lower level managers and field staff have already started). Population
workshops address the program's need for decentralized and informed management in the field.
101.  Provincial Governments'participation. As the population program expands and becomes more
client-focused, decentralization to the provinces will become increasingly important. Needs vary greatly
across the country, and among districts. Provincial Government commitment becomes more critical as
line departments, particularly the DOHs, come into the program.  Provincial Population Welfare
Departments, empowered to act, will be more willing to cooperate with other departments toward a
common goal.
102.   Some progress has already been made in decentralizing the budget to the provinces (see para. 97
above). In future, the Population Welfare Departments would also be given line department status, and
would have the authority to hire and fire personnel. This should resolve the problem of PWP personnel's
ambiguous legal status and establish a career structure capable of attracting qualified staff.
27 However, only Rs 710 million of this allocation was spent, mainly due to delayed releases of funds to the provinces. See
Chapter 111, para. 171 for details.



23
103.  The Government of Pakistan is also now committed to increasing the delivery of family planning
services through other line departments' facilities, particularly the Department of Health - an issue only
recently resolved under the SAPP. The training of health staff in family planning practices is now well
under way, although the logistics of contraceptive supply and actual performance in delivering family
planning services remain problematic. Under the PWPP, senior officers with responsibility for family
planning would be appointed within the Department of Health; job descriptions would be revised to
include family planning responsibilities, more preservice (including compulsory testing) and in-service
family planning training would be required for Department of Health staff, and the supply of
contraceptives would be improved. Provincial Departments of Education would expand their teacher
training program in family planning, and Target Group Institutions (parastatals) would be linked to
provincial Population Welfare Departments, Field programs would be developed with other line
departments (such as agriculture, social welfare, and women's development). Provincial inter-ministerial
and interdepartmental bodies dealing with population would meet regularly.
104. A human resources plan for population welfare staff. Improving staff development for the PWPP
would mean increased clinical and nonclinical training, with a special focus on recruiting and training
Village Family Planning Workers. Under the PWPP, the staff and budget of the Directorate of Clinical
Training would be increased. After a major reorganization of nonclinical training, DOH staff and staff
from other cooperating agencies would be trained in the management of the program. The first human
resources development plan for the Ministry of Population Welfare would also be developed.
105.  Private sector and NGO participation. Pakistan's extensive private health sector holds great
potential for delivering family planning services - particularly in urban areas, and among those who have
already exhibited a strong demand for such services. The potential for private sector family planning
activities, furthermore, is likely to increase over time, as urbanization, female education, incomes, and
awareness of family planning increase.
106.  As for increasing the role of nongovernmental organizations, under the PWPP, it is intended that
about 18 percent of family planning services would be delivered through NGOs by the end of the Eighth
Plan. To enable greater NGO participation in the program, the National Trust for Population Welfare,
a new autonomous coordinating and support NGO, has recently been established. The Trust will broaden
the focus of the NGO program, develop the capacity of NGOs, and improve NGO coordination and
information-sharing. Institutional development of the Trust was started in 1994/95 under the SAPP.
During the Midterm Review (para. 225 and Annex 18), the share of family planning services delivered
by NGOs would be assessed, with a view to identifying ways to increase that share.
107.  Under the PWPP the quality of training and information, education, and communications
materials provided for Registered Medical Practitioners would be evaluated and improved. Professional
medical groups (such as the Pakistan Medical and Dental Council, the Pakistan Medical Association, and
the College of Physicians and Surgeons of Pakistan) would take an active role in stressing to their
members the importance of reproductive health, which should help institutionalize family planning and
reproductive health activities in Pakistan's medical profession (paras. 153, 163).
108.  As an extension of the Social Marketing of Contraceptives Project, a plan for sustaining the sale
of Sathi condoms in the marketplace is being drawn up. Under the PWPP, organization and management
arrangements would be streamlined. The Government of Pakistan would take responsibility for policy
and strategy development, while professional managers would take responsibility for operational



24
management. The flow of funds would also be streamlined, and the social marketing of other types of
contraceptives would be introduced.
109.   Under the PWPP, the quality of training and information, education, and communications
materials for hakeems and homeopaths would be increased.  The curriculum  would be revised,
furthermore, based on participatory evaluations.
110.  Supply of contraceptives. An adequate and reliable supply of contraceptives is essential to the
functioning of any family planning program.  To improve the cost-effectiveness of contraceptive
procurement under the PWPP, this function would be contracted out to an international agency (UNFPA),
which would monitor inventory and distribution of supplies regularly. Pakistan's MPW will undertake
a comprehensive study of the contraceptive flow system during 1994/95. Once problems in the
distribution chain have been identified, appropriate corrective action can be taken. Further research on
contraceptive logistics is also planned for 1994-96.
111.  Area-based service delivery. In the third year of the Family Welfare Program (1995/96), the
MPW and the Population Welfare Departments will move toward decentralization and an area-based
approach to service delivery. The need for family planning information and services would be assessed
for a defined population or area. Then, all available resources and channels would be coordinated to
meet those needs in the most acceptable and cost-effective manner. The success of this approach will
depend on wide local participation in planning, cooperation among those who control the resources,
willingness to look at alternatives, associated training and technical support, and a responsive information
system. It will also require that the decentralization of authority to district and tehsil levels be carried
out as planned. By June 1995, some provinces will select one or two pilot districts or tehsils for intensive
testing of this approach, with prospects for expanding such programs revised in the light of experience
to other areas later.
112.   Quality of services. At this stage in the expansion of the program, when demand is largely in
the early stages, it is especially important to ensure that the services provided are of sufficient quality,
reliability and choice to satisfy clients. This will involve: making services more client-oriented, focusing
on increasing the number of satisfied continuous users, and using regular follow-up to decrease the
number of dropouts; strengthening the standards for pre- and in-service training, especially for the Village
Family Planning Workers; reviewing and revising curricula as required; improving the reliability of
contraceptive supplies and assuring an adequate mix of contraceptive methods; providing appropriate
counseling on choice of methods; and revamping the system for quality control and the assessment of the
program's degree of client orientation.
C. Detailed Program and Project Description
113. This section presents in detail the program and project components: expanding family planning
coverage and improving service quality; increasing the demand for family planning services; and
strengthening institutional support for family planning.
Expanding service coverage and improving service quality
114. This series of program components aims at expanding family planning service coverage through
outlets of the MPW, public sector health facilities, and the private sector. At present it is estimated that
only 5 percent of the rural and 54 percent of the urban population, have access to family planning



25
services within five kilometers of the user's residence. Such limited coverage cannot meet existing
demand for services, much less the enhanced demand contemplated under the Population Welfare
Program Project. Increasing access to services, therefore, is a high priority.
115.   By the end of the Eighth Plan, the Government expects to raise coverage of family planning
services to 70 percent of the rural and 100 percent of the urban population, by expanding the family
planning services of the MPW, the Department of Health and the private sector. The Government also
seeks to increase contraceptive prevalence from 14 percent in 1992/93 to 24 percent by 1997/98. Because
Pakistan's sociocultural environment places tight restrictions on rural women's ability to move outside
the home and neighborhood, moreover, a major new emphasis of the program is to have community-
based female workers and female staffed outreach services in mobile service units deliver services directly
to homes and communities. Greater availability of family planning services at primary health and
maternal and child health facilities (where women may more readily go) is another way to tailor services
to the needs of women.
116.   To improve the quality of care, attention will be given to ensuring that service providers have a
reliable and appropriate range of contraceptive supplies to meet the different needs of couples of different
ages, health conditions, and socio-economic circumstances. Efforts will also be directed to improving
the counselling and interpersonal skills of providers, and improving the regularity of follow-up care.
Continued in-service training and improving the clinical supervision of service providers is expected to
increase their technical competence. It will also provide front-line workers with a female support structure
that was previously lacking.
Services delivered by the Ministry of Population Welfare and Population Welfare Departments
117.  Family Welfare Centres. A network of 1,296 Family Welfare Centres forms the backbone of
public population welfare service structure. To improve the quality and effectiveness of Family Welfare
Centres, the PWPP would introduce changes in staffing patterns, relocation (where necessary), and
improved clinical supervision. The Family Welfare Centres would have fewer staff on site, for while
Family Welfare Workers would still work out of these centers, their Family Welfare Assistants would
be able to travel afield, away from the center. Female Family Welfare Assistants would work in
communities near the centers not served by Village Family Planning Workers. They would register
couples, make regular home visits to motivate and counsel couples, supply contraceptives (condoms, pills)
and refer clients as necessary. Male Family Welfare Assistants would be relocated, either to DOH
district- or tehsil-level outlets or to tehsil population welfare offices to help with the supply and
distribution of contraceptives to the MPW and the Population Welfare Departments, Department of
Healths and private outlets.  They would also facilitate outreach information, education, and
communications and family planning services.
118.   To improve family planning accessibility and coverage, Family Welfare Centres that are poorly
located to serve their catchment area or that are near other family planning facilities (such as a health
outlet) would be relocated. Supportive supervision of Family Welfare Workers by technical officers using
Mobile Service Units would also be introduced on a wide scale. From 1993/94, Family Welfare Workers
and Family Welfare Assistants in selected pilot districts will be retrained in their new roles. This
intensive retraining will begin in 1994/95 and will involve mapping of the catchment area of each Family
Welfare Centre and the identification of sites for family planning camps. By 1997/98, after a thorough



26
review of the pilot areas, the improved system will be expanded to all districts. This effort will take up
17.3 percent of the total program budget28 (provincial: Rs 1,571 million, federal: Rs 32 million).
119.   Mobile Service Units. Currently, 131 Mobile Service Units are operating in Pakistan's Population
Welfare Program. The Eighth Plan envisions an additional 120, with 55 more to be in place by the end
of 1994/95. Mobile Service Units will deliver outreach family planning services, support and supervise
Family Welfare Workers and, where operational, Village Family Planning Workers. In the past, Mobile
Service Units have been hampered by lack of staff caused by government-wide bans on recruitment and
the difficulty of attracting female doctors to the program, as well as by lack of operational resources (such
as fuel, transportation, contraceptives and medicines).
120.   Plans to recruit female doctors on a contract basis and to staff half of the Mobile Service Units
with senior paramedics (Field Technical Officers) should help relieve recruitment problems. Efforts to
improve the management of the program should reduce operational constraints. It may also be possible
to improve links between Mobile Service Units and their target communities by identifying and training
a suitable liaison within each village. Where appropriate, Mobile Service Units could also supervise
Village Family Planning Workers and center staff. This effort will require 4.5 percent of the total
Population Welfare Program budget. (provincial: Rs 409 million).
121.   Reproductive Health Service Centres.  Under the Population Welfare Program  contraceptive
surgery and the use of clinical family planning methods are to be given greater emphasis. The MPW now
has seventy-nine Reproductive Health Service-A Centres that provide clinical methods of contraception.
An additional thirty three centers are to become functional by the end of the Eighth Plan (including twelve
that should have become operational during the Seventh Plan but whose opening was delayed for lack of
staff, the failure of the Department of Health to provide land at hospital sites, and delays in construction).
122.   The lack of female doctors prepared to train and join Reproductive Health Service-A centers is
a particular problem in Balochistan and Sindh. An incentive package for female doctors is now being
offered in the hope of alleviating the problem. In future, the Ministry of Population Welfare and
Department of Health will adhere to the schedule laid out in the PC-ls for establishing Reproductive
Health Service Centres. This effort will require 10.6 percent of the total Population Welfare Program
budget (provincial: Rs 938 million, federal: Rs 28 million).
Community-basedfemale staff in the Village Family Planning Workers Program.
123.   Expansion of the Village Family Planning Workers initiative is the major MPW  service delivery
network goal during the Eighth Plan. These women would take services directly to the home by door-to-
door visits in villages that are otherwise without family planning services. Their number is to be
increased twelve-fold to 12,000 - one in each village with more than 2,000 residents. Village Family
Planning Workers are to be trained as family planning motivators, counsellors, and providers of condoms
and pills. For other family planning methods, the village workers will refer clients to nearby MPW and
Population Welfare Department or other health facilities.
28 These figures were derived from the approved PC-I. Since actual amounts will be determined during the annual reviews,
these are indicative. The percentage figures indicate the relative importance of the component in the overall program.



27
124.   Locally selected to serve the community in which they live, Village Family Planning Workers
would be recruited on a contract basis. They would receive an honorarium of Rs 1,200 per month, the
same as the new community health workers. They would get training and supportive supervision
(including logistical support), from specially trained female paramedics based at tehsil level. In response
to early experience, the ratio of workers to supervisors was substantially improved. Operations research
has played a key part in the careful development of the Village Family Planning Worker strategy; a pilot
study has been ongoing in two districts of NWFP and four districts of Punjab since 1992-93. A
workshop on existing experience in VFPW implementation was held in June 1994.29 Recommendations
on quality of care, in-service training, supervision, and logistics have been taken into account for further
implementation. Operations research will continue to provide feedback on this program to MPW and the
DPWs.
125.   A strengthened management support system, increased training effectiveness, improved clinical
supervision, broader support for the village-based program, and enhanced security of remuneration will
be crucial to the success of this ambitious village-based initiative. In 1994/95, the MPW will appoint full-
time staff to manage the program both at the center and in the provinces. It will also initiate a twice-
yearly program review. By 1994/95, the MPW will also appoint more training staff for the Village
Family Planning Worker program; hold refresher courses for workers, trainers, and supervisors; start
a field-level competence assessment; and provide materials for trainers and village workers. The
refresher training, field assessment, and materials development will continue in 1995/96. By 1994/95,
the MPW will also adjust its other activities (such as, nonclinical training; information, education, and
communication; research; and monitoring and evaluation) to provide support for this major initiative.
Finally, to prevent attrition and to attract the best qualified candidates, the MPW will implement
recommendations to improve security of remuneration. This effort will require 9.9 percent of the total
population budget (provincial: Rs 896 million, federal: Rs 9 million).
Services delivered by provincial line departments, and target group institutions
126.   Provincial line departments.  The number of health outlets of provincial line departments
providing family planning services is expected to increase from around 4,000 at the beginning of the
Eighth Five-Year Plan to 6,000 by the end of 1994/95, and to over 7,000, by the end of the Plan. The
majority will be Department of Health outlets. Expanding the MPW and Population Welfare Departments
and integrating into the public health service, as proposed, will require institutional strengthening.
During 1994, the MPW and the Departments of Health will review current training and contraceptive
supply arrangements to Department of Health outlets, introduce family planning methods into all pre-
service health personnel training programs, and revise the health personnel job descriptions to include
the provision of family planning services. By mid-1995, all health outlets with female staff should be
able to provide family planning services. This effort will require .78 percent of the total Population
Welfare Program budget (provincial: Rs 72 million, federal: Rs 1 million).
127.   Target group institutions. During the Eighth Plan the number of public health outlets providing
family planning services at the workplace should increase from 174 now to 284 in 1994/95, and to 450
by the end of the Plan. The MPW will provide training; contraceptive supplies; and information,
education, and communications materials. An improved supply system should help the Government meet
29 Ministry of Population and Population Council, Workshop on Village Based Family Planning Worker Quality Improvement,
Final report of a Workshop held on June 12-14, 1994, Saidu Sharif, Pakistan.



28
the contraceptive needs of employees at Target Group Institutions. This effort will require 0. 1 percent
of the total Population Welfare Program budget. (federal: Rs 9 million).
Services delivered by the private sector
128.   The number of Traditional Birth Attendants involved in the program is set to increase from 5,000
now to 7,000 by the end of 1994/95. Traditional Birth Attendants get a retainer fee of Rs 250 per month
to motivate couples to use family planning and to supply contraceptives (condoms and oral pills) to
women. In the past, inefficient operational support (such as late payment and contraceptive shortages)
have undermined the commitment and contribution of Traditional Birth Attendants to the program.
129.  Institutional strengthening measures (as outlined in paras. 94 to 112) and improvements in the
support provided by Family Welfare Workers should strengthen and encourage the family planning
activities of Traditional Birth Attendants. Involvement of hakeems and homeopaths is set to increase from
2,500 hakeemns and 1,500 homeopaths now to 7,400 and 3,020 respectively by 1994/95 and to 14,750
and 4,900 by the end of the Eighth Plan. Operational management of the subprogram is to be transferred
to the Provinces. Likewise, the number of Registered Medical Practitioners to be involved in the
program will increase from 3,700 now to 11,100 by 1994/95, to over 22,000. To meet these targets and
maintain involvement will require improvements in training; information, education, and communications
materials; and the contraceptive supply system.
130.   Medical practitioners are to take part in a participatory evaluation of Population Welfare Program
training and materials in 1994/95. Drawing on their professional experience, they will help to ensure
that the training suits the needs and interests of Registered Medical Practitioners. In succeeding years,
regular update meetings will be held to address new topics. To emphasize the importance of family
planning within the medical profession, professional medical associations will also he more involved in
the motivation and training of Registered Medical Practitioners. This effort will require 1.5 percent of
the total Population Welfare Program budget (provincial: Rs 136 million, federal: Rs 2 million).
131.   Nongovernmental Organizations are active in family planning primarily in urban and periurban
areas.  They have demonstrated the success of community-based distribution approaches and been
substantially more cost-effective than the Government at delivering family planning services. By the end
of the Eighth Plan, NGO services are expected to serve 18 percent of all continuiing acceptors, which will
require a significant expansion in the range and number of NGOs participating in the program.
132.   The new coordinating body, the National Trust for Population Welfare, will promote NGO
capacity building and networking and seek to increase family planning awareness, interest and capacity
among NGOs. It will finance approved NGO projects (either wholly or partly) that meet the Trust's
criteria for both the project and the administering NGO (see Annex 15). The current PC-I allocation of
Rs 300 million is likely to be insufficient to meet potential demand or to achieve the program's target.
Details of the funding modalities were agreed during Negotiations, and a detailed financing plan for the
Trust and NGOs will be agreed upon by the Ministry of Population Welfare, Economic Affairs Division,
and donors after the Fund is functioning. This effort will require 3.3 percent of the total Family Welfare
Program budget (federal: Rs 300 million).
133.   Under the Eighth Plan the Social Marketing Program will seek to maintain the distribution of
Sathi condoms and - based on feasibility studies - to introduce other contraceptives (such as oral pills)
during 1994/95. By June 1995, annual sales of oral contraceptives from private outlets are targeted to



29
increase 20 percent. The Social Marketing of Contraceptives program will continue to focus on urban
and periurban areas, using the private sector to promote, market, distribute, and deliver family planning
services.
134.  The program is to be donor-financed off-budget by the Overseas Development Administration and
Germany's Kreditanstalt fiur Wiederaufbau, but it remains within the scope of the PWP. Operational
management is to be contracted out to social marketing experts. The Ministry of Population Welfare will
contribute a small amount to SMC-related information, education, and communications activities.
Because of this arrangement, this effort will require Rs 409 million off-budget and Rs 14 million on-
budget.
Increasing demand for family planning services
135.  In the medium and long-term, it is expected that the sociocultural environment surrounding
fertility decisions in Pakistan will change as female education, urbanization, and incomes increase. The
Ministry of Population Welfare's strategy is to concentrate on improving information, education, and
communication regarding family planning by making it more personal. The emphasis will be on using
interpersonal communication to inform potential users and stimulate demand, whether through the Village
Family Planning Workers or other channels. Information, education, and communication efforts will also
seek to target men, and all staff will be trained to be more effective communicators.
136.  The information, education, and communication program. Pakistan's MPW and PWDs intend
to strengthen and expand the information, education, and communication program to increase the
acceptability and demand for family planning.  In the past, the information, education, and
communication program has lacked direction and co-ordination; the basis of its messages was not well
researched and were frequently aimed at the general public rather than at target audiences. Information,
education, and communication activities have so far focussed on raising awareness of the benefits of a
small family and family planning but have failed to create general acceptance for contraceptive practice.
(According to the PDHS, for example, 78 percent of married women know of at least one contraceptive
method but only 21 percent have ever used one). Outreach visits by family planning service providers
have been limited, and with little interpersonal communication and counselling, the number of "satisfied
clients" is low. Interpersonal contact is the bridge between supply and demand, and well-targeted and
appropriately delivered information, education, and communications activities should stimulate demand
as it informs.
137.  At present, the MPW  is in the process of developing an information, education, and
communication strategy with assistance from UNFPA. Increasing the number of VFPWs has expanded
the program's community base. Female Family Welfare Assistants will now be reoriented toward the
community, and outreach Mobile Service Units will enhance the potential for interpersonal communication
and counselling. Activities to be financed include mass media messages (using both broadcast and print
media), interpersonal communication, and community events. Under the PWPP, technical assistance for
information, education, and communication activities is being planned to enhance community development
and interpersonal communication skills; assist with information, education, and communications activities
in the provinces; and to help develop information, education, and communication and audience research
systems. This effort will require 6.6 percent of the total Population Welfare Program budget (federal:
Rs 380 million, provincial: Rs 222 million).



30
138.   Provincial line departments. In provincial health services, health promotional and educational
activities are rudimentary overall and especially for family planning. Over the course of the Eighth Plan,
therefore, the MPW will continue to take prime responsibility for information, education, and
communication, providing training and developing materials. But with the proposed strengthening of
DOH education capabilities (including education regarding maternal and child health and family planning)
under IDA's various Family Health projects, and the ADB's Health Care Development Project, provincial
health services should be taking an increasingly active part in encouraging family planning as part of the
routine work of their primary health care staff.
139.  In the education sector, the introduction of population education in the formal school curriculum
has the potential of reaching a large target group. The training of secondary school teachers to teach
population education will continue, and by the end of 1994, a population education curriculum (with
associated materials) will be developed for primary schools, the curriculum will be revised for middle
and secondary schools, and training packages for resource persons will be developed. From 1994/95 to
the end of the Eighth Plan, training primary and secondary school teachers how to teach about population
will continue.
140.   The private sector. By investing heavily in promotion and advertising in the private sector, the
Social Marketing Project has succeeded in desensitizing the sale of condoms. Sathi condoms are now
publicly displayed and sold by retailers. Promotion and advertising, therefore, will continue and is set
to expand as new products are brought on-stream, which should significantly boost the national
information, education, and communication program. Scope to increase and improve NGO activities in
information, education, and communication exists but needs to be developed further.  The Social
Marketing of Contraceptives will require Rs 14 million of the federal PWP budget.
141.   Targeting men, older women and communities. To create an environment more supportive of
family planning, the Population Welfare Program Project would initiate community-based activities that
focus on greater interpersonal communication. Previous information, education, and communication
efforts have targeted potential acceptors, but neglected men, religious leaders, and older women (who are
often influential mothers-in-law). The relocation of male Family Welfare Assistants to the community
is directed towards increasing men's awareness and support for family planning. The new cadre of
village-based workers should substantially increase community support for family planning. Small local
experiments (including a pilot in one district in each province), would explore ways by which
communities could be involved directly in the planning, delivery, and monitoring of family planning
services. To further increase support by opinion leaders, including religious leaders, a study would be
carried out to recommend how to use better the religious leaders in support of the PWP. Study tours for
opinion leaders to other Islamic countries (Bangladesh, Indonesia) with successful family planning
programs should also prove helpful.
Institutional Support for Family Planning
142.   To increase the overall efficiency and effectiveness of family planning services and to facilitate
their planned expansion, the Government plans to strengthen the institutional capacity of the family
welfare program in several key areas. These reforms should improve the MPW capacity as a service
provider and as a facilitator for other organization's activities in the sector. In this way, they will also
enhance the capacity of participating organizations to provide services.



31
Adequate and timely financial support
143.  To improve the Population Welfare Program's financial planning capacity, the MPW  has
undertaken preparatory work to change its financial planning procedures. As of July 1994, financial
expenditure sanctions were largely delegated to the provinces, and appropriate financial powers were
passed along progressively to the district level and below. Revised procedures for the release of funds
to the Provinces will be fully integrated into the Annual Development Plan (ADP) and Provincial
Consolidated Funds. Under the new plan, financial releases are now being made by the 15th of the first
month of every quarter to facilitate Population Welfare Program spending.
144.  Strategic and operational planning. To strengthen the MPW's strategic planning capability, a
long-term Institutional Development Plan is to be developed with assistance from the Asian Development
Bank (ADB). The plan would identify organizational weaknesses and suggest measures to improve skills
in planning and management and to assist with the decentralization process. Provincial operational and
annual Work Plans have recently been initiated and are proving useful tools for program managers. A
series of workshops on strategic planning and management development for MPW and PWD middle and
senior managers started in March 1994. Similar workshops for lower-level field staff began in August
1994.
145.  Another important step is the improvement of the management information system (MIS). The
current MIS has several deficiencies: too much information is collected and presented to managers in an
unusable form, cost information is insufficient, and information only flows upward. The new system will
provide for information to flow both up and downward, and will be based on actual performance at the
field level. The system is under development and is currently being pilot tested in the North-West
Frontier Province.
146.   Under the Population Welfare Program Project, with UNFPA support, each Province would
receive one month of technical assistance in monitoring and MIS development in the first year of the
program, while the MPW would receive four months of assistance. Following the framework put in
place under the SAPP, the monitoring and evaluation system to be developed would include the
monitoring of program inputs (more in the initial stages), intermediate outputs, and - as some impact
was felt and data on it became available - program outputs, or impact, would be analyzed. To measure
final impact, data would be collected through the Pakistan Integrated Household Survey (PIHS), which
also includes a module on fertility. As of June 1994, a review of the monitoring and evaluation system
has been undertaken, and a modified system is being tested on a limited scale.
147.  The PWPP would require closer coordination between the MPW and research institutes to make
sure that information gained from operations research was channeled into the development of the
program. In the past, many studies have been of academic interest but have not been relevant to the
program. Recent experience with the Population Council's pilot field evaluation of the Village Based
Family Planning Worker Program, however, illustrates the value of prompt feedback from operations
researchers to program management. The MPW is now reviewing operations research already completed.
A research agenda is scheduled to be completed by early 1995, and new research activities commissioned
accordingly. Technical assistance is also being planned for the National Institute of Population Studies
(NIPS), Population Council, and UNFPA to evaluate the program in 1994-96.
148.  Provincial Government participation.  Over the years, the administrative structure of the
population program has become centralized, and the MPW now makes most key decisions about planning



32
and management. But because the PWP is primarily a field-oriented program, federal control has resulted
in delays in implementation and in program designs ill-suited to local needs. Furthermore, locating
training federally and splitting responsibility for information, education, and communication activities
between the MPW and the Provinces has hindered coordination with the provincial program and limited
the responsiveness of the communications campaign to the Provinces' different needs. Federal control
of the program has also limited provincial Government interest in - and commitment to - the program.
149.   Now that the program is expanding rapidly, there is a need to reassign responsibilities between
federal and provincial Governments to build stronger programs at the provincial and district levels and
to make management and responsiveness to local needs more efficient.  In principle, the MPW  has
decided to decentralize many of its current population welfare functions. The recent decisions to allow
provincial Accountants General to release funds to Population Welfare Departments and to include
population in the provincial Annual Development Plan are important steps toward this end. Proposals
have also been made to have a single Annual Development Plan allocation for the PWP rather than the
current twelve separate allocations. Responsibilities for specific areas (for example, staff training), will
be transferred to the Provinces according to an approved plan.  Responsibility for the program  in
Federally-administered areas will be transferred to those areas to give them a status similar to the
Provincial PWDs.  During Negotiations, assurances were obtained that population program
responsibility in the Federally adminiistered areas will be transferred to those areas with a status
similar to that being followed for the Provinces.
150.   Coordination. Interdepartmental coordination among the federal MPW and Population Welfare
Departments and the provincial Departments of Health has proved difficult. However, with the growing
high-level commitment to integrating family planning into maternal and child health care programs and
the evident commitment of senior Department of Health managers, progress in this area is being made.
The Punjab and Sindh Departments of Health have already assigned senior officers to be responsible for
reproductive and maternal and child health (including family planning), and other provinces are expected
to follow suit. Population cells have been created within the Ministry of Health -,d the four Departments
of Health, and these are now functional. In June 1994, furthermore, DOH job descriptions were revised
to include family planning. A draft is currently being reviewed that includes family planning performance
as a measure of competence in health worker's annual performance evaluations. Overall, performance
and coordination at the Provincial level has improved.
151.   This success must now be replicated at the district and facility level. The track record of District
Technical Committees for coordinating activities between the departments is poor, and district health
managers have yet to give family planning the priority required if it is to be absorbed into Pakistan's
system  of primary health services.  The establishment of an PWP focal point in the provincial
departments and the appointment of district-level maternal and child care and family planning supervisors.
however, should stimulate greater attention to family planning in the districts. The introduction of
Assistant Inspectresses of Health at the district level, furthermore, should provide critical support to
female health and family planning workers.
152.   Training of provincial line department staff. To date, the MPW has trained over 2,000 female
health workers in family planning methods and helped develop family planning capacity in Department
of Health training schools. With the projected expansion of family planning outlets in line departments
from 4,702 to 7,387 during the Eighth Plan period, additional family planning training will be crucial.
Of the projected expansion, 4,352 of the benchmark outlets, and 7,026 of the target are DOH outlets.



33
In future, basic training for health staff will include family planning, which will also be integrated into
the DOH's in-service training.
153.   The establishment of district health development centers under IDA's Family Health Projects,
furthermore, will bring clinical family planning training and supervision resources to the district level.
This should strengthen the Department of Health's family planning services and provide local clinical
training resources for the MPW and Population Welfare Departments. The Pakistan Medical and Dental
Council, which is responsible for setting professional standards in Pakistan, has recently endorsed
including family planning in the postgraduate curricula for community medicine, obstetrics and
gynecology, and family medicine, and to increase emphasis on developing medical competency in the
subject. The College of Physicians and Surgeons of Pakistan has also chosen reproductive health as the
theme for its annual conference in January 1995, a significant development since this body is actually
responsible for the medical curriculum.30
154.   Under the Family Welfare Program  Project, population education within the provincial
Departments of Education would also be substantially expanded.
155.   Population Welfare Staff. In the past virtually no personnel planning has been undertaken by
the Ministry of Population Welfare. Many officers are due to retire in the next few years, and failure
to develop middle-level managers will result in serious weaknesses in program management. The
administrative status of Federal and provincial MPW and PWD employees - which affects their career
structure, security, benefits, pensions - has yet to be resolved, affecting staff morale.
156.   Because the Population Welfare Program is expanding and changing rapidly, a comprehensive
human resource development plan (complete with targets for in-service and pre-service training) is being
formulated and should be complete by June 1995. Present initiatives to recruit staff locally and to
provide incentives for female doctors at least comparable to those offered by Departments of Health are
positive steps towards improving staff quality and employees' working conditions. During Negotiations,
assurances were obtained that: (a) no bans on the recruitment of service delivery staff will be
introduced or enforced during the project period; and (b) the administrative status of population
program staff will be resolved by December 1995.
157.   To meet the increasing demands of the Population Welfare Program, the MPW's clinical and
nonclinical training schools also require strengthening. In particular, the rapid expansion in the number
of Village Family Planning Workers and their trainer-supervisors will place excessive demands on the
twelve existing regional clinical training schools. By the end of the Eighth Five-Year Plan, these schools
will be expected to help train 12,000 VFPWs (plus replacements) and staff for 186 training centers, as
well as to provide refresher training for both groups. Over the Plan period, therefore, a total of 8,400
staff (paramedics, teachers, and NGO paramedics) are slated to undergo clinical training. In addition,
the three nonclinical Population Welfare Training Institutes (responsible for management and information,
education, and communication training of nonclinical staff), are weak, having suffered from years of
underfunding. Nevertheless, these institutes are tasked with training 95,000 program personnel,
employees of other departments, and community-based groups from 1993 to 1998. (The bulk of non-
clinical trainees, 49 percent, will come from community-based groups.)
2 While the curriculum ifor paramedicals has been revised to emphasize family planning further, the medical curriculum has
not.



34
158.  To assess the PWP's training requirements and Pakistan's family planning training capacity, a
national Family Planning Training Plan is to be developed with assistance from UNFPA. ADB's new
population project will start with physical improvements and staff development in Regional Training
Institutes and Population Welfare Training Institutes. The proposed decentralization of the training
schools to the provinces should also improve coordination and planning between the training institutes
and the provincial programs they serve.
159.  Another major development of the program is the introduction of clinically trained, female
supervisors at tehsil level and supportive supervision throughout the service delivery structure. Given
the social segregation of women and men in Pakistan, gender-specific supervision is important and should
help improve a weak aspect of the program and thereby raise the quality of care.
160.   The Private Sector.  A major, and very encouraging development for nongovernmental
organizations working in population was the establishment, in November 1994, of a National Trust for
Population Welfare, with an autonomous Board, elected Chairperson, and nonvoting government
representation to replace the NGOCC (See Annex 15). The Trust would eventually have provincial
offices, which would nominate or elect representatives to the national body. The MPW has provided Rs
100 million as an initial endowment, with an additional allocation in 1994/95. During Negotiations, the
Government confirmed the final approval, registration, initial endowment, and selection of the Board of
Directors for the National Trust Fund for Population Welfare. Assurances were obtained during
Negotiations that the Trust will continue to operate in a manner satisfactory to IDA.
161.  It is likely to take six to nine months before the Trust becomes fully operational, and while it is
being established, funds available for NGO support under the SAPP (through the Participatory
Development Fund) will be used to cover the transition. The existing direct funding system through
Economic Affairs Division, which is especially suitable for the larger NGOs, will continue.
162.  In recognition of the NGOs effectiveness in the sector, a special allocation of funds within the
Population Welfare Program Project would be established to support NGO population projects. Funds
would be channelled through the Trust, which would appraise both the applying NGO and the substance
of the proposal according to criteria acceptable to IDA. The Trust would also be responsible for
monitoring performance and finances and auditing approved projects. While all NGO population-related
projects (broadly defined to include improvements in the health and economic status of women and
children) would be eligible, preference would be given to proposals in the areas of community
participation, cost-recovery, operations research, and inter-NGO networking. Technical assistance to help
develop the Trust's capacity is in place for 1994/95. This would be continued into the Population
Welfare Program Project period as necessary. (See Annex 15 for further details.)
163.  In order to strengthen the integration of health and family planning and to address reproductive
health issues more fully, it is important for the program to involve Pakistan's medical profession more
directly in providing family planning training and services. The country's three most important medical
professional organizations have now agreed to support actively family planning and reproductive health
activities. The Pakistan Medical and Dental Council has agreed to include family planning in relevant
postgraduate curricula; the Pakistan Medical Association is actively encouraging doctors to become more
involved in providing family planning and reproductive health services; and, in January 1995, the
College of Physicians and Surgeons had reproductive health as the theme for its next annual conference.
Efforts to enlist the cooperation of traditional medical practitioners (such as hakeems and homeopaths)
will continue.



35
164.  The private sector in Pakistan has considerable expertise in product marketing, distribution, and
management, and this expertise helped make the Social Marketing of Contraceptives Program a success.
The Social Marketing of Contraceptives also benefitted from US-funded technical assistance and study
visits to other successful social marketing projects. This private sector initiative is expected to continue
under the next phase of the Social Marketing of Contraceptives Program, following the withdrawal of
USAID funding from Pakistan. So far, a management contract has been awarded to cover the interim
period until the proposed KfW-funded condom project can be implemented. New targets for 1994/95 and
onwards are being established. Earlier problems with the mechanism for direct disbursement of donor
funds to the implementing agency and use of receipts have been resolved. ODA financed feasibility
studies for introducing social marketing of hormonal contraceptives are now in progress.
165.  Contraceptive supply. Inefficiencies in the contraceptive supply and logistics system - primarily
delays in procurement and breakdowns in the distribution system - have led to shortages of supplies at
service outlets and in the Central Warehouse. Given the expected growth of the program, the increasing
numbers of Village Family Planning Workers who will need to be supplied and the expansion of the line
departments' outlets, these inefficiencies could get much worse.
166.  To improve the cost-effectiveness and coordination of contraceptive procurement, therefore, an
international agency (UNFPA) will undertake procurement on behalf of the Government and all donors.
It will also regularly monitor inventory and the distribution of supplies. To identify problems in the
distribution chain, the MPW intends to undertake a comprehensive study of the contraceptive flow system
by December 1994. Further research on contraceptive logistics is also planned for 1994-96. To improve
availability at the facility level, field experiments of alternative distribution methods (such as provincial
buffer stocks), have already been undertaken, and local institutions will conduct quality control checks
on the contraceptives. During Negotiations, GOP, IDA, and UNFPA agreed on actions to be taken if
emergency purchases of contraceptives become necessary.
D. Environmental Considerations
167.  The proposed Population Welfare Program Project was determined to be in screening Category
C, which includes projects that are expected to have no significant negative environmental impact. On
the contrary, by reducing the population growth rate and hence population pressures on the environment,
this project is expected to have a substantial positive affect on the environment. No environmental
analysis, therefore, has been prepared for this project.
III. COSTS, FINANCING, MANAGEMENT, AND IMPLEMENTATION
A. Costs and Financing
Financing Pakistan 's Population Welfare Program
168.  Public expenditure on family planning in Pakistan has been extremely modest and has fluctuated
in response to variations in program strategy and political commitment. (See Annex IB, Table 1, for a
summary of past public funding and expenditures for the Population Welfare Program). During the
1980s, expenditure on population averaged 0.06 percent of GNP and never exceeded 0.07 percent.
Expenditures increased by almost 60 percent between 1983/84 and 1985/86, rising from 0.04 percent to
0.07 percent. In 1986/87, however, half of this gain was lost. Population averaged 0.24 percent of



36
Pakistan's total public expenditure during 1983-1992 and less than 10 percent of the country's expenditure
on health.
169.   Since 1990/91, Pakistan's Government has made its population program a much higher priority.
Although the magnitude of the fiscal adjustment required in the early years of the Seventh Plan resulted
in a slight fall in population expenditures in real terms in 1988/89 - 1989/90, expenditures have risen
substantially in real terms during subsequent years. In 1991/92, for instance, population was exempted
from the general cut in the Annual Development Programme imposed on other development programs,
and an additional 10 percent was allocated to it to accelerate the implementation of key program
components. In the last year of the Seventh Plan period, the allocation for the population program
surpassed that envisaged in the original Plan. Actual expenditures exceeded the ADP allocations, in fact,
throughout the Seventh Plan period, reflecting the Pakistan Government's growing commitment to
population.
170.   Foreign assistance funded 19 percent of Pakistan's Fifth Plan, 53 percent of the Sixth Plan, and
40 percent of the Seventh, with USAID accounting for 65 percent of total foreign assistance under the
Sixth Plan and 87 percent under the Seventh. When USAID withdrew funding from all sectors in
1992/93, foreign contributions to the population program fell dramatically (see Annex I B, Table I). The
result was a decline in the total population program size, but Government funding for the program
substantially increased (by almost 20 percent) which avoided a larger program reduction.
171.  It was estimated that in 1993/94 (the first year of the Eighth Five-Year Plan, and the first without
USAID funding), Pakistan's population expenditure would not reach the 1991/92 level of Rs 763 million.
In fact, population expenditure was Rs 710 million, short of the target of Rs 1,100 million projected at
the start of the Social Action Program Project. Foreign aid levels increased slightly from the 1992/93
low of Rs 174 million to Rs 187 million in 1993/94. Government financing, however, fell by one
percent, from Rs 529 million in 1992/93 to Rs 523 million in 1993/94. Erratic, delayed, and incomplete
releases of allocated funds, delays in reaching agreement with donors on program funding, and delays
in allocating funds by components combined to create this shortfall in Government financing. The policy
dialogue under SAP and the design of this project focus on achieving higher and more reliable
Government contributions in line with the Government's own announced plans.
Total Program Costs
172.   The Government prepared and approved detailed plans (PC-Is) for the implementation of the
Population Welfare Program in the Eighth Five-Year Plan period (see Annex IB, Table 2). Over the five
years, annual expenditure on the population program is set to increase by 132 percent in real terms,
implying an average annual growth rate of 18 percent in real terms. The PWPP's proportion of total
Government expenditure is set to rise from 0.25 percent in 1992/93 to 0.63 percent in 1997/98.
173.   Under the Government's proposed program, provision for family planning services is to grow
at an annual average real rate of 25 percent. Thus the relative share of service delivery in the total
population budget would rise from 56 percent in 1992/93 to 73 percent in 1997/98 - largely because
of funding for the new cadre of Village Family Planning Workers. At the same time, the share of
administrative costs is set to decline from 26 percent in 1992/93 to 15 percent by 1997/98, for a planned
five-year average of 16 percent. (This relative decline reflects a small real fall in the provision for federal
administrative costs and restrained growth in the provision for provincial administration). Thus the
Government would be devoting a larger share of its resources to concrete program needs. Concentrated



37
in the early years, investment items (training, vehicles, equipment and furniture, civil works, etc.) will
account for 12 percent of the total population budget in 1993/94, 11 percent and 12 percent of the totals
in the next two years, and just 2 percent in the last year. Remuneration accounts for 39 percent of the
total provision for operating costs over the five years, with the share declining from a budgeted 43
percent in 1993/94 to 37 percent in the final year.
174.  These objectives call for sustained Government commitment to compensate for gross under-
investment in population programs in the past. Yet given the very low starting point, Pakistan's planned
annual expenditure on population is still very modest both in absolute terms and when considered relative
to overall Government expenditure. Nonetheless, as planned, this phased expansion of public expenditure
on population is ambitious, with most of the planned increase occurring in the first two years. The total
expenditure budgeted for population in 1993/94 is 42 percent higher in real terms than actual expenditures
were in 1992/93, with provisions for operating costs alone 22 percent higher in real terms than actual
expenditures in 1992/93. The planned total expenditure for population in 1994/95 is 39 percent higher
in real terms, with operating expenditures set to grow by 37 percent.
175.  In agreement with IDA, the Government of Pakistan has revised the financing plan to provide
a more realistic but still very ambitious pattern of growth for its population program over the four-year
period covered by the proposed project (Table 3.1 and Annex 14). This new financing path still calls
for a very sustained Government effort.
176.  During the Seventh Plan period, Government financing averaged 55 percent per year (excluding
1992/93 when USAID's withdrawal disrupted the program). By the end of the Eighth Plan under the
revised growth pattern, Government financing will increase gradually to 60 percent. This growth in the
Government's share will entail an annual increase in public financing of 25 to 27 percent in real terms
during the four-year period.
177.  A substantial increase in expenditure on the population program as a whole would take place in
1994/95 (76 percent in current terms, or in real terms 66 percent, over the expected 1993/94 expenditure)
to finance a major investment effort and to make up for the shortfalls of the previous two years. From
1995/96 onwards, program expenditure in current prices would be set to grow at about 31 percent or 22
percent in constant prices. The four-year total cost of the proposed population program would be Rs
10,339 million. During Negotiations, assurances were obtained that: (i) the overall level of spending
on the population program would rise by at least 22 percent a year (in constant price terms) in fiscal
1995/96 to 1998/99 and that the total size of the PWP for the same period be Rs 10.34 billion,
subject to the possibility that a different percentage may be agreed in fiscal 1998/99, the first year
of the Ninth Plan, with a resulting different aggregate over the four-year period; and (ii) in fiscal
1995/96 to 1998/99 the Government's contribution will rise from 57 percent to 60 percent of total
spending on population, including the Government financing an increasing proportion of
incremental costs. Satisfactory progress in implementing the SAPP supported population program
for 1994/95 would be a condition of Credit Effectiveness.
Cost Estimates for the Proposed Population Welfare Program Project
178.  The proposed IDA Credit for the PWPP would support the entire Population Welfare Program
during the final three years of the Eighth Plan period, and for one year beyond, which should ensure a
smooth transition into the next Plan. It would complement and build on support provided by IDA and
other donors through the SAPP during the Eighth Plan's first three years. Under both projects, the scale



38
of assistance is determined on the basis of past performance with annual agreements required for future
activities. The proposed IDA Credit would thus operate according to the procedures agreed for SAPP
and would be fully consistent with SAPP.
179.   Present estimates of project costs, extrapolated to 1998/99, are based on actual costs and budget
projections for the five-year program. The total cost of Pakistan's population program from 1995/96 to
1998/99 is estimated at US$287.6 million or Rs 10,339 million (see Table 3.2). Base costs are estimated
at US$233.8 million and contingencies at US$53.8 million. Physical contingencies at US$11.6 million
are relatively small, because support is partly on a program basis. Price contingencies of US$42.4
million reflect the latest Bank forecasts of local and international inflation.3' The foreign exchange
component of the overall program is estimated at US$105.0 million, or 37 percent of total costs.
International price increases for each project year (July I to June 30) are set at 1.0 percent for 1994/95, 2.3 percent for
1995/96, 2.6 percent for 1996/97, 2.6 percent for 1997/98, and 2.6 percent for 1998/99. Local price increases are set at 8.4
percent for 1994/95, 7.9 percent for 1995/96, 7.4 percent for 1996/97, 7.3 percent for 1997/98, and 6.8 percent for 1998/99.

180.  Under the "program approach" envisaged for this project, IDA has reached agreement with the
Government on virtually the entire four-year Population Welfare Program proposed by the Government;
thus this program would be eligible for support from the IDA Credit.  (The only exceptions are
provisions for the construction of offices for federal and provincial headquarters and for the National
Institute for Population Studies and expenditure on land acquisition, duties, and taxes.) Funding for the
construction of two Regional Training Institutes is already provided by ADB. The eligibility of new
elements introduced in the future will be determined through the annual agreements.
181.  Cost estimates for the proposed population project were derived from the approved and detailed
five-year plans of expenditure presented by the MPW for the PWP in the form of Activity PC-ls, and
from  independent descriptions of activities at the Federal and individual Province level.  (New
construction, largely of office buildings, is outside the scope of the Activity PC-Is.) The MPW estimates
of costs are based on Government procedures, which are satisfactory to IDA. Costs of equipment and
furniture, vehicles, and training are based on present prices as verified by recent MPW experience. Costs
of minor civil works are calculated using the standard Government contract rates. Provisions for salaries
and general allowances, for travelling and other allowances, and for contraceptive surgery fees are based
on current Government rates. Estimates of vehicle operating costs follow current Government provisions.
Costs of contraceptives are based on agreed MPW and UNFPA/donor estimates of requirements and
international prices. Costs of medicines are based on recent Government experience.
182.  In adjusting the original MPW estimates, partial provisions for cost escalation were eliminated
to bring all estimates to a consistent 1994/95 price base. Following standard Government practice MPW
estimates for construction costs were presented in constant price terms. To reconcile the detailed figures
presented in the PC- I documents with the approved figures for the totals, the PC-I figures were adjusted
proportionally. Because of shortfalls in actual budgets and expenditures compared with the Activity PC-I
figures for 1993/94 and 1994/95, IDA estimates were used to calculate 1993/94 expenditures, agreed
figures with the Government were used for the 1994/95 budget, and the Activity PC-1 figures were used
as the basis for the estimates for succeeding years. Rates of growth incorporated in the MPW plans have
been modified, however, in light of actual expenditure, and the expansion path has been smoothed. As
presently planned, the program should grow consistently in real terms 22 percent a year from 1995/96
to 1998/99 - the last three years of the Eighth Plan and the first year of the Ninth. This smoother
expansion path represents a challenging but feasible objective. During Negotiations, it was confirmed
that the approved PC-ls will be interpreted flexibly, in keeping with the spirit of the 1989 Planning
Commission instructions, to support the program approach with its requisite annual agreements on work
program and expenditures.
183.  For the four years of the PWPP, the cost of contraceptive supplies is estimated at US$25.3
million (derived from an estimate of US$43.0 million total contraceptive requirements for Pakistan's
whole population program, US$17.7 million of which would be financed by on-going projects, see Annex
IB, Tables 3A and 3B). Vehicle requirements under the project are estimated at about US$3.5 million
(US$6.1 million total population program requirements, with much of the remainder being provided under
on-going projects).
184.  The project cost also includes provisions for contraceptive social marketing (largely financed by
on-going projects) and for support to nongovernmental organizations (NGOs) that have hitherto fallen
outside the standard Government budget, being almost entirely financed by USAID and other donors.
Estimates of extra-budget expenditure for social marketing are based on expenditure plans established



42
before the withdrawal of USAID assistance. Estimates of additional extra-budget support for NGOs are
based on previous levels of Government and donor support.
185.   Costs for customs duties and taxes, to be financed by the Government, include an estimated
US$12.8 million for direct and indirect taxes and duties on civil works, goods, and services.
186.    Allowances for physical contingencies are estimated at 5 percent of the adjusted base cost of the
four-year program  and amount to US$11.6 million, or 4.0 percent of total program  costs.32  These
contingencies are relatively small because of the program nature of the support to be provided and
because of the scope for reallocation within the program total. Price contingencies are estimated at
US$48.7 million, or 14.7 percent of total program costs. Total contingencies represent 23.0 percent of
base costs.
187.   The foreign exchange component of the total program  (including contingencies) is estimated at
US$105 million, or 37 percent of total costs (see Table 3.2). It is estimated that foreign funds pay for
10 percent of the population program's civil works, 50 percent of its vehicles, 40 percent of furniture and
equipment, 100 percent of contraceptives, 50 percent of medicines, and vehicle operating costs, and 30
percent of information activities, and other operating costs.
188.   Because of the nature of the sector, the investment component of the program  is only 5 percent
of the total and it is scheduled to be virtually complete by the end of the Plan period. In the first year
after the end of the Plan period, therefore, 98 percent of expenditure will be recurrent. Over the four
years of the project, total incremental recurrent expenditure is estimated at US$56 million. But as
stressed above, although the rapid rate of increase should result in a far more satisfactory level of funding
than has been the case in recent years, the overall size of the program will remain modest, and
affordability is not a significant issue. The provision of adequate funds for nonsalary operational
expenditures will be a key consideration when discussing the annual operational plans.
189.   Through program  support, IDA funds will reimburse previous Government expenditure on the
basis of submitted statements of expenditure. Funding for everything but the NGO component of the
project, will be channelled through the standard Government system (Annex 15).
Total Population Program Funding
190.    Table 3.1 outlines the financing plan agreed upon for the four years of the PWPP (1995/96 to
1998/99), based on the revised phasing of annual expenditures. The approach for financing is to allocate
the funding requirement between Government and donors, with the Government funding a given
proportion of the program from its own resources and the donors financing the rest. After subtracting
expected aid from other sources, the Population Welfare Program Project would fill the gap. The details
of the respective shares of Government and donor funding would be determined within the already agreed
framework as part of the annual agreements.
'' In Government cost estimates, the only explicit provision for physical contingencies are annual "unallocated expenditures"
totalling 1 percent (in real terms) of the five-year total. These arose as residuals between the original Five-Year Plan figures
and the finally approved, detailed expenditure figures for the Plan period. For Government purposes, expenditure provisions
can be adjusted as required during annual budget preparations (initially, within 15 percent of the original budget in the PC-1)
by reallocation within the program total and, if necessary, by increasing the annual totals. The need for explicit provisions here
arises because the rate of expansion has been moderated.

191.   Over the project's four-year period, the share of Government financing for population programs
(which averaged 55 percent per year during the Seventh Plan, except for 1992/93) is set to increase
gradually to 60 percent. This growth in the Government's share would entail an annual increase of 22
percent in the Government's contribution in real terms during the four-year period (Table 3. 1). The
presently calculated Rs 957 million (US$ 28.5 million) for 1995/96 would bring the Government s share
to 58 percent, substantially higher than in the recent past. Overall Government financing would amount
to Rs 6,149 million (current terms), leaving a shortfall of Rs 4,190 million. During the same period, on-
going donor aided projects are expected to finance about Rs 2,203 million in program-related activities.
Given the funding needs envisaged for Pakistan's Population Welfare Program, however, this would
result in a financing gap of Rs 1,987 million, or US$55.1 million for capital and incremental recurrent
costs.
192.   Ongoing aid projects. Over the four-year project period, disbursements from on-going and
expected projects are anticipated to reach US$63.9 million, of which US$2.4 million of ADB financing
will fund construction outside the scope of the PWPP (see Table 3.3 for the breakdown of estimates by
donor, excluding the ADB construction financing).

193.  IDA. After contributions from existing projects and the agreed upon Government contribution
are accounted for, a funding gap of US$55.1 million -or 19.2 percent of total costs over the four years
- remains. In addition, allocations have been made for US$5 million for technical assistance and US$5
million for an NGO Fund. An IDA Credit of US$65.1 million, therefore, was agreed to cover the gap,
technical assistance and the NGO Fund. Given Government contributions, donor financing would cover
a falling share of total costs and of incremental costs. IDA financing will cover a declining share of
incremental recurrent costs. During Negotiations, the financing plan for the program and project was
