Proposed Project Funding
194.    The Federal Government will make available to the provinces and the federal areas part of the
proceeds of the proposed Credit, in accordance with its standard budgetary procedures, for implementing
their respective parts of the program.
195.   The proposed PWPP would follow on and use the same program  approach as the SAPP,
Imailntainling the same financing mechanisms. The project would support the program as a whole without
discrimi inatling amonig activities. With the exception of contraceptives, vehicles, technical assistance, and
NGOs. IDA would fund the same proportion of all qualifying expenditure, after conventional project
support to the PWPP has been taken into account. In order not to discourage other donor funding for
the PWPP, it is agreed that the Government would meet with other interested donors to seek and confirm
fundinig of the Population Welfare Program for the coming year, before the annual review of the IDA-
tunided PWPP. IDA would then provide funding only as a last resort.
196.  TIo ensure timely procurement and financing of critical inputs, however, the project would finance
1)() percent ot certain costs: contraceptives (US$25.3 million), vehicles (US$2.3 million), technical
assistance (US$5.() million), and support to NGOs (US$5.0 million). All these goods and services would
he procured in accordance with IDA Guidelines and procedures, and corresponding funds would be
earmarked in the Credit Agreement.
197.   Given the present high degree of federal control, it is not possible to introduce any incentive or
comilpetitioni for donor funds among provinces. As the program decentralizes, this may become feasible
and would potentially become part of the annual agreements.
B. Disbursemenit, Procurement, Accounts and Audit
Disbursement
198.   Credit disbursemilent under the proposed PWPP would follow the same procedures developed for
the SAPP. To ensure that Government follows through with increased allocations and the timely release
and expenditure of funds, the project would reimburse against actual expenditure and on the basis of
actions alread,y taken. The project would reimburse the Government for a proportion of all eligible
expenditures incurred in the population sector excluding those already funded by other aid projects. The
annual agreemenit hetween Government and IDA (and other PWPP program donors) would include
detailed identification of all eligible categories of expenditure.
199.   Certain categories of expenditure (such as office construction) have been identified as outside the
project and ineligible for PWPP funding. Others (such as land acquisition, taxes, and duties) are ineligible
under IDA rules.  Apart from these exceptions, all expenses incurred in delivering the population
program would be eligible (including salaries, operating expenses, supplies, goods, civil works, and
maintenanice expenses). Given the nature of the population program, the only potential problem might
arise from the need to exclude taxes and duties. However, as US$37.6 million have been earmarked for
contraceptives, vehicles, technical assistance, and support of NGOs (all categories financed at 100 percent
by IDA), the resulting reimbursement rate on other eligible expenditure would be such that there would
be no risk of IDA financing ineligible expenditure items. The tax component, therefore, would not be
isolated for purposes of calculating reimbursement.



45
200.   At present, the functional and object classification for budgeting and reporting is adequate to
distinguish PWPP from  non-PWPP expenditures as now  envisaged.  Should new  categories of
noneligibility be agreed in the future, however, the Government would develop (and agree with IDA on)
suitable classifications. The existing reporting system is adequate to permit monthly identification and
reporting of all qualifying expenditures.
201.   As with SAPP, reimbursement would be claimed at least quarterly and perhaps more frequently,
depending on the capacity of the federal Government to generate the claim. PWPP and NGO claims
would normally be made in the form of statements of expenditure (SOEs) that would specify categories
of expenditure (civil works, staff costs, goods, operating expenditure, etc.) as well as the eligible
expenditure total. In those few cases where contracts for goods and civil works exceed US$200,000
equivalent, consulting firms exceed US$100,000 equivalent, individual consultants exceed US$50,000
equivalent, and NGO contracts exceed US$100,000 equivalent, full documentation would be required.
Each SOE will be supported by a financial statement, (retained by the borrower) separating expenditure
by function and object, and by a summary of ADP-scheme expenditure.  The SOEs would not
differentiate between foreign and local content. Where an SOE covered expenditure within an ongoing
project, the SOE would specify the portion eligible for PWPP reimbursement. Recoriciliation of the
statements of receipts from other donors would also be required. All claims would be certified by the
Secretary of the Ministry of Population Welfare. The Ministry would then submit them to IDA. Only
claims above US$100,000 equivalent would be submitted to IDA for reimbursement/payment.
202.   NGO Special Accounlt. The newly created National Trust for Population Welfare (NATPOW)
would manage the project-financed NGO Fund for which US$5 million has been initially earmarked. In
order to facilitate implementation, a Special Account would be operated by NATPOW, through which
financial grants would be provided to both large and small non-profit organizations to carry out projects
approved by the Trust. After the project went into effect and the required withdrawal application was
received, IDA would make an initial deposit of US$300,000, out of an authorized total allocation of
US$500,000. This account would be used for all local and foreign expenditures less than US$100,000
equivalent. Only expenditures above US$100,000 could be submitted directly to IDA for payment
without using the Special Account funds. The Trust would submit applications for replenishment at
monthly intervals, or whenever the amount to be replenished exceeded 20 percent of the initial deposit,
whichever occurred first. Replenishment of the account would be contingent upon full justification of
the appropriate use of funds by the Trust (see Annex 15 for operational details of the National Trust for
Population Welfare component).
203.   Credit disbursements would be handled as follows:
*      CContraceptives and vehicles.  Finance 100 percent of foreign expenditures of direct
imports, 100 percent of ex-factory cost for locally manufactured items procured through
ICB, and 65 percent of local expenditures for items procured locally;
*      Technical assistance anid grants under the NGO Fund. Finance 100 percent of the costs,
net of taxes.
*      Other items. Reimburse the Government for a fixed percentage agreed upon annually
between the Government and IDA.



46
204.  The proposed IDA Credit of US$65.1 million would be disbursed from 1995/96 to 1999/00.
Actual annual disbursement would depend on performance, the pattern of expenditure agreed upon, and
the availability of other donor funds. (See Annex 17 for estimates of annual disbursements by main
categories of expenditures.)
Procurement
205.  The program nature of this project makes it difficult to forecast both the total and IDA-financed
procurement amounts for different categories of expenditure. The figures given below, therefore, should
be regarded as indicative only. Final details would be discussed and fixed as part of the regular yearly
agreements between the Government and IDA. Given the close association between the PWPP and the
SAPP, procurement procedures for the PWPP and SAPP would be the same. The arrangements
summarized below mirror those already approved for the SAPP (see Table 3.4 and Annex 16).
206.  Over 90 percent of the proposed project's total costs would be recurrent. Moreover, since this
program would be implemented throughout the country, most procurement would involve small contracts
dispersed over wide geographical areas. Such contracts would be suitable only for local competitive
bidding and prudent shopping. Large procurement operations, on the other hand, would be required for
contraceptives, vehicles, and consultant contracts. With minor exceptions, these would be suitable for
international competitive bidding based on the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents. Procurement would
be conducted by the MPW, Population Welfare Departments, and nongovernmental organizations as
appropriate. UNFPA is expected to carry out some procurement for the Government under arrangements
satisfactory to IDA.
207.  The civil works component of the proposed project would be small (about US$ 0.2 million
Government and IDA financing). Equipment and furniture expenditure (for small clinic equipment, kits
for paramedical staff, medical accessories, and small audio-visual equipment) over the four-year period
would total approximately US$1.1 million Government and IDA financing. Expenditures on medicines
(pharmaceuticals and reagents) are estimated at US$5.5 million Government and IDA financing. All
items would be procured through local competitive bidding (with the exception of items that cannot be
grouped into packages of at least US$25,000 up to an aggregate not to exceed US$300,000 per agency,
US$1.5 million in total, which may be procured on the basis of prudent shopping).
208.  Government's local competitive bidding procedures (already in use in many ongoing IDA-funded
projects) are in compliance with IDA guidelines.  To address shortcomings identified in their
implementation, provisions have been made under the SAPP for training of staff involved in procurement
at the federal and provincial levels. This is currently being implemented in the MPW and the Population
Welfare Departments, and it will be extended to cover the procurement of goods and services under
international competitive bidding procedures for the MPW. Under the SAPP, consultants now review,
among other things, the selection process for all contracts above US$50,000. These arrangements would
continue under the PWPP and would strengthen the procurement capacity of the executing agencies.
209.  IDA agreed to the Government's request that the four main types of contraceptives (about
US$30.5 million of IDA financing) be procured through UNFPA following IDA guidelines for
international competitive bidding, which should generate a steady supply of good-quality, inexpensive
contraceptives. To ensure continuity of supply shouid shortages occur, it was agreed that contraceptives
to an estimated total cost of US$600,000, could be procured through UNFPA or locally by the MPW
using local or international shopping. UNFPA will provide long-term technical assistance to MPW and



47
the Population Welfare Departments for planning and undertaking contraceptive procurement (see Annex
16).
210.  The procurement of vehicles (about US$2.3 million of IDA financing divided among the
agencies) should be mainly conducted by provincial authorities through international competitive bidding.
However, in order to meet urgent needs, vehicles totaling up to US$500,000 may be procured through
local competitive bidding. Standard bidding documents for the procurement of vehicles have already been
approved by IDA. IDA would undertake review of all international competitive bidding procurement.
211.  IDA would review all ICB contracts and contracts above US$200,000 equivalent, as well as the
first four contracts in each agency irrespective of their value; all other contracts would be subject to
selective post-review. Consultant services would be engaged by the federal or provincial authorities as
appropriate. All consultants would be selected in accordance with IDA guidelines, which includes prior
review of all contracts with consulting firms costing the equivalent of US$100,000 or more and with
individual consultants costing the equivalent of US$50,000 or more. IDA's prior review would also be
required for all contracts expected to cost the equivalent of US$100,000 or more under the NGO Fund.
The training, technical support, and direct assistance provided by the SAPP Support Unit to federal and
provincial staff would be instrumental in facilitating all local or international procurement of goods and
services. MPW staff at the federal and provincial levels would also be able to call upon the Ministry of
Planning and Development, which is more experienced in international competitive bidding.
Accounts and Audits
212.  Accounts for the proposed population project would be maintained by the MPW  and the
Population Welfare Departments in accordance with sound, and locally recognized, accounting principles
and practices satisfactory to IDA, as agreed under SAPP. The accounts, SOEs, and procurement
documentation will be audited at the end of each year by independent auditors acceptable to IDA. The
audit reports would be submitted to IDA no later than nine months following the end of the year to which
the audits refer. The reports would include an assessment of the adequacy of the accounting system and
internal financial controls, the reliability of the SOEs as a basis for Credit disbursements, and the degree
of compliance with agreed financial arrangements. During Negotiations, assurances were obtained
that: (i) independent auditors, acceptable to IDA, will be appointed in a timely manner to undertake
annual audits and prepare audit reports of the Project Accounts, Special Account, and SOEs for
each province, federal area, and the federal level; and (ii) certified copies of the audited Project
Accounts and the Special Account Statements - together with the auditor's report and opinion
thereon and a certified copy of the auditor's report and opinion on the SOEs - will be provided
to IDA within nine months of the end of each fiscal year.
C. Project Management and Implementation
213.  Since the proposed project follows a program approach, the arrangements for project management
and implementation are the same as those for the Pakistan's population program as a whole. The
program would be implemented by the Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW) and the associated
Provincial Population Welfare Departments (PWDs). Overall responsibility would lie with the Federal
Secretary of Population Welfare, with the federal Director General of Programmes as the project's main
focal point. Where necessary, the implementing agencies would be reinforced with technical assistance
and institutional modifications. (See Charts 1 and 2 for organizational charts of the MPW and a typical
Population Welfare Department.)

49
214.  In preparing the SAPP and the PWPP, the MPW and the Population Welfare Departments have
built on the five-year plans developed for the Eighth Plan period. Modified after review by IDA and the
cooperating donors, these plans were then approved by the Government through the standard PC-I
process. Under the SAPP, annual Operational Plans have now been substantially implemented for
1993/94 and 1994/95, and are largely complete for 1995/96, the first year of PWPP. They include a
budget Annual Development Programme for the year and Policy Matrix, or Implementation Plan agreed
upon for each provincial Population Welfare Department and for the federal MPW (see Annex 8). They
detail bottleneck issues and the steps to be taken to address them during the coming year and specify
monitorable targets, against which performance in addressing the issues can be measured. In addition,
agreement has been reached between the Government, IDA, and cooperating donors on the overall issues
to be addressed during the PWPP, a framework of steps to be taken, and guidelines for technical and
managerial measures to improve implementation in each major federal and provincial program
component. Under the proposed project, this overall issues framework would need to be updated in the
later years of its implementation. At Negotiations, the 1995/96 operational plan was confirmed.
215.  Implementation of the PWPP would be geared to coincide with the annual budget preparation
cycle. The main review would take place in March/April of each year. It would involve both an internal
review by federal and provincial officials and a review by IDA and the cooperating donors. These
reviews will focus on the implementation of the operational plan and on expenditures for the current year.
They would also draft operational plans and detailed expenditure plans for the coming year. If there were
no agreement on the future program, or if there were insufficient compliance with the current year's
program, corrective efforts would be sought in discussions looking towards eventual agreement. Funds
for reimbursement would only be available when agreement was reached. Following agreement, the
federal Ministry and the Population Welfare Departments will then finalize the budget and Policy Matrix
for the year. During Negotiations, assurances were obtained that: (i) annual operational plans for
each project year will be prepared and the activity, financial, and reform content of these plans vill
be agreed with IDA; and (ii) the Governnents will implement these agreed operational plans, and
monitor implementation progress, to the satisfaction of IDA.
216.   At present, the budgets require only federal approval.  However, an important part of the
decentralization measures involve improved coordination with and involvement in population activities
by the provincial governments.  Steps may be taken to include funds for population programs in
provincial budgets. In future, therefore, Provincial Assembly scrutiny and approval of project operational
plans may be required, and these plans may involve key reform measures. Once the plans have received
formal political approval, IDA and cooperating donors would make sure that they accord with present
agreements and confirm the amount of support available.
217.  Implementation would take place through federal and provincial agencies. Constraints arising
from the unusual administrative structure of the present population program are now being addressed.
and steps to decentralize authority to the provinces and below should improve implementation.
218.  Actions have also been taken to make the release of approved funds more timely, both at the
provincial level and in the field. The center has committed that, in the short term, adequate releases will
be made. From 1994/95, furthermore, the provincial population program will be financed through a tied
federal grant to the provincial governments' Annual Development Programme, which should substantially
reduce the provinces' dependence on the center for the release of funds. Further steps to delegate
financial authority within the provinces should help to speed the release of funds. During Negotiations,
Government confirmed that revised procedures for the release of funds to the provinces are now fully



50
integrated into the provincial Annual Development Programmes and Consolidated Funds and are operating
effectively. During Negotiations, assurances were obtained that, to facilitate full spending of the
budgeted amounts, funds will continue to be released to the Ministry and Departments of Population
Welfare by the fifteenth of the first month of every quarter.
219.  The multisectoral approach of the project would require the active involvement of a number of
ministries and departments (particularly dealing with health and education) and the private sector
(especially NGOs, private doctors, and social marketing through commercial channels).  Effective
coordination and participation of these various agencies is crucial. The program specifies a number of
steps to promote active coordination, including:
*     The establishment of intersectoral Population Councils at the highest level in the federal
and provincial Governments.
*     The establishment of senior-level federal and provincial working committees to focus on
such key activities as the provision of family planning services through health facilities.
*     Delegation of authority and provision of funds to appropriate implementing agencies
(such as the Ministry of Education for the school population education component).
Decentralization of authority to the provinces and to local governments.
-     The sustained involvement of implementing agencies (in addition to the Population
Welfare Ministry and Departments) in program preparation and review.
-     Greater autonomy for involved NGOs and other private sector population activities.
220.   It is intended that the district and tehsil would progressively become the focus of implementation
during the later years of the proposed PWPP. District and tehsil population officers would therefore have
substantially enhanced levels of authority. Regular district meetings on population would be held, chaired
by the District Commissioner or other senior officer. The existing tendency to implement individual
population program components independently would be replaced by a more integrated, area-based
approach that focused on the district and tehsil.  Mechanisms would be established to increase the
involvement of family planning clients, community groups, and field staff. With increased participation
would come greater enthusiasm, a greater focus on the client and better feedback from the field.
Experiments with different methods of increasing participation are now in hand for different types of
communities.
221.   The proposed project includes a phased plan designed to energize program management (Annex
12). This plan would orient program management more towards the lower levels of the organization,
towards the public, and towards results particularly in the field. Revised management responsibilities
with clearer priorities including greater stress on supportive supervision would be developed for each
administrative level. The recently approved Asian Development Bank-sponsored population project
includes a major management development effort designed to achieve these ends through consultancies
and training. The proposed PWPP's program approach, and particularly the annual review process,
would also contribute substantially to this process.



51
222.   To make sure that decisions are based on real data, efforts would also be made to strengthen and
expand the Management Information System (Annexes 10 and 12). The proposed expansion in the
number of field workers alone will impose significant additional demands on the management, supervision
and training systems. Because sound implementation also requires attention to the cost-effectiveness of
different components, the proposed project includes a framework for comparing costs and outputs (Annex
13) to assess different interventions.
Technical assistance
223.   Cooperating donors continue to provide key technical support for Pakistan's population program
in a wide range of areas. During the preparation of this project, an attempt was made to estimate and
analyze the total technical assistance requirements for the whole population program (Annex l1).
Although requirements for the later years of the program were difficult to predict, the gaps identified by
this analysis were relatively few.
224.   Government management of technical assistance is an area that needs to be strengthened in order
to make better use of the available resources. To facilitate the implementation and monitoring of the
program, the Government will contract for technical assistance from an institution of international
standing in the population field with experience in South Asia, which will provide consulting services
particularly in the areas of program monitoring, operational research, and the design and management
of technical assistance to provide consulting services in these and other areas (estimated cost US$ 4.5
million, see Annex I 1). This assistance would continue throughout the project and would contribute to
the annual overall review and the monitoring of individual project components, both for the Government
and for cooperating donors. The draft technical assistance contract for the appointment of consultants
to assist with program monitoring was cleared by IDA during Negotiations.
Monitoring and evaluation
225.   Given the comprehensiveness of the proposed PWPP and its focus on results, implementation-
oriented monitoring is essential.  The project's monitoring and evaluation system would help the
Government to assess the project's progress in implementing its annual work programs, and in the
adoption and implementation of the policy (and other measures) included in the operational plans. It
would also require a thorough, joint Government and IDA mid-term review and evaluation of the
project's impact on service coverage, quality, and family planning use. Annual assessments of the
project's progress in implementing its operational plans should enable it to substantially improve services.
(See Annex 10 for the core indicators and data sources agreed upon for the monitoring of each major area
and component of the project.) Prior to Negotiations, the Government and IDA agreed on the final list
of monitoring indicators. During Negotiations, assurances were obtained that a joint, Government
and IDA, Midterm Review of the project will be held before July 1997.
226.   A substantial cross-sectoral monitoring system has already been established through the SAPP and
is now in use. The PWPP would build on the population part of this system. The MPW and Population
Welfare Departments' established internal monitoring system provides reliable data on the distribution
of family planning services and contraceptives, under the PWPP. This system would be refined based
on experience gained from a continuing pilot scheme in the North-West Frontier Province.  Data
processing and dissemination could be improved to increase the utility of the information gathered.



52
227.   As of nlow output and outcome monitoring is typically addressed through a continuing program
of regular demographic and household surveys. These would be continued and enhanced. Additional
demographic and health surveys would be conducted every two years, and further initiatives would be
taken through the SAPP in conjunction with such efforts as the Pakistan Integrated Household Survey.
Monitoring is currently focused mainly at the federal and provincial levels. With a more decentralized
admiinlistrative system and increased feedback from the field, a greater focus on the district level and
helow should evolve. During Negotiations, assurances were obtained that: (a) the Federal and
Provinicial Governments, with the assistance of consultants to be recruited by the Federal
Government, will systematically moniitor program implementation, provide semi-annual progress
reports, and participate in annual Government and donor reviews; and (b) a Implementation
Completion Report will be submitted by the Government to IDA within six months of the closing
date of the project.
IDA Supervision requirements
228.   With no other IDA projects in Pakistan's population sector, the scope for economies through
multi-project supervision missions is limited. Some economies could be realized, however, through joint
Illissionls for health projects and for the later years of SAPP. Initial annual supervision requirements are
estimilated to be 12 staff weeks. (See Annex 18 for a project supervision plan).
IV. BENEFITS AND RISKS
229.   The project will help the Government achieve its population welfare objectives by supporting a
major expansion in family planning service availability, and informing and stimulating demand for
services. It will increase Pakistan's very low contraceptive prevalence rate and start to reduce its very
high fertility and population growth rates, which presently constrain economic and social development
in many ways. Services will be of higher quality, more available, and of increased effectiveness. The
project will help women attain their reproductive health goals, as well as provide a substantial number
of work opportunities to women. Ambitious targets have been set to expand population coverage to 100
percent in urban areas and 70 percent in rural areas, from 54 percent and 5 percent at present, a near
doubling of contraceptive use, and a 10 percent fall in fertility is projected. Important aspects of the
service delivery program include: a crucial new village outreach program (increasing village workers
f'rom a few hundred to 12,000, such that every village of 2,000 or more people has one worker),
increased service delivery points (including an extra 200 Family Welfare Centres and a doubling to 25 1
of Mobile Service Units); enlarged staff pre- and in-service technical training (over 4,000 trainees during
the five years): and strengthened population information activities to increase the effective demand for
family planniiing and generate community support.  Support for population objectives by other line
departmiienits and the private sector will improve sharply through provision of family planning services
hy provincial health departments and other line agencies, increased population education in schools,
expanded social marketing of contraceptives, and a doubling of NGO activities. The government will
benefit from the various institutional development components, particularly the management, planning
and technical skill enhancement activities. Lastly, through lower fertility and population growth, the
project will facilitate the achievement of human developnment and poverty alleviation, improve the health
and reduce the mortality of mothers and children, improve women's status, and reduce environmental
pressures.



53
230.   During preparation, attempts have been made to analyze and reduce project risks. But this
remains a risky initiative given poor past performance. The program as a whole faces two generic risks:
failing to increase contraceptive use sufficiently to meet the demographic and health objectives; and failing
to improve the availability and quality of services sufficiently to meet service demand. Underlying these
are four specific risks. The first, and critical, risk is that support for the program will fade as in the past,
due to political or religious pressures, or because results are slow in coming. Political support for the
program is currently high, but success for the program will de. qnd upon the Government maintaining
the conmuitment demonstrated in the past two years. The inclusion of population prominently in the SAP
and the SAPP helped strengthen political resolution. Through enhancing the communications program
for opinion leaders, the phased funding, and other practical ways being developed under the annual
agreement mechanism, stronger commitment will be fostered. But this will continue to be a difficult area.
The second risk is that the implementing agencies lack the management capacity to execute the ambitious
program, and that the program approach will strain this limited capacity even further. This risk is being
addressed by a program of management training and sustained technical support, by decentralization and
increased participation, by detailed development work, and by a continuing program of operations
research. The joint monitoring of progress, program development workshops, and annual agreements
will also make important contributions. The third risk, as happened in the past, is that poor performance
of some provinces and agencies, especially the health authorities, will persist mainly due to lack of
accountability for success. This risk will be mitigated by the already initiated decentralization reforms,
crucially by increasing the responsibility of the provinces for the program including requiring
accountability from other departments at senior levels. The increased level of provincial political support
and the SAPP efforts to improve inter-sectoral coordination and partnership are also important factors.
Finally, given the substantial contribution that the private sector is intended to make to the program, there
is a risk that this will be impeded by persistent problems of inadequate support for NGOs and recurrence
of earlier antagonism that the Government demonstrated to the commercial sector. Completing the
establishment of an independent Trust to channel Government and donor population support to NGOs
would greatly reduce this risk; relations with the private commercial sector are now reasonable and, in
future, companies will have increased autonomy over trading decisions. Although the project is risky,
the risks seem eminently worth taking in view of the potential for raising per capita living standards,
improving the status of women and producing substantial macroeconomic benefits.
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
231.   As discussed above, this project is based on annual agreements on operational plans - defined
to include budgets, program activities, and reform measures - which are made within the already
agreed: (a) implementation plan for the Eighth Five Year Plan period; (b) guidelines for technical and
managerial measures to improve implementation; and (c) the overall issues which are to be addressed
during the project. Examples from these documents are at Annex 8. Annually in March/April, the
Government will discuss with IDA and cooperating donors: (a) progress in implementing the population
program and the agreed reforms for the current year; and (b) their intentions for funding and content of
the population program and the details of the reforms for the coming year. If satisfactory progress has
been made during the past year and the program, budgets and reforms for the coming year are agreed,
then the next year of the population program will be supported with program funding from the PWPP.
232.   Annual agreements for the population program have been completed for 1993/94 and 1994/95
under the SAPP, and for 1995/96 (subject to final confirmation) under the proposed PWPP. Examples
from the agreed matrices and documents, in the Government's language, are at Annex 8. Similar
agreements will be made for subsequent years during the implementation of the PWPP. Satisfactory



54
progress in 1994/95 with the population aspects of the SAPP would be a condition for IDA to proceed
with the PWPP.
233.   During Negotiations, the Federal and Provincial Governments as appropriate provided assurances
that:
a.     program responsibility in the Federally administered areas will be transferred to those
areas with a status similar to that being followed for the Provinces (para. 149);
b.     no bans on the recruitment of service delivery staff will be introduced or enforced during
the project period (para. 156);
c.     the administrative status of population program staff will be resolved by December 1995
(para. 156);
d.     the National Trust for Population Welfare will continue to operate in a manner
satisfactory to IDA (para. 160);
e.     the overall level of spending on the population program will rise by at least 22 percent
a year (in constant price terms) in fiscal 1995/96 to 1998/99, and that the total size of the
PWP for the same period be Rs 10.34 billion, subject to the possibility that a different
percentage may be agreed in fiscal 1998/99, the first year of the Ninth Plan, with a
resulting different aggregate over the four-year period (para. 177);
f.     in fiscal 1995/96 to 1998/99 the Government's contribution will rise from 57 percent to
60 percent of total spending on population, including the Government financing an
increasing proportion of incremental costs (para. 177);
g.     (i) independent auditors, acceptable to IDA, will be appointed in a timely manner to
undertake annual audits and prepare audit reports of the Project Accounts, Special
Account and SOEs for each province, federal area and the federal level; and (ii) certified
copies of the audited Project Accounts and the Special Account Statements - together
with the auditor's report and opinion thereon and a certified copy of the auditors report
and opinion on SOEs -  will be provided to IDA within nine months of the end of each
fiscal year (para. 212);
h.     (i) annual operational plans for each project year will be prepared and the activity,
financial and reform content of these plans will be agreed with IDA ; and (ii) the
Governments will implement these agreed operational plans, and monitor implementation
progress, to the satisfaction of IDA (para. 215);
i.     to facilitate full spending of the budgeted amounts, funds will continue to be released to
the Ministry and Departments of Population Welfare by the fifteenth of the first month
of every quarter (para. 218);
j.     a joint, Government and IDA, mid-term review of the project will be held before July
1997 (para. 225);
k.     the Federal and Provincial Governments, with the assistance of consultants to be recruited
by the Federal Government, will systematically monitor program implementation, provide



55
semi-annual progress reports, and participate in annual Government and donor reviews
(para. 227); and
1.    an Implementation Completion Report will be submitted by the Government to IDA
within six months of the closing date of the project (para. 227).
234.  There is one special condition of Credit Effectiveness: satisfactory progress in implementing the
SAPP supported population program for 1994/95 (para. 177).
235.  Subject to the above understandings and conditions, the proposed project provides a suitable basis
for an IDA Credit of SDR 45.0 million (US$ 65.1 million) to the Government of the Islamic Republic
of Pakistan.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
The Demographic Situation in Pakistan: Recent Trends and Prospects
Introduction
1.    As the census has been postponed from 1991 to October 1994 (expected), estimates of the current
population of Pakistan are increasingly influenced by assumptions about levels and trends of fertility,
mortality, and migration since the previous census in 1981. Such assumptions are based on the findings
of several surveys conducted in the past decade. Unfortunately, these surveys have yielded different
estimates of vital rates, resulting in uncertainty about population size. This annex will review estimates
of fertility and mortality from the surveys and will present the most plausible estimates of demographic
indicators for Pakistan.
Fertility
2.     During the 1980s and early 1990s, four national surveys were carried out that produced estimates
of the total fertility rate (TFR). The 1984-85 Pakistan Contraceptive Prevalence Survey (PCPS) estimated
a TFR of 6.0, implying a decline of almost one point from estimated levels for the 1970s. However, the
results of the 1984-88 Pakistan Demographic Surveys (PDS) put the TFR back at the previous level ol
6.9. Establishing the level of fertility became even more difficult when the two most recent surveys
produced very different results. The 1990-91 Pakistan Demographic and Health Survey (PDHS) reported
a sharply reduced TFR of 5.4 for the period 1986-91, whereas the Pakistan Integrated Household Survey
(PIHS) of 1991 put the TFR at 6.3 for 1987-91.
3.     Various consistency checks of the data have led to the conclusion that the TFR derived from the
PDHS is too low. Women appear to have reported their children as older more often in this survey than
in the PIHS, resulting in current fertility estimates that are low relative to the reported number of children
ever born. If current fertility is adjusted to take account of such age misplacement, the TFR from the
PDHS would be 5.8. Another adjustment procedure suggests a TFR of 6.1 for 1987-91. In addition,
the estimate for contraceptive prevalence in the PDHS was well below the level expected for the reported
level of the TFR. On the basis of trends shown in the adjusted PDHS estimates, it can be concluded that
fertility did not decline very much until the late 1980s, when the survey data indicate a moderate decline.
4.     The recently begun fertility transition appears to result from rising age at marriage as well as
increasing contraceptive use. Between 1981 and 1991, the proportion of women aged 15 to 19 who were
never married increased from 70.6 to 75.2 percent, while the singulate mean age at marriage (a measure
of the mean number of years lived before first marriage) increased from 20.2 to 21.7. Contraceptive use
is concentrated in urban areas, where 26 percent of women were users in 1990-91 (31 percent in major
cities), compared to 6 percent in rural areas. In the urban areas, contraceptive use has increased from
16 percent (1984-85 PCPS), whereas in rural areas the surveys report no meaningful change. However,
a re-interview survey indicated that contraceptive use may be under-reported.
5.    It is evident from the surveys that different fertility patterns have emerged in urban and rural



71
Annex 2
Page 2 of 4
areas. Whereas in the 1970s rural fertility was below urban fertility, the 1980 surveys show urban TFRs
to be 0.7 to 1.2 points below rural levels. The overall decline in fertility is therefore largely the result
of declining urban fertility rates. Interestingly, provincial fertility differentials have been small, despite
considerable differences in levels of development across the regions. Sindh emerged as having the lowest
fertility, but this is primarily due to the large urban population of Karachi.
Table 1 summarizes survey findings on fertility for the country and for urban and rural areas.
Mortality
6.     Three of the four fertility surveys also collected data on infant and child mortality. Table 2
summarizes these findings. As with fertility, the findings present a conflicting story, with considerably
higher mortality in the PIHS than in the PDHS, with the PDS estimate intermediate. Not only are the
levels different, but the downward trend evident in the PDHS data is not nearly as clear in the PIHS data,
in which the decline starts only in the 1987-91 period. A re-interview round of some of the PDHS
respondents indicated considerable omission of infant deaths and cast doubt on the PDHS estimates.
Based on the re-interviews, the PDHS estimate of the IMR for the nine year period before the survey is
107. Thus, even with a downward trend in mortality, the IMR is likely to have been at least 100 around
1990. Infant and under-five mortality of males exceeds female mortality by about 15 percent and 10
percent, respectively.
7.     Since mortality rates at other than very young ages are largely unknown, mortality indicators such
as life expectancy at birth and the crude death rate are derived from model life tables selected on the basis
of survey-based infant and child mortality rates. Such model life tables indicate that life expectancy at
birth was about 59 years around 1990. It is very likely that in the reproductive ages, female mortality
rates continue to be higher than corresponding male rates. Estimates of the maternal mortality ratio cover
a broad range, from 227 to 756 maternal deaths per 100,000 live births, although it is clear that whatever
the exact ratio maternal mortality in Pakistan is high.
Population Estimates and Projections
8.     The World Bank population projections are made from a starting age-sex distribution to which
age-specific fertility, mortality, and migration rates are applied. The 1981 census age distribution was
projected back to 1980, and average five-year rates are applied iteratively to the base age distribution.
Net migration is assumed to be negative, and refugees living in camps are not included in the estimates.
With population growth of over 3.5 million per year, net migration is an insignificant component of
population change. Table 3 shows the projected population year by year for the period 1980 to 1995 as
well as the average five-year fertility and mortality rates used in the projection. These rates are based
on the surveys discussed above for the period 1980-89 and extrapolations for the period 1990-94.
9.     The projection shows that, while the population of Pakistan was about 83 million in 1980, by
1995 it will grow to about 130 million.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
History of Family Planning Programs in Pakistan and Lessons Learned
A. Historical Overview
1.    Family planning programs in Pakistan have undergone a variety of changes as they have
developed over the last forty years. These changes are characterized by different policies and strategies,
and can be divided into four distinctive periods.
2.    Initial period (1953-65). Family planning activities in Pakistan began in 1947 with small-scale
voluntary efforts in the private sector. These were institutionalized with the founding of the Family
Planning Association of Pakistan (FPAP) in 1953. The Government of Pakistan (GOP) first became
involved in the late 1950s but did not have a separate family planning program.
3.    Single-Purpose family planning (1965-77). In 1965, with the introduction of an independent
population program in the Third Five-Year Plan, Pakistan became one of the first large developing
countries to adopt an explicit population policy with specific fertility reduction goals. From 1965-1977,
the program, whose scope was limited to the population sector, aimed to increase contraceptive use
through various supply-side strategies.  The late 1960s saw the creation of a Family Planning Council
at the federal level and Family Planning Boards in the provinces as autonomous organizations to direct
and oversee the population program under the Ministry of Health.
4.     The major program components included using dais (traditional birth attendants) as outreach
agents, with each dai covering a population of 2-3 villages in rural areas or 2,000 people in urban areas.
Their task was to inform and motivate fertile couples to use contraceptives. The program also had a
strong emphasis on clinical contraceptives such as intra-uterine devices (IUDs). To compensate for the
shortage of medical personnel, special paramedical personnel (Lady Family Planning Visitors) under the
supervision of Lady Doctors were trained and hired primarily to perform  IUD insertion.  The
communications strategy included mass media and printed materials.
5.     The population program  under the Third Plan (1965-1970) was based upon the efficient
distribution of supplies and the motivation of potential acceptors. The program emphasized meeting
specific annual targets regarding the number of acceptors of traditional and clinical contraceptives at
national, provincial, and district levels. But the target-oriented approach turned out be ineffective in
reducing fertility. Pressure to achieve unrealistic goals and the indiscriminate spread of resources resulted
in low quality services and minimal effort to stimulate demand for family planning services in areas
(especially cities) where latent demand existed. The program was structured so that a large number of
people reported to a single supervisor, making it impossible to provide adequate supervision and on-the-
job training to family planning officers and field motivation staff.



75
Annex 3
Page 2 of 5
6.    In the early 1970s, following an evaluation of the population program which concluded that the
program to-date had little impact, there was a shift in outreach strategies. A new strategy called the
Continuous Motivation System (CMS) was introduced nationwide in 1973. The focus of the program was
shifted from achieving contraceptive distribution targets to effective communication with and follow-up
of clients. Under CMS, teams consisting of one male and one female, who were literate and educated,
visited couples at home about four times a year to motivate them to accept and continue contraception,
and provide them with contraceptives. However, the CMS had only a slightly greater impact on the
population growth rate than the previous target-oriented approach. One of the major problems of this
phase was the unstable political climate. Political events of 1971 delayed the start of CMS until 1973
and new disturbances in 1977 led to program cancellation; the program was never able to be properly
implemented. Another problem was the difficulty in meeting the qualifications required for couple teams.
Even after standards were relaxed, no more than 75 percent (much lower in some areas) of positions were
filled, and staff turnover was high.
7.     With a change in the leadership of GOP and the family planning program, a new contraceptive
inundation scheme (CIS) was initiated in 1975 to complement CMS. The objective of this scheme was
to overcome any contraceptive supply bottlenecks and to allow supply to create demand. With USAID
assistance, CIS was to distribute condoms and oral contraceptives through a network of 50,000
shopkeepers and local agents as well as field workers. Due to the inadequate distribution system and the
overestimation of available outlets, the CIS had little impact on contraceptive prevalence.
8.     Suspension of family planning programs (1977-80). Because of the after-effects of the 1977
general elections and the resulting transition to a military government, the program was suspended.
There were virtually no field activities or motivational campaigns during 1977-80. In 1978, the Family
Planning Council was abolished. Although family planning clinics continued to function, they received
little support, and performance and utilization indicators sank to an all time low.
9.    Multi-sectoral family planning (1980-present). A significant change in the population program
took place in 1980. First, responsibility for population was transferred from the Ministry of Health to
the Population Welfare Division (PWD) under the Ministry of Planning and Development. Second, the
family planning program was revived in the form of a Three-Year Population and Social Welfare Plan
(1981-84), which adopted a multi-sectoral approach. This was a significant change from the single
purpose, single-sector approach practiced previously. The new program emphasized links between the
population program and other development sectors of the economy, and incorporated maternal and child
health into the program, as well as the enhancement of opportunities for female education and
employment. The CMS was discarded due to lack of success in 1981. A broad-based Information,
Education and Communication (IEC) program, which was to promote the small family size norm, was
introduced. However, the three-year program had only limited effects on family planning indicators.
The transition from a single-purpose program to a multi-sectoral program was not fully achieved. The
problems in this period included inadequate technical support (especially for non-clinical management),
failure to clarify the division of responsibilities between the federal and provincial governments, and lack
of political support.
10.   The population welfare program under the Sixth Plan (1983-88) continued the multi-sectoral
strategy of the previous three-year Population and Social Welfare Plan. An important policy change in
this period involved the partial defederalization of activities. Provincial Population Welfare Departments



76
Annex 3
Page 3 of 5
(PWD) were established, with implementation responsibilities for field activities such as service delivery,
field supervision, program monitoring, and local information, education and communication activities.
The federal government retained responsibility for policy formulation, planning, training, procurement,
seeking foreign funding, social marketing of contraceptives, IEC coordination, overall monitoring and
evaluation and support for NGOs.
11.    During this period, steps were taken to strengthen administrative controls, ensure the supply of
contraceptives, and overhaul the service delivery system. In 1985, the NGO Coordinating Council
(NGOCC) was established to provide more flexible funding to NGOs in the hope that NGO service
delivery outlets would supplement population welfare activities. In 1986, the National Institute of
Population Studies (NIPS) was established to undertake research in population policies and issues, provide
policy guidelines for solution of problems, and to conduct independent evaluation of the population
welfare program. In 1986, Social Marketing of Contraceptives (SMC) was introduced to facilitate the
distribution of contraceptives through private channels.
12.    Despite ambitious targets (service outlets of the GOP's population welfare program were expected
to cover one-third of the population, while the outlets of other delivery systems were to cover the
remaining two-thirds), the effective coverage of FWCs was estimated to be only 10 percent at the end
of the Sixth Plan. Even if outlets operated by others were taken into account, the coverage would be 15-
20 percent ot the total population and less than 5 percent among the rural population. A major setback
was that the multi-sectoral approach was not yet fully operational. The performance of service outlets
of other department and agencies was unsatisfactory. In addition, a lack of technical supervision,
especially at the district and tehsil levels, and a lack of firm political commitment from the top
government leaders adversely affected the program.
13.   The policies and strategies of the population welfare program under the Sixth Plan were continued
in the Seventh Plan (1988-93). The program was characterized by an emphasis on integrating family
planning in the health structure, a shift toward clinical contraceptive methods, emphasis on improving
field supervision and increasing effectiveness of the IEC program, and promotion of greater involvement
of NGOS, hakeems and homeopaths. Outlets of the Health Departments were expected to provide family
planning services, in accordance with the 1985 decision of the Executive Commission of National
Economic Council (ECNEC), which mandated that all health outlets should provide family planning
services. Primary responsibility for training of staff and supply of contraceptives was given to PWD.
14.   In 1990, the PWD was moved from the Ministry of Planning and Development and was given
ministerial status as the Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW). The population program received open
political support from the top GOP leaders. In 1991, the then Prime Minister publicly supported family
planning and stressed the urgency of the population problem. He subsequently issued a directive to all
federal and provincial ministries/departments to extend full support for the population program. Between
1991 and 1993, the Accelerated Program was implemented in order to revitalize the on-going population
program. (Details of the present program are given in the main text.)



77
Annex 3
Page 4 of 5
B. Lessons Learned
15.   Some shortcomings and problems have persisted throughout the history of family planning
programs in Pakistan. Some of these have been recognized in recent programs, while others have not
been seriously addressed. A summary of these problems is presented below as a guide to future work
in the population sector.
Lack of Political Support
16.   Sustained, high-level political support and commitment to the population program were missing
until 1990. In the extreme case, this led to the abolition of the population program. The low priority
accorded to the family planning program eroded its ability to attract sufficient resources, resulting in sub-
optimal program performance. Moreover, without such high-level commitment, there was no incentive
for other development sectors and line agencies to provide full support to the program.
Administrative and Organizational Issues
17.   Lack of program continuity. Frequent changes in organizational structure and leadership have
created considerable uncertainty for staff and client groups. The program structure did not evolve
through a series of incremental adjustments, based on thorough evaluations, but rather emerged as a new
and different program whenever there was a change in management. As a result, programs did not have
a chance to develop beyond the initial stage. Although this tendency has reduced since the early 1980s,
the merits of modifying the program at the margin still need to be underscored.
18.   Lack of "regular" status in the budget. The population program continues to be financed from
the government's annual development budget rather than the regular budget, with unfavorable implications
on staff recruitment and development. Despite their induction into the regular civil service in 1982,
employees of the population program are not yet on the regular budget, creating job uncertainty. Failure
to integrate population staff into the regular civil service limits opportunities to build commitment toward
population goals within the civil service, and discourages recruitment of well-qualified professionals.
19.   Lack of a well-established relationship between the population and health departments. The
unique position of the population program within the Pakistan public administration system has created
difficulties in establishing a working relationship with ongoing health programs and personnel. This has
led to a history of animosity and poor coordination between population and health departments.
20.   Division of responsibilities between federal and provincial levels. There is continuing conflict
between federal and provincial authorities regarding such issues as supervisory responsibility, the status
of population staff relative to normal civil service positions, and fiscal responsibility for program
development. Although field responsibilities were transferred to the provinces in 1983, the real transfer
of power has been limited since all expenditures are still fully funded from the federal annual
development budget. The sub-optimal coordination between federal and provincial authorities has been
one cause of inefficient implementation.



78
Annex 3
Page 5 of 5
21.   Inadequate supervision. Past programs often did not provide adequate clinical and non-clinical
supervision. Part of this problem was due to the large number of people reporting to a single supervisor,
which has now been improved. Complicating the issue is the need for female field staff to be supervised
by a small number of female professionals.
Strategic Shortfalls
22.    Neglect of demand.  Supply-oriented strategies followed in the past have assumed that the
effective distribution of contraceptives and related services would by themselves lead to significant
improvements in utilization and continuation of family planning methods. However, given the social and
economic environment in which the program must operate, characterized by low rates of female literacy,
high infant mortality, and strongly pronatalist religious and social values, the neglect of demand-creation
strategies explains at least partly why the population programs have been ineffective.
23.    Weak outreach program. Efforts to influence demand through improved IEC and outreach
programs have been weak or ill-directed. The early emphasis on print media and public advertising via
billboards and pamphlets proved to be ineffective in reaching the largely illiterate, home-based target
population. Management of IEC continues to be restrictive and centralized, resulting in failure to
establish viable communication mechanisms to reach target groups. Although face-to-face strategies have
been recognized as a more effective outreach scheme, they are constrained by poor staffing and training
and lack of systematic approaches in planning and evaluation. Moreover, the focus of outreach has been
on creating new family planning acceptors of family planning, with minimal attention to follow-up of
existing clients, resulting in high drop-out rates.
24.   Insufficient supply system. In spite of the program's supply-oriented emphasis, coverage of, and
access to, family planning services are still limited, particularly in rural areas. The expansion in service
delivery outlets has been modest compared to the growth in population, and the quality of services less
than satisfactory. Inadequate equipment, staff absenteeism, depletion of contraceptive supplies, and poor
maintenance of records are common among service delivery outlets.
25.    Unrealistic target setting. Targets for birth rate reduction and contraceptive adoption were
unrealistically high and, consequently, were never achieved. Over-ambitious targets imposed unrealistic
expectations on the field staff and led to fabrication of performance data, which in turn made objective
evaluation impossible. Because the program was never able to meet the targets, it was often unfavorably
judged, contributing to low staff morale.
26.   Inadequate monitoring and evaluation.  The past programs lacked built-in monitoring and
evaluation systems. Major gaps included overreliance on ad hoc survey work in the early stage of the
program, and later, failure to develop a service statistics system which could be used for analyses of
continuation rates and actual use. This led to confusion between the distribution of methods and actual
use, resulting in a false sense of confidence in program progress.



79
Annex 4
Page 1 of 3
PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Organization of the Population Program in Pakistan
Multi-Organizational Issues and Relationships
1.    Pakistan's overall population effort involves a number of key organizations, including the
Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW) and the provincial Population Welfare Departments, the Ministry
of Health and the provincial Health Departments, various NGOs, the Ministry of Education and other line
departments, semi-government organizations such as Pakistan Television (PTV), and the private sector,
notably the press and social marketing organizations. In addition, other providers of health services such
as registered medical practitioners, traditional birth attendants (TBAs), homeopaths, and hakeems also
play a part. As Fig. 1 indicates, there is some hierarchy to the relationships among these various
organizations and groups, reflected in varying levels of involvement in the planning and implementation
of the population program. While these various organizations, groups and individuals do not necessarily
share the same activities, it is important that they all work in a coordinated manner toward achievement
of the same policy goals and objectives. MPW, as the chief organization in the program, needs to take
the lead in coordinating the activities of, and allocating available resources among, participating
organizations/groups, in order to make most effective use of limited resources and to improve overall
program achievements.
2.    Given MPW's strategy of limiting its own role and expanding delivery of population services
througlh other organizations and groups, ensuring coordination and good management is essential to the
program's success. Inter-organizational relationships are therefore critical. MPW needs to develop a
service and support function whose 'clients' are other organizations and groups, in order to:
a.     minimize service delivery duplication;
b.     maximize sharing of materials and resources, particularly human resources;
c.     ensure sharing of information, from both national and international sources;
d.     ensure sharing of national and international technical assistance; and
e.     ensure funding of any activities that demonstrate high impact.
3.     PWD's relationship with the Health Departments is particularly critical, since they have the
capacity to expand and strengthen service delivery. However, the relationships have had a history of
animosity. Moreover, adding family planning services to Health's already long list of tasks will mean
that MPW will have to compete for time and attention. The provision of family planning services by
Health Department staff will, therefore, have to be well planned and relatively simple to implement, or
it is probable that they will not be fully provided.



80
Annex 4
Page 2 of 3
4.     The relationships with the various groups which are independent of the Government, must also
be supportive and positive. These groups are all private operators that are not controlled by GOP. To
elicit their cooperation, MPW  must relate to them as if they were clients. Their participation needs to
be supported and, if possible, rewarded. The annual IEC press contests are a good example of how
relatively inexpensive incentives can stimulate widespread participation in a program activity without
direct payment for action or service.
5.     Improved inter-organizational relationships are needed at all levels. They will also benefit from
special review from time to time. As part of the preparation of the present project, representatives of
all  the   key   organizations   met
periodically.  This proved extremely

7.     There also may be some benefit in examining changes in structures and relationships at field level.
For example, the introduction of the VFPWs and a major expansion of MSUs, are likely to benefit from
increased interaction and a direct relationship. Relationships with the new Village Health Workers and

other field units of the Departments of Health will have to be worked out. Improvements in the
organization and functioning of the PWD will be facilitated through current technical assistance in the
areas of organization and management funded by the Asian Development Bank.
8.    The MPW workplan calls for a top-to-bottom organizational analysis in 1994/95. This exercise
will focus on questions concerning structures and how they facilitate or inhibit performance.  To
maximize effectiveness, the exercise should begin at the bottom, that is, at community and tehsil levels,
and proceed with the analysis by working upwards.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Developing a Program Approach for the Pakistan Population Sector
The Program Approach
1.     The current interpretation of 'program  approach' planning, negotiating, and agreeing foreign
assistance implies that more 'upstream' types of planning will be done and/or negotiated between the
donor organization and the government. 'Upstream' planning embraces policy and strategic planning,
whereas project planning usually covers only one or more of the program's broad components, with
policies  and   strategies  of
secondary  interest.   It also

4.     The program approach implies that both Government and the partner donor organization will
manage guided by policy and strategy. While this has been the textbook recommendation for top
management of large organizations for over twenty years, it is rarely done. One of the reasons is that
the structure presented in Figure 2 is rarely worked out. A set of policies may be written, but if they
are not programmatically connected to strategies and operational components, then they cannot be used
as a basis for management decisions. Therefore, clarifying and agreeing this program 'pyramid' and how
it will fit together, top to bottom, is necessary in order to agree what the policies and strategies will imply
in practice, how decisions regarding them will be made during implementation, and how progress will
be measured. Without this comprehensive review and agreement process, the policy/strategy consensus
essential to a program approach is at risk of producing objectives that are not connected to feasible



84
Annex 5
Page 3 of 12
implementation realities. Therefore, the program approach requires that donor organizations review not
only 'upstream' but 'downstream' aspects of the program as well, so that in partnership with the
Governmnent they can develop and maintain a comprehensive view.
5.      The paragraph above mentions the need to measure the achievements of policies and strategies.
This implies that the program pyramid needs to have its mirror image in the monitoring and evaluation
system (see Annex 10). Thus, the entire system can be represented as a diamond rather than a pyramid,
as shown in Fig. 3. Only with the monitoring and evaluation half mirroring the planning half can
managers begin to manage by policy and strategy. The 'feedback' lines on the left of the diamond must
be real and practical. The program approach necessitates that senior managers and planners must manage
the entire spectrum.
6.      In a project approach, analyzing the 'big picture' at the outset and during implementation is
relatively easy for both parties. Each can select their favorite component(s) and focus on them.
However, senior manager and planners may just pay lip service to the overall goals and achievements
of the sector's program  when following the conventional project cycle.  The program  approach
necessitates agreeing on the total program hierarchy not only at the outset, but also throughout
implementation. The annual reviews maintain the policy dialogue which is a decision-making/revision
process--a process of managing by policy/strategy.  The successful design and implementation of this
approach will essentially force government and donor managers to focus on the program's priorities and
ways to improve the ability of the concerned organization(s) to achieve those objectives.
