7.     The focus on long-term outputs and results, which is a fundamental difference between project
and program approaches, implies that managers will have to remain flexible. By definition, in social
change programs not all developments relevant to program achievements are predictable. Therefore, if
managers are to be rewarded for achievement of long-term results, and nothing else, they must be allowed
to alter the program's components if any of these fail to perform or perform significantly less well than
others, e.g. with respect to cost-effectiveness. Without this, there will be little real change from the
project approach. It is like telling a construction manager to build a pyramid, for which he/she will be
paid only when the last cube is in place on top, but prohibiting him/her from changing any materials,
tools, or work arrangements during the construction. In construction, nothing is ever built exactly to
plan, which is why many authorities require both the pre-approved and the 'as built' plans. The program
approach recognizes that there will be differences between as-planned and as-implemented. Previously,
such differences were considered indications of poor planning or management or both. Now it is
recognized that they reflect operational reality that must be accommodated by good program management.
Unless this principle is recognized, program managers are at great risk of operating the 'activity trap'--
many people in the organization are busy, but there is no productive output that moves the organization
toward its policy objectives.
8.     Comprehensive analysis and decision making in the program approach must be followed with
comprehensive support. Ideally, the community of donor organizations will provide funding so that all
agreed program components can be fully operationalized as planned and phased and financial resources
are not a limiting factor to the program's managers. The latter should never have to say, 'we couldn't
do X because we didn't have money.' Valid constraints to effective performance could be any of the
following:
a.    lack of numbers and/or able, experienced staff;
b.    lack of ability to change the way staff performn due to organizational inertia;
c.    lack of needed hardware or software (organizational) technology to assist in solving
problems;
d.    lack of willingness on the part of managers or staff to adopt changes in practices;
e.    lack of time for a newly developed and installed system to  operate effectively and
generate the expected improved output/results; and
f.    lack of resources such as equipment, supplies, transport, information, telecommunication,
facilities, etc.--these should only be short-term constraints since adequate funding should
be available in the medium term.
This type of program assistance should constantly test the organization's managerial and operational
absorptive capacity.
9.     The program approach implies that the donor community is prepared to fund all components of
the program. In the development of the current project, IDA, the largest potential donor and the advocate
of a program approach, fortuitously became the last donor to become involved in assisting GOP. This
more easily allowed IDA to become the donor of last resort, that is, to fill any funding gaps which either



86
Annex 5
Page 5 of 12
the Government or the other donors could not fill.  'Picking up the missing pieces' might seem an
unattractive position to many donors, since it appears to minimize potential influence. However, with
this approach, funding can be seen in its proper perspective, that is, as an input. The funding input is
obviously important for whatever it supports, but most important it 'buys' the donor a seat at the policy
dialogue table which, if appropriately used in partnership with the Government, is the most significant
influence that donors should have or seek.
10.    As mentioned above, the program approach requires active donor cooperation and coordination.
As the current process of project preparation has demonstrated, this does not necessarily imply total and
direct joint program planning among the donors. As long as the donors are willing to actively and openly
cooperate with each other, the process can work no matter when a new donor enters the donor 'pool.'
All donors need not apply the program approach in the operational mechanics of thei- respective
relationship with the Government. However, they must acknowledge the part they are playing in the
overall program assistance effort and must coordinate their operational and financial inputs so that these
can be managed by senior managers in the same manner as all other assistance.
11.    The task of the program approach is to specify a program to a stage at which funding and
operating organizations can agree on what should happen (policy, strategy, and operational objectives);
with what results (outputs); and with what requirements (conditions, resources, and time). If information
concerning the program were complete, then all potential donors could analyze the program plan and
make their judgements. However, since sector programs will tend to have long time frames, rarely would
detailed work plans for the entire program period be required before program approval. Planning at a
detailed level five years in advance would in most cases amount to wasted effort. This implies that
approval has to be given on the basis of less than complete information at each level of planning. The
'art' of program planning, therefore, is to decide in each situation when there is adequate informationi for
each level of planning, in addition to the appropriateness and feasibility of the technical, organizatiolnal,
managerial, economic/financial, political, and operational content of the plan(s).
Stages of Proiect Development
12.    The general characteristics of the program approach can be summarized as follows:
a.     it is based on agreements about the entire sector in the long term (at least five years)
including:
i.     what will happen -  the sector's overall policies;
ii.    how policies will be implemented -  overall strategies;
iii.    how strategies will operate -  broad comporients;
iv.    how the program will be macro manZaged over the entire project period, that is,
how  policy  and  strategic  planning, monitoring  and  evaluation  will be
implemented;
v.     macro management over long periods accepts the inevitable need for strategic and
possible policy change; therefore, the agreements must be structured to allow
flexibility. As noted above, while macro policies are not expected to change
significantly over the life of the project, strategies at the end could be different
than those at the start;



87
Annex 5
Page 6 of 12
vi.    the agreed policy and strategy framework will be coupled with defined outputs
for each strategy, which will be the strategic monitoring indicators.
b.     framing the assistance agreement around the points listed above results in a macro
outcome-based 'contract. ' It is similar in nature to consulting or construction contracts
which are 'product delivery based' as opposed to 'time and materials based.' Exactly
how and using which short-term strategies the product is delivered is up to the contractor;
c.     while delivery will be up to the 'contractor,' part of developing the agreement will be
macro feasibility testing of the proposed program and the contractor's organizational and
managerial capacity to deliver the program's 'products.' In a sense, this resembles pre-
qualification of the contractor;
d.     financial assistance to the program  may be proportionately distributed across the
policy/strategy framework in harmony with each strategy's resource demands; or the
distribution may be selective and cover only some strategies--this will depend on the
judgment of the 'contractor.' For example, the organization may want to protect the
resource position of some strategies and external assistance may help to guarantee this.
In either case, the resources themselves should have no direct impact on the micro
management and operations of the program, that is, middle managers should not do
anything differently with or without the program assistance.
13.    The stages in development and implementation of program assistance are as follows:
a.     Pre-planning.    In  developing  effective  program-approach  assistance,  a joint
government/donor planning team should reach agreement concerning the following:
i.     the status of existing or current planning efforts (stage of completion, level of
completion, and quality of completion) for all levels of planning--policy, strategy,
broad component or project plans, and work plans;
ii.    the relationship of this planning process to other international, national, regional,
provincial, and local planning efforts;
iii.   the categories of information required for each level of planning;
iv.    the integration of planning efforts so that all decisions made are internally
consistent and all planning-generated information clearly relates to other levels
whether 'downwards' and 'upwards';
b.     Appraisal.
1.     since program assistance covers the entire sector (or major sub-sector), the design
process must analyze the programmatic and organizational functions and
operations of the entire sector;



88
Annex 5
Page 7 of 12
ii.    in most sectors there are many and varied functions and operations which occur
at different levels and locations; therefore, the links among the common ones
must be analyzed both horizontally and vertically in order to test the feasibility
of efficient performance;
iii.   the conclusions of this assessment are then reviewed and agreements made about
any needed changes in the policy or strategy aspects--these may concern either
programmatic or organizational/managerial policies and strategies;
iv.    a modality for the program's financial assistance must be agreed;
c.     Negotiations.  The final policy and strategy framework will be agreed, including
conditionalities (particularly those which focus on mechanismiis to ensure flexibility),
along with the related financing mechanisms and expected quantity distributions (both
strategic and time based);
d.     Program Implementation.
i.     the implementing organization(s) will make any needed mnacro-level organization
and/or management changes necessary as agreed.  In most cases, these will
probably relate to setting up policy/strategic planning as well as linked
monitoring/evaluation systems (the external program assistance agencies will be
able to interact with program managers only if and when these tools are
operational).  At the beginning of the project, organization and management
changes will likely be a higher priority than programmatic changes;
ii.    external supervision will be done jointly with senior program  management,
focusing on review of strategic outputs and joint decisions concerning needed
policy and/or strategy changes, if any.  This may or may not involve the
redefinition of strategic outputs.  Making changes in broad components or the
nature of component operations will be up to the program's managers.
e.     Program Evaluatiotn.
i.     Depending on the project's duration, there may or may not be a major mid-term
evaluation. Any such evaluation would aggregate all monitoring and evaluation
information generated during implementation and would assess macro progress.
This should be fed into a strategic planning exercise where the entire set of
policies and strategies is reviewed and needed changes decided;
ii.    in a project of shorter duration, this type of overall assessment should be
completed at the end of the assistance and fed into the policy and strategic
planning exercise for the next program period.



89
Annex 5
Page 8 of 12
Pakistan 's Population Welfare Program
14.    The program approach is particularly well suited to the population sector in Pakistan for the
following reasons:
a.     achieving the policy and strategic goals of Pakistan's population welfare programll will
require a multi-sectoral effort. While these efforts can and should vary by delivery
organization, geographic area, administrative level, etc., they all have the same comlmiloil
goals;
b.     at this stage of program development, there is considerable need for expalnsioll coupled
with experimentation on how to achieve this cost-effectively.  A flexible program
approach will support a strategic decision-making process and adjustment of resources
as decisions are made about what works and what does not;
c.     Since the population welfare program is trying to change humani behavior, it is difficult
to predict macro resource requirements, particularly in a program which is rebuildinig:
and
d.     The sector's several components, the number and variety of organizationls involved, and
the unavoidable political overtones together result in a complex program 'puzzle,' yet the
sector's policy goals are few and relatively simple. The program approach can help to
keep the focus on the common goals, since the approach rewards achievements, niot
activities.
Preparation Activities
15.   In order to begin preparation of IDA's program support project, the missioll discussed with the
Government. and particularly with the Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW). IDA's understanding of
the program support approach and how it differed from the project approach. They also advised the
Government that as far as they were aware, the program approach had not yet been applied in the
population sector in any other country. In addition to IDA's lack of experience, the Governmenit was
advised there were no specific guidelines on how to develop such an approach. The Governmiielnt Is
understanding was therefore solicited as the mission engaged in the process of ilventing the developmnenit
of a program approach project without a clear picture of the format or contents of such a project.
16.    The mission reviewed the draft federal PC-1 and one provincial draft PC-I (Punjah) in order to
gain understanding of the broad program components being proposed. To expand theil understandinig
of these proposals, the mission then met with a variety of managerial and program staff and reviewed
plans, recent performance, and problems.
17.    At the same time, work began on the development of format frameworks which would provide
a common guide for further project development. These frameworks went through several trials. The
major concern in the design of these formats was to create a tool which senior MPW managers along with
World Bank staff could actually use during program implementation as guides for decision making. As
part of the process, it was necessary to conceive how the various levels of the planning/management



90
Annex 5
Page 9 of 12
system  might be structured and hiow  it could relate to the monitoring/evaluation/MIS system  which
generates most of the information used by managers.
18.    It was agreed that the point of departure should be some type of policy/strategy framework. A
draft of this framework was derived from the contents of the federal and provincial (Punjab) PC-ls. The
broad components found in the PC-Is were then entered into the framework.
19.    The next step was to expand the basic policies, strategies, and broad programs within the
framework to identify the major planning, implementation, and monitoring/evaluation activities, with
indicators for what would be expected year by year. Coupled with each activity was a statement of
expected major outputs at the end of each year. Based on the nature of the activity and the output, the
nature of the expected major strategic decision was also defined (see Annex 10).
20.    The detailed framework to analyze each federal and provincial component (e.g. the 18 identified
at the federal level in the PC- I) includes the categories of information specified below for implementation
plans over six years (two years of SAP assistance plus four years of project assistance). It is recognized
that each component, in keeping with the GOP's overall plan, will relate to a set of activities continued
from the present or started during the five-year period. In keeping with the program approach, these
categories of information are an attempt to define the major or macro plan elements, not the micro
aspects. Therefore, each component should be defined in at most two pages, and probably less. Annual
reviews will discuss and agree on details within the five-year framework.
a.      Cotmiponenit Objective (defined verv briefly).
i.      what is to be done/achieved, defined as a result or product', e.g.
( I )    changed, expanded, improved knowledge, attitude, or practice/behavior;
(2)    increased quantity of persons served and/or quality of service of those
being served;
(3)     changes  in  quantities  or  overall  performance  of organizations'
involvement in the program; and/or
(4)     quantity or quality of information produced or studies completed;
ii.     why is this to be done, that is, why is this component important to the total
program;
iii.    by wlleni is it to be achieved--in most cases this will he the end of the project
period;
iv.     where is the objective to be achieved--in most cases this will be countrywide, but
several componients are targeted for specific areas;
'Result/product objectives are used here rather than process objectives. Process objectives, which also have an
important role, are included under Component Indicators below. Examples ot process objectives would be to coniduct X
number of training courses or to procure Y number of contraceptives.



91
Annex 5
Page 10 of 12
v.     who will implement the objective--in most cases this will be one or more
organizations or an organizational unit; 'who will benefit?' should be defined
under 'what';
b.     Component Strategy. This should define briefly how the objective will be achieved. In
addition to the nature of the strategic change planned for, the definition should specify
the type of activity(ies) to be altered. In general, the following strategic choices can be
identified, which may or may not be mutually exclusive:
vi.    developing a completely new activity or group of activities;
vii.   expanding an existing activity(ies) to new geographic areas, new groups in the
same areas, or more persons within the same groups in the same areas;
viii.  improving quality and/or effective performance of an existing activity(ies),
without major changes in quantity of activity;
ix.    diversifying existing activity(ies) without changing the fundamental type of
activity; and
x.     reducing existing activity(ies) in scope or coverage--while this would not be
frequent, it is a valid strategic choice.
c.     Component Contents. This should be a brief summary of the major operational plans
cross-referenced to the more detailed plans in the PC-1 and/or the 'Operational &
Implementation Plans' volume. Only in cases where major departures from the PC-I and
operational plans are proposed would details be necessary; such cases are likely to be
few.
d.     Component Indicators. These should specify:
xi.   the nature of the indicators which will measure the quantity and/or quality of
change expected under the 'what' aspect of the component objective. The
primary indicator should generally be only one item which demonstrates that the
overall results are being achieved, but this may be supplemented with one or two
progress indicators which measure annual changes in component content or major
operations;
xii.   the current and end-of-project levels for each indicator. (The end-of-project level
could be in absolute or relative terms depending on the circumstances);
xiii.  in order to monitor and supervise, GOP and Bank managers will need annualized
indicators against which to measure progress toward the final objective. It is not
to be expected that the difference between the current level and the desired future
level will be achieved in a straight-line progression;



92
Annex 5
Page 11 of 12
xiv.   the method of measurement. specified as the manner in which the indicator can
be best monitored. e.g. through normal organizational records, periodic audits,
special surveys, etc.
e.     Comnponent Issuies (if any). Key issues that would be major impediments to effective
component implementation which exist or can be anticipated should be identified. In
general, the resolution of these key issues will require major GOP policy or strategic
decisions and should be scheduled as major decision points during the five years. If they
are current issues needing early resolution, they may be candidates for Conditions of
Negotiations or Effectiveness. Issues may be classified into the following types:
xv.    economnic/financial - not enough resources allocated to produce a 'critical mass'
of activity. Thus, a given componenit would not have a reasonable chanice ot
making a significant impact or achievinig its objectives-
xvi.   organiizationial capability/capacity - the organizationi(s) designated to achieve a
componient's objectives do not have the requisite operational scope (geographic
or programmatic), operational systems, experienced maniagers, or experienced
staff (particularly technical staff); developing these during the project period
would often be difficult;
xvii.  hum7Zani resouirces - availability of persons in-country who have thie requisite
experience and skills to achieve component objectives are very limited (no matter
how they are organized); developing skills throughi traininig during the project
period is likely to be difficult;
xviii.  political support/public opiniion - while political support for the overall
population program may he adequate, support for a given componenit may be
weak, or it seems unlikely that support wvill easily develop during the project;
xix.   technology development - one or several of the key techinologies needed to
effectively achieve a component's objectives do not exist or are not currently
generally used in the country;
xx.    bureaucratic processes & procedures - while one or several organizations
seemingly may have the capability/capacity to achieve the desired objective, their
respective procedures, systemns or other maniagerial functions may be significant
constraints that put achievement of objectives at risk.
21.    The preparation missions classified sonie of these issues, so as to assist the GOP an(d the Bank
to see common patterns of issues which may call for some program-wide or cross-comilponent
solutions.
22.    These broad component definitions were also aggregated to a more macro level in order to
portray:
a.     how the overall program will be implemilented over time;



93
Annex 5
Page 12 of 12
b.     where and what are expected to be the major management decisions (what seems critical
and marginal); and
c.    how different strategies and components are grouped and linked.
23.   While the exact format of the program-approach documentation is under development, its intent
and requirements will be more general than PC-ls. While the Bank is seeking to alter its process and
documentation requirements, the GOP has not yet taken a similar decision. The past practice of a
relatively complete textual and micro-financial match between the PC-1 and the SAR will no longer be
necessary, and the relationship between the two documents must be seen in a different perspective. The
SAR must encompass the PC-1 but need match only at the level of policy, strategies, and the scope of
broad components with their related broad allocations.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Summary of Provincial and Federal Program Components
1.    This annex summarizes the provincial and federal components of the Population Welfare
Program. It is divided into two main parts: (a) the provincial program components, which are
primarily concerned with service delivery; and (b) federal program components, which cover clinical
and non-clinical training, contraceptive procurement, NGO coordination, target group institutions
(TGIs), research, monitoring and evaluation, and the population program in the federal areas. Details
mainly relate to the Eight Five Year Plan period and the associated PC-ls.
Provincial Program Components
2.    Since many program components are similar across provinces, this section presents: (a) a
description of the objectives and strategies of each component; followed by (b) a matrix showing
comparable activities across provinces. Except where indicated, the component will be undertaken in
all the four provinces (Punjab, Sindh, North-West Frontier Province, and Balochistan).
3.     Objectives and strategies
a.    Family Welfare Centres (FWC) component. The objective is to improve coverage,
quality, and efficiency of the family planning service outlets and thereby increase
contraceptive prevalence. This component will rationalize and expand the distribution
of FWCs and introduce a quality improvement system. The main activity is to
establish new FWCs, with a mechanism for quality improvement, distributed
appropriately in both urban and rural areas.
b.    Reproductive Health Service Centres (RHS-Cs) component. The goal is to increase
service coverage of Reproductive Health Service centres and the use of clinical
contraceptive methods. The component will expand the geographic distribution of
RHS centres and introduce a quality improvement plan.
c.    Mobile Service Units (MSU) component. The objective is to increase service coverage
by Mobile Service Units and use of clinical contraception, especially in rural areas.
The component will expand the number of fully functioning Mobile Service Units and
improve the management support and monitoring.
d.     Village Based Service Delivery component. The goals are increased service coverage
and contraceptive use in rural areas. The component will expand village-based
services beyond present pilot areas, primarily through increasing the number of active
and fully supported Village Family Planning Workers (VFPWs).



95
Annex 6
Page 2 of 16
e.     Traditional Birth Attendants (TBA) component. The objective is to increase service
coverage and contraceptive use in rural areas. The component will increase the
number of TBAs actively involved in family planning service delivery and motivation,
and improve supportive links with the population welfare and health system. (Punjab,
Sindh, North-West Frontier Province, and Balochistan)
f.     Provincial Line Department component. The objective is to expand the coverage of
the population program cost-effectively through improved interdepartmental
collaboration. The component aims to improve implementation efficiency at health
outlets of provincial line departments (e.g. health, labor, and local government) in
providing family planning services. The strategies include expanding the service
delivery network, increasing the output of trained staff in line departments,
coordinating reporting and monitoring, and providing the health outlets with
contraceptives, equipments, IEC materials and sign boards. (Punjab, Sindh, North-
West Frontier Province, and Balochistan)
g.    Registered Medical Practitioners (RMP) component. The goal is to increase the
availability and use of family planning in the urban and peri-urban areas. The
component will increase the number of RMPs involved in motivation and delivery of
family planning and improve provision of support from the population program. The
RMP component will be managed and monitored by the provincial PWD officers and
implemented by district and tehsil officers.
h.     Hakeems and Homeopaths component. The objective is to increase the acceptability
and availability of family planning services in urban and rural areas. The component
will increase the number of hakeems and homeopaths as family planning agents to
carry out motivation and delivery of family planning services. The provincial offices
will be responsible for planning and managing the component as a whole, leaving
responsibility for implementation to the district and tehsil levels.
i.    Information, Education, and Communication (IEC) component. The goals are to
increase fertile couples' knowledge regarding family planning, to increase social
acceptance of family planning, and to heighten awareness and motivation for adopting
family planning methods. The component will slightly expand the existing mass
media activity and improve targeting, substantially increase community and
interpersonal promotion of family planning, improve quality of group education and
client counseling, and diversify family planning promotion activities.

Federal Program Components
5.     Nongovernmental Organization (NGO) component. The objective is to increase availability,
acceptability and use of contraceptives by mobilizing the support of NGOs to promote and provide
family planning services. The component will increase government and donor funding for NGOs,
expand family planning services through community-based distribution projects, improve NGO's IEC
and monitoring activities, and improve NGO networking and coordination. A major new
development is the proposed establishment of the National Fund for Population Welfare, to replace
the NGO Coordination Council (NGOCC). The specific objectives of the proposed NGO program
reflect the government's commitment to broaden the scope of NGO participation in population and,
more broadly, to create institutional capacity at the community level to enable communities to
participate in the delivery and management of social services (See Annex 17, Nongovernmental
Organizations in Population, for details).
6.     Main activities.
a. In the area of reproductive health services, the program aims to: develop the capacity of
NGOs and community-based organizations to promote and deliver quality reproductive health
services; expand NGO community based service delivery; experiment with innovative methods
of delivery, cost-recovery and integration of reproductive health with other social services;
increase the participation of communities in the planning, delivery, monitoring and
management of reproductive health services; and improve links between communities, NGOs
and government services.
b.   The NGO program also aims to: increase demand for basic social services; and increase
the involvement of the private sector in IEC activities.
c.  To institutionalize population as an integral part of development, the program will
undertake activities to: broaden awareness of population and the social and economic factors
that influence desired family size, amongst NGOs and CBOs; and assist and support NGOs



102
Annex 6
Page 9 of 16
with the integration of population concerns/activities into their program planning and
operations.
d. Lastly, as part of its institutional development activities, the program will: develop in-
country NGO technical and managerial capacity to enable (a) to (c); support community-
based institution building to enable (a) to (c); support the development of the Population
Welfare Trust Fund; and facilitate NGO networks, information-sharing and coordination.
7.     Target Group Institute (TGI) Component. TGIs are the main public sector organizations,
employ a large work force and have their own health service outlets. The employees of TGIs and
their families do not normally use other public- or private-sector health facilities. The objective of
this component is to expand program coverage by providing family planning services at health outlets
of TGIs. This includes the training of TGI health staff, provision of contraceptives and IEC
materials, and an improved reporting system.
8.     Main activities
a.     Expand family planning facilities of TGIs from 174 to 450.
b.     Train 238 female doctors, 23 gynecologists, 421 female paramedics, and 24
motivators.
c.     Supply TGIs with contraceptives. In the five year plan period, this component aims
to provide 11 million condoms, 440,000 oral pills, 80,000 IUD cases, 300,000
injectables, and 28,000 contraceptive surgery cases.
d.     Provide TGIs with IEC materials including signboards for every outlet to indicate the
availability of services, posters and charts to promote family planning, films to be
shown at the outlets, and information pamphlets regarding family planning to be
distributed to visiting clients.
9.     Information, education and communication component. The objectives are to increase the
knowledge of fertile couples regarding family planning and to improve social acceptance to motivate
them to seek modern contraceptive methods. The component will slightly expand the existing mass
media programs (T.V., radio, and press), substantially expand the interpersonal communication
activities, diversify the existing activities, and improve quality and targeting of activities.
10.    Main activities
a.     Launch communication campaigns, which will be reinforced and repeated at regular
intervals, through radio, television, press, printed materials, films, traditional media
(such as stalls and give-away items), participatory activities (such as rallies), and
interpersonal communications (such as Village-based Family Planning Workers and
TBAs). The mass media campaigns primarily target the urban population, which has
higher literacy, while the mainstay of the campaign in rural areas is interpersonal.



103
Annex 6
Page 10 of 16
b.     In the five year plan period, prepare/publish about 4,000 radio spots, 6000 TV
jingles, 2,000 press advertisements, 1.2 million calendars/posters, 82,000 brochures,
1.25 million method booklets, and 17 seminars/workshops. For interpersonal
campaigns, 12,000 Village Based Family Planning Workers, over 7,000 TBAs, and
more than 2,500 male and female family welfare assistants as well as
RMPs/hakeems/homeopaths will be involved.
11.    Clinical Training component. The goal is to provide an adequate number of competent
clinical personnel through improved pre-service and in-service training programs in the Regional
Training Institutes (RTI) and other venues throughout the country. The component will increase the
number of those trained, improve the quality of training, assist the health institutions to improve their
family planning training, and improve planning and coordination of training with other agencies.
12.    Main activities
a.     Collect baseline data for selected indicators. Ensure reporting system will include this
information.
b.     Upgrade existing facilities (17 RTIs in the country) and fill staff positions.
C.     Establish five new RTIs with an additional 185 posts.
d.     Train and evaluate all cadres including trainers; carry out curriculum and teaching
reforms; develop new training programs; cooperate with health department training
schools; evaluate the programs periodically; and produce AV materials.
e.     Institute a regular planning and review process involving district and provincial
managers of the PWDs and RTIs, DOH, health department training schools and other
agencies.
13.    Non-Clinical Training component. The objectives of this component are two-fold. The first
is to improve MPW & PWD's operational performance by focusing on institution building and
management/staff development. To achieve this goal, the component will expand management and
staff training. The second objective is to actively involve the nation's leadership at all levels in
achieving the objectives of the population program. Specifically, the aim is to increase national,
provincial, and community leaders' knowledge of population dynamics, its impact on development,
and family planning issues. It will achieve this through an expansion of existing seminars and
training programs.
14.    Main activities
a.     During the five year plan period, provide training to 6,700 program personnel
(management and operational staff), 41,700 employees of other nation building
departments (health, social welfare, education, labour, local government, etc.), and
46,700 community based groups (opinion leaders, elected representatives, NGO
volunteers, RMPs, hakeems, homeopaths, etc.).



104
Annex 6
Page 11 of 16
b.     Redesign training programs to be more job-specific, and to emphasize problem
solving.
15.   Social Marketing comnponent. The objective is to promote and expand the availability and use
of contraceptives and family planning services through the private sector in urban and peri-urban
areas. The component will first aim to increase the sale of condoms and, then, to expand the range
of contraceptive methods to be privately marketed (such as oral pills, injectable contraceptives, and
IUDs). This component will be implemented by commercial companies and non-governmental
organizations.
16.   Main activities
a.     Provide support for promotional activities (such as mass media) and packaging.
b.     Train wholesalers and retailers in how to market contraceptives.
c.     Train private medical and paramedical practitioners in the delivery of family planning
methods.
d.     Contract a distributor for condoms. The distributor will sell approximately 45 million
units of condoms in the first year at the agreed retail sale price. The quantity will be
raised in the subsequent years, totalling to 275 million units over the five year period.
e.     Provide commercial companies with contraceptives other than condoms at low
promotional prices for an agreed period of time (with donor assistance).
17.    Contraceptive Requirement and Distribution component. The objective is to provide a reliable
supply of contraceptives to service delivery points. This is directed not only at the MPW and DPW
outlets but also specified categories of health centres, NGOs, physicians, and other health care
providers. The component will increase the variety of contraceptives on hand at delivery points,
reduce stock-outs, and control wastage and diversion within the system. The Directorate of
Monitoring and Supplies (Islamabad), the Central Warehouse (Karachi), and the Provincial and
District Officers located in their respective areas will manage this component.
18.   The following table presents projected procurement of key contraceptives for each year of the
Plan period. These estimates - agreed between the Government and donors - were based on
projections made during the appraisal mission, and are a downward revision of the earlier PC-1
estimate, which were unrealistically high. These estimates include procurement for SMC.

19.    Maini Activities
a.     Provide supplies to service delivery points through procurement, storage, and
distribution, supported by a contraceptives monitoring system.
b.     In order to improve utilization, expand services, and ensure smooth supply of
contraceptives:
3      pack condomiis in small and attractive packets so that they could be easily
distributed;
3      regularly test the quality of condoms at the National Research Institute of
Fertility Control;
3      expand the contraceptive delivery network to new service outlets (such as
healtlh outlets, VFPWs and RMPs);
e      ensure timely and prompt supply of contraceptives by a computerized system
of inventory control, stock, and supply;
*      experimenit with innovative approaches to ensure uninterrupted flow of
contraceptives;
*      improve storage facilities in the Central Warehouse and in the districts; and
*      evolve an efficient monitoring and evaluation system.
c.     Regulate the process of inventory control, distribution and storage through various
monitoring instrumiienits and pre-coded forms. Contraceptive logistics are defined in a
manual to be used at all levels.
20.    Researchl, Moniitorinig, and! Evaluation componienit. The objective is to increase effectiveness
and efficiency of service delivery through evaluation and research which will provide information
relevant to priority managemenit concerns and guide management actions. The activities for this
component include: streamlining data collection, intensification of field observations, more operational
research, and improved links betweenl informationi produced by the system and the needs of managers.



106
Annex 6
Page 13 of 16
21.    Main activities
a.     Improve and computerize the management information system (MIS). Simplify
procedure for collection, storage, and retrieval of information. The information to be
monitored and analyzed would include: contraceptive inventories, service delivery
infrastructure, performance of the service delivery network, staff and positions at
various levels, the transport and logistics system, client profiles, continuity in the use
of contraceptives, following-up of clients, validation of performance, and field
monitoring.
b.     Undertake special surveys and impact studies as part of program monitoring.
c.     Provide support for the National Research Institute of Fertility Control (NRIFC), the
National Research Institute of Reproductive Physiology (NRIRP), and the National
Institute of Population Studies (NIPS), to complete ongoing research projects and
carry out new studies. NRIFC is responsible for carrying out research on the
acceptance, effectiveness and side-effects of various types of contraceptives. NRIRP
conducts fertility-related biomedical research. NIPS is a nonclinical research
institution which conducts substantive and methodological research in population and
development.
d.     Support teaching and research activities of the Population Studies Centres (PSC) at
University of Agriculture (Faisalabad) and University of Karachi.
22.    Federal Five Year Population Welfare Program in the Islamabad Capital Territory. The
component will provide family planning advice and services, covering a full range of family planning
methods. Services to eligible couples will be provided using all channels of delivery such as FWCs
and MSUs of the MPW, health outlets of other agencies (including DOH especially as part of MCH
services), DOH hospitals for reproductive health services, and community-based services by using
TBAs and private sector practitioners (such as RMPs, hakeems, and homeopaths).
23.    Main activities
a.    Improve quality and access of FP services through ongoing 14 FWCs and MSUs, and
add three more centres.
b.     Increase DOH outlets from 43 to 54, RHSC(A) from 2 to 3, and RHSC(B) on
demand/need basis.
c.     Deploy 120 TBAs and link them with FWCs.
d.     Involve 200 clinics of RMPs/hakeems/homeopaths.
e.     Continue the education of all service delivery staff in methods of FP services and
advice.



107
Annex 6
Page 14 of 16
f.     Strengthen the existing contraceptive supply and reporting system to link effectively
the existing and proposed service delivery network.
g.     Establish a monitoring and evaluation system to measure continuously performance
through agreed indicators with concurrent corrective measures.
h.     Support service delivery through IEC using interpersonal communication, AV vans,
and printed materials.
24.   Federal Five Year Population Welfare Program in Azad Jammu and Kashmir. The component
will provide family planning advice and services covering a full range of family planning methods.
Services to eligible couples will be provided using all channels of delivery such as DOH outlets as
part of MCH services, DOH hospitals for reproductive health services, community-based services by
using VFPWs, FWCs, and private sector practitioners like hakeems and homeopaths.
25.   Main activities
a.    Improve quality and access of FP services through ongoing six FWCs.
b.    Increase DOH outlets from 90 to 120, RHSC(A) from 1 to 2,.
c.     Deploy 47 VFPWs in rural villages of Muzaffarabad district. Train them prior to
service and follow up with refresher courses.
d.    Involve 300 clinics of hakeems and homeopaths.
e.     Provide in-service training in family planning methods and advice to 10 gynecologists,
30 WMOs, 100 LHVs, 250 paramedics, and 500 hakeems and homeopaths.
f.     Strengthen the population cell of AJK Health Directorate by additional manpower and
transport to improve ongoing supervision, and monitoring and evaluation.
g.     Strengthen the existing contraceptive supply and reporting system to link effectively
the existing and proposed service delivery network.
h.     Establish a monitoring and evaluation system to measure continuously performance
through agreed indicators with concurrent corrective measures.
i.    Support service delivery through IEC using interpersonal communication, AV vans,
and printed materials.
26.   Federal Five Year Population Welfare Programn in the Northern Areas. The component will
provide family planning advice and services covering a full range of family planning methods.
Services to eligible couples will be provided using all possible channels of delivery such as DOH
outlets as part of MCH services, DOH hospitals for reproductive health services, FWCs, and the non-
profit private sector especially the Agha Khan Health Services of Pakistan (AKHSP).

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Letter of Development Policy
The letter of development policy for the population sector is attached.



Annex 7
Government of Pakistan                     Page 2 of 12
Ministry of Population Welfare
Islamabad, the 22nd November, 1994.
From:
Muzaffar Mahmood Qureshi,
Secretary,
To,
Mr. D. Joseph Wood
Vice President
South Asia Region
The World Bank
Washington DC 20433
U.S.A.
Dear Mr. Vice President,
LETTER OF POPULATION POLICY
I am writing to you in connection with the proposed Population Welfare Programme
Project, and to inform you about policy decisions and measures that the Government has already
taken, or is about to take, to attain Pakistan's population welfare objectives.The support of the
Government of Pakistan to an integrated policy of Population and Development has been openly
declared at various national and international forums. Social Sector development is especially
being emphasized in the Social Action Programme. Population Welfare is an integral part of
SAP.
2.    As you are aware, the Ministry of Population Welfare is taking the lead in implementing
the Population Welfare Programme under the Eighth Five-Year Plan (1993-98). The objective
is to reduce the currently high level of unmet need for family planning access, particularly in



112                                       Annex 7
Page 3 of 12
rural areas, by strengthening demand for family planning. Achievement of this objective will
simultaneously contribute to individual reproductive health and welfare, and slow Pakistan's
high rate of population growth.Reducing unmet need by only half its current level would raise
contraceptive prevalence from 14 to 24 percent. The first two years of this programme are being
partly financed by the Social Action Programme (SAP), supported by the Government of
Pakistan with assistance from multilateral and other donors, and the last three under the
proposed Population Welfare Programme Project (PWPP) which has been appraised by the Bank
Missions. The SAP aims to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the public sector delivery
system for basic services through more realistic planning, a series of policy reform measures,
improvements in implementation, and restructured expenditure programs that emphasize
improvements in the quality of services. The PWPP will build on the improvements in the area
of population welfare by improving access and outreach, testing cost-effective approaches to the
integration of family planning with other MCH services, increasing demand for family planning,
and strengthening management.
THE POPULATION SITUATION IN PAKISTAN
3.    The Government of Pakistan is aware that Pakistan's population growth rate of 2.9% is
unacceptably high and must be reduced. It poses a major obstacle to the country's attempts to
reduce poverty, improve social indicators, and become a dynamic, middle-income economy. The
economy has to grow at 3 percent per annum, just to maintain the same level of per capita
income. If current fertility rates prevail, the population is expected to double in the next 24
years. Recognizing the urgency of the situation, the Government is committed to addressing the
population issue as a matter of national development priority.
2



113
Annex 7
Page 4 of 12
4.    During the current Five year Population Welfare Plan, 1993-98, the Government intends
to sustain and increase its high level of demonstrated political commitment to the programme,
embark on a stronger and more explicit information, education, and communication (IEC)
campaign, expand the number of outlets offering family planning services, improve the quality
of services, and overcome the physical and social distance that constrains the delivery of family
planning services through outreach programmes. The latter is particularly important and is the
rationale for our new initiative on village-based family planning workers.
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAMME 1993-98
5.    The Population Welfare Programme approved on January 25, 1994 is an integral
component of the country's socio-economic development programme, with the specific goal of
promoting the small family norm and providing the means to achieve this objective. The primary
demographic objectives of the programme in the Eighth Five-Year Plan are:
a.    To raise the level of current contraceptive practice from 14% in 1992-93 to 24.4% by
1997-98.
b.    To prevent 4.661 million births during the Eighth Five year Plan Period.
C.    To reduce the crude birth rate from 39.00 per thousand population in 1992-93 to 35.00
per thousand population by 1997-98.
d.    To bring down the population growth rate from 2.9 percent per annum in 1992-93 to
2.6% by 1997-98. This would also contribute to national health objectives by reducing maternal
and infant mortality.
6.    The accomplishment of these ambitious programme objectives will be achieved through
key programme strategies. These include providing an institutional set-up at the national level



114
Annex 7
Page 5 of 12
to facilitate the tasks of policy planning, coordination, financial management, and monitoring
and evaluation; assuring an adequate supply of contraceptives; supporting NGOs and private
sector initiatives such as social marketing of contraceptives; imparting clinical and non-clinical
training; designing and implementing an effective IEC strategy; conducting specialized research;
and implementing the population programme in the provinces and federal areas.
NEW INITIATIVES UNDER THE SAP
7.    The Government is also seeking to resolve long-standing problems in the delivery of
social services. Under the SAPP, the Government of Pakistan intends to improve the design and
implementation of social services programme, increase the level of effort, and build political will
to address the long-standing neglect of the social sectors. Under the SAP Project with respect
to the population programme, the Federal and Provincial Governments have committed
themselves to undertaking institutional reforms in support of the programme, namely: (a) making
adequate financial allocations and releasing them on schedule; (b) establishing and strengthening
strategic and operational planning mechanisms; (c) increasing the participation of Provincial
Governments through enhancing responsibilities, decentralizing more and more functions within
the province, and strengthening coordination with Provincial line departments; (d) developing
a human resources plan for population welfare staff; (e) increasing private sector involvement,
including that of NGOs; and (f) ensuring an adequate supply of contraceptives. Further reforms
and new programme initiatives will be undertaken in the next year of the SAPP and strengthened
by the PWPP. Main features of the policy action plan for both the immediate future and the
longer term are summarized below:-



115
Management                                                                       Annex 7
Page 6 of 12
(i)   Financial Resources
8.    An increased budgetary allocation of Rs.9. 1 billion has been agreed for Eighth Five Year
Plan Period. The PC-Is for the entire five-year Population Welfare Programme were approved
on January 25, 1994. For the year 1994-95, ADP allocation has been revised from Rs.996.160
to Rs. 1200.It is anticipated to be further raised to Rs. 1270 million in the budget revision in
March 1995.
9.    In order to provide for increases in operational costs with better service delivery, the
budget is planned to increase gradually over the next 5 years.
(ii)   Decentralization
10.   The scale of resources to be mobilized under the Population Welfare Programme, and
the expansion of services at the district and village levels, require the improvement of provincial
management in the planning and implementation of programme activities. The Ministry of
Population Welfare and the Provincial Governments have taken major steps to increase
Provincial Governments' involvement in Population Welfare Programme. As an early start,
specific measures were approved by the Federal Cabinet in October 1993. These are: (a)
including the Provincial Population Programme in the respective province's annual development
programme, but keeping the programme totally federally funded, combined with releasing funds
through the Provincial Accountant's General Departments; (b) including family planning in the
rules of business of the Provincial health departments; (c) where necessary, and especially for
female medical officers, recruiting staff on contract; and (d) including the population programme
in the regular reviews of all Provincial development programmes.



116                                           Annex 7
Page 7 of 12
11.         Following the decision taken in the first meeting of the Social Sector Committee
of the Cabinet (SSCC) held on September 22, 1994, Population Welfare Programme review is
included in the regular charter of duties of the Deputy Commissioners and Assistant
Commissioners. At the Provincial level it is to be reviewed by Chief Secretaries.
12.         In the Provinces, Population Welfare is included as part of the Provincial PSDP
for fiscal year 1994-95. Funds for the first and second quarter have been released within the
initial month of each quarter. Other measures to facilitate Population Welfare Programme
implementation are underway. The SSCC has approved release of funds for Population Welfare
to provinces on six monthly basis.
13.   Efforts to decentralize the administration of field programmes are being supported
through the establishment of the divisional and tahsil management tiers and a move towards an
area-based approach to service delivery, with the district as the most important management unit
is in process. Under the proposed set-up, the District Population Welfare Officer will be
responsible for coordinating the family planning service delivery activities of a variety of outlets,
whether from the Provincial Departments, the Department of Health, or private practitioners and
NGOs.
(iii)   Integration of Health and Family Planning
14.    Steps have been taken to ensure that family planning needs are addressed in conjunction
with maternal and child care objectives. The Government is encouraging increased participation
of the health sector in providing family planning services, as reinforced by the Cabinet decision
and by launching of a special Programme of community health workers for provision of primary



117                                        Annex 7
Page 8 of 12
health care and family planning. Ministry of Population Welfare and Departments of Population
Welfare are providing the Ministry and Departments of Health with training programmes on
family planning, IEC material, and supplies of contraceptives. Provincial Departments of Health
will ensure that family planning services are provided at health outlets. The pre-service
curriculum for health workers is being expanded to include family planning. Training of health
workers to provide family planning services has been undertaken in the past year, and recently,
District Technical Committees have involved both health and population welfare officers in the
management of family planning service delivery in the field. Building on these initiatives, the
Government intends to move toward effective integrated provision of maternal and child health
and family planning services.
(iv)   Human Resources Development
15.   The delivery of an improved package of services to more clients will require careful
planning of the human resource requirement of the Population Welfare Program. To improve
the management of field operations, tahsil and divisional levels have already been introduced
and are being made effective. The ban on recruitment of personnel has been lifted for Population
Welfare Programme employees and positions at various levels are being filled.
The recruitment of female staff as front-line workers in the Government's new major initiative
for Village-Based Family Planning Workers to expand service delivery in rural areas is stressed.
For long-range planning of strategic goals and human resource needs the Resident Mission
World Bank has been requested to collaborate with Ministry of Population Welfare in preparing
a human resource development plan to take into account staff development and succession to
managerial positions.



118
Annex 7
Page 9 of 12
FURTHER PWPP INITIATIVES
Service Support
(i)    Expanding Access and Outreach
16.   To expand access and outreach in the rural areas, the programme has initiated the
deployment of Village Based Family Planning Workers, and the use of Mobile Service Units
(MSUs). The Village-Based Family Planning Worker (VBFPW) initiative involves the
recruitment of educated, married women from their respective villages to provide services
directly to the female clients. This is the major new initiative of the PWP which is expected to
make a significant difference in its outreach capacity. It will also serve as a catalyst to mobilize
communities in the design and provision of family planning services. In the urban areas, Family
Welfare Center (FWCs) are in process of being located to better locations especially to meet the
needs in low-income, thickly-populated urban areas. The redefinition of FWC staffing will also
field male and female motivator to work in the communities to build community support as well
as improve the referral system for clinical contraception. Through this initiative, the programme
intends to expand coverage from 5% to 70% in rural areas, and from 54% to 100% in urban
areas.
(ii)   Improved Quality
17.   Effective technical supervision and counseling of clients on all family planning methods
available, how they work, and about their contraindications and potential side-effects is
emphasized. Attention is also being given to cleanliness and privacy in family welfare facilities,
and to having them open at convenient hours for clients. FWCs and VFPWs are also seeking



119
Annex 7
Page 10 of 12
to provide clients with an appropriate array of basic health information and services, which
contribute to positive client-provider relationships. Capacity to supervise and provide in-service
training for FWC staff and VFPWs will be strengthened by employing lady medical officers in
MSUs.
iii) Contraceptive Supplies
18.   An adequate range and supply of contraceptives at all service delivery outlets is essential
to any population programme. The programme has started addressing the problem of inadequate
supplies, stockouts, and limited method availability at family planning outlets by improving the
procurement and distribution of contraceptives. A thorough review of the contraceptives
distribution system, and experimental or interim solutions, such as provincial buffer stocks, are
in hand. In the last fiscal year, proposals to implement provincial and district buffer stocks have
been implemented: the four provincial headquarters have been provided two months stock of
contraceptives to meet emergency shortages in any district. These supplies are replenished as and
when requested by a province.
Programme Support
(i)   Information, Education, and Communications
19.   Community support for family planning is as important as individual couples awareness
of family planning methods. In this regard, the population Welfare Program is expanding its
mix of IEC activities beyond mass media events, Interpersonal communication with clients and
community outreach service are emphasized. Special efforts directed to eliciting community
support, particularly of influential, men, and religious leaders.



120
Annex 7
Page 11 of 12
20.   It may be mentioned in this context, that celebration of the World Population Day in July
this year were extended to ten days programmes for creating awareness of population issues
from the grass root level to national level with joint effort of Ministry of Population Welfare,
other federal ministries, especially, Ministry of Information and Broadcasting, Provincial
Population Welfare Departments, NGOs and the Private Sector.
(ii)   Improved Monitoring and Evaluation
21.   An improved monitoring and evaluation system to regularly provide information on
program  inputs and performance is being introduced. It links information on  financial
utilization, infra structure, staff position, and contraceptive performance. Initiated at the
provincial level from October 1994, it will gradually go down to District and service outlet
levels. Information on acceptors, by method, continuation and dropout rates will be obtained
from the Clinical Record Cards filled at the FWCs. This system will be revived in a phased
manner. Information from the Contraceptive Prevalence Survey, currently in process, will
provide the CPR to supplement the service statistics. Future sample surveys, operations research
studies and program evaluations will provide feedback on the impact of the program, while
identifying possible measures to improve service delivery.
Private Sector Involvement
22.   The Government of Pakistan realizes that the private sector is a valuable partner in the
delivery of family planning services. In order to assist non-governmental organizations in the
population sector, the NGO Coordinating Council has been restructured and reorganized as the
National Trust for Population Welfare. It will have a small and autonomous Board, an elected
Chairperson, and non-voting government representation. GOP is providing Rs. 100 million as



121
Annex 7
Page 12 of 12
an initial endowment. Efforts are also being taken to expand the distribution of contraceptives
through private channels, and to enlist the participation of private practitioners-medical doctors,
as well traditional practitioners in family planning through the social marketing of
contraceptives.
23.   I would like to reiterate the Government of Pakistan's firm commitment to implementing
an effective population welfare programme towards the interest of individual families' welfare
anu the country's long-term development objectives. I sincerely hope,that with the partnership
of the World Bank and other donors, we will be able to achieve these worthwhile objectives.
Yours sincerely
(Muzaffar Mahmood Qureshi)

i.    to review progress in implementation of recommendations fmm 1992 (AVSC)
assessment, and finalize action plan for year 2.
ii.   to initiate investigation of problems of recruitment and retention of lady doctors.
c.   Give Director Technical in PPWD more responsibility for operational budget and
management of RHS centers.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Implementation Plans:
An Approach and Examples
The Project
1.    Achievement of objectives as guidance in implementation.  In this project the Ministry of
Population Welfare (MPW), the provinces, and the federal areas would each prepare their own detailed
implementation plans in the context of the project's objectives. (See Chapter II for Program and Project
Objectives). In addition to the program's demographic and service objectives, there are a number of
organizational and cross-provincial program objectives which must be also implemented during the life
of the project. These include: (i) decentralization; (ii) finance and budget system reform; (iii) improving
involvement and commitment of other departments and TGls; (iv) strengthening coordinating bodies to
accommnodate better the population program; (v) expanding political commitment; (vi) managing an area-
based approach to service delivery; and (vii) increasing use of satisfied continuing users.
2.     During implementation top managers would manage policy, strategy, and achievement of broad
objectives, while middle and lower level managers would focus on achieving operational objectives within
the agreed policy/strategy framework. Management of implementation will, therefore, not only be
focused on objectives, but also "objectives divided" and delegated.
3.     Project design and implications for implementation. The project supports a nationwide effort,
which is sub-divided and modified to geographic realities. It will be implemented by operational
managers at the provincial level and below, with requisite administrative and financial authority, who
would adjust implementation to meet local needs (see Chapter 11, Sections B and C for Summary
Description and Detailed Program and Project Description). Each operational manager will also need
to adapt their program activities to the political/public opinions and reactions in their service area.
4.     Projectfinancing and financing's influence on implementation. As this program is essentially
public sector financed, the flows of funds are bureaucratically constrained. Several organizations, and
different levels within each, have decision authority over expenditures (see Chapter III and Annex LB for
details). Effective implementation depends on timely and appropriate releases. In the past, when this
program was totally Federally financed and controlled, delayed and erratic flows of funds seriously
impeded implementation. The procedures are now being altered to decentralize operational authority
within the system. The smooth Federal-Provincial flows of funds will facilitate implementation by
ensuring adequate allocations to line items and improving the timing of releases. One key to changing
financial implementation will be the levels of real control which district population officers have.
Implementation Arrangements
5.     Organizational responsibilities. The Ministry of Population Welfare has responsibility for the



136
Annex 9
Page 2 of 15
national population program, and therefore the implementation and management of the Population Welfare
Program Project. Overall responsibility rests with the Federal Secretary of Population Welfare. The
population program is multi-sectoral, and as such is implemented through a range of government
departments and private sector agencies. The roles of major organizations are as follows:
* Federal MPW:
(i)    oversees the provision of services through provincial Population Welfare Departments
(PWDs);
(ii)    provides services in federally administered areas;
(iii)   implements national IEC activities;
(iv)   responsible for key management support functions such as contraceptive supplies,
training, monitoring and evaluation, and program strategy and planning;
(v)    supports intermediary bodies to manage funds for NGO projects, to generate NGO
support for population, and provide technical and management support to member
organizations;
(vi)   provides policy and strategic direction to social marketing projects implemented through
commercial channels; and takes the lead in inter-sectoral coordination.
* PWDs:
(i)    are responsible for the day-to-day implementation of the program in the province;
(ii)   facilitate and support the population related activities of other public sector organizations,
such as the provincial Departments of Health (DOH) and Ministry of Education;
(iii)   provide contraceptives and training to the DOHs to support the integration of family
planning into the health services; and
(v)    provide training and supplies to traditional birth attendants (TBAs), private doctors,
homeopaths and hakeems to promote and supply contraceptives in their private work.
* The Ministry of Education:
(i)    develops population education curricula; and
(ii)   supports teacher training related to teaching population education in schools.
Implementation and Agreements
6.     Annual monitoring and planning. Annual planning will be carried out by a combination of inter-
locking planning methods.  The multi-round discussions which agree on policy reform matrices will
continue for top level planning. The operational implementation planning methodology will be further
refined and finalized during the March/April 1995 Project Launch Workshop(s), and then continued with
modifications as necessary. Overall, it is expected that planning will conform to a process and format
which is amenable to automation (application of project management software) for easier
tracking/monitoring and up-dating. At the higher levels of management (provincial and federal), it is
expected that implementation will be planned and monitored with project management software. At
district level and below these functions will be mostly manual, but done in a common format that can be



137
Annex 9
Page 3 of 15
aggregated into the computer based formats and data fields.
7.    Participation. While ultimate responsibilities and authorities are very important, participation
in decision making in this type of program is equally valuable. The following are suggested participants
in the major national/provincial program implementation decision making groups.
a.    Intra-Government Organizations - MPW; Population Welfare Departments; Ministry of
Finance; Planning Commission; Establishment Division; Ministry of Health; Departments
of Health; Provincial Planning & Development Departments; Provincial Finance
Departments; Ministry of Education; Departments of Education; medical faculty
representatives.
b.     Other National Organizations - NGOs; private sector organizations involved in social
marketing; Pakistan Medical Association; etc.
C.    International Organizations - IDA; ADB; ODA; the Netherlands; The Population
Council; CIDA; USAID (if still represented); EU; KfW; UNFPA.
Additionally, the successful series of provincial and district program workshops which bring together
working level representatives of those involved with the program, across all levels, will continue (see
Chapter II, para. xx).
8.    Implementation operations. In order to facilitate implementation, managers require well designed
aids to help them analyze, make implementation decisions, and monitor/follow-up. This will include
providing each manager with a complete "Implementation Volume." This volume would be a set of
documents, manuals, and other management tools which would be used as a detailed guide for day-to-day
implementation of the project. it would include: Federal and Provincial PC-ls; Broad Program Issues
and Policy Actions; Policy Matrices 1995/96; Annual Work Plans; Component Summaries of Federal and
Provincial Components; detailed cost tables; physical and financial indicators; procurement plans; training
plans; TA requirements; IDA procurement procedures; other donor plans; the IDA Staff Appraisal Report
of the PWPP; and the Aide Memoire of the IDA and cooperating donor Appraisal Mission.
9.    Implementation begins with the "translation" of resources into services and then those services
into changes in fertility and reproductive health related behavior. The program's resources include
people, information, supplies, facilities, transport, and money. An operational manager can control the
provision of many of these, but some for example due to procurement economlies of scale, s/he cannot.
Therefore, coordinated implementation plans are necessary if im)plemiientation is to proceed on schedule.
With such a large complex program, the only feasible way to improve coordination is through integrated
project management workplanning, coupled with good communication systems for resolving scheduling
and other implementation problems.
Implementation Plan
10.   Plan for entire project period. The key components of the implementation plan are:
a. Policy & Strategic plan (see Chapter II, Section A)



138
Annex 9
Page 4 of 15
b. Financial Plan (see Annex IB. and "Phasing" Annex)
c. Technical Assistance Plan (see Annex 11)
11.   Indicative plans. Due to the scope and national nature of this program a number of inter-related
operational plans are required. The PC-Is, agreed ADPs, and policy matrices have provided a solid basis
for the preparation and up-dating of these plans, but they need to be desegregated so that it is easier for
each manager to understand what he/she is to implement. The task of coordinating these different plans
will not be simple, but current versions of project management software make it technically feasible and
relatively easy if managers regularly keep each other informed of progress. The first such detailed annual
plans for the project will be finalized during the Project Launch Workshop (March/April 1995), but much
of the conceptual framework and relationship building has already been accomplished during project
preparation. In the meantime, draft plans have been prepared which indicate the structure and the
relationships between one type of plan and another, as follows.
a. Overall Summary Plan of entire project (see example attached)
b. Geographic Region (see example of Provincial Annual Plan attached)
c. Components (see example of Federal Component Plan attached)
12.    Contingency planning for optimal implementation. Implementation managers at all levels need
to maintain consciousness of the sequencing of supplying resources and integrating them to "translate"
them into quality services. The discipline of project management workplanning underlines the critical
sequence of relationships so that managers can focus on the critical paths to providing good services.
13.    Criticalfactors in program objective achievement. The overall plan example indicates that the
project is supporting a variety of social services most of which began before the project and which will
continue after the project completes. This project will expand and improve many if not all of them, but
this requires many separate and yet concurrent efforts. This makes overall implementation difficult as
most tasks are not lock-step or operationally interrelated (as tasks tend to be in, e.g. construction). The
ideal implementation situation is for limited public/client adoption (or the demand for services) to be the
only inhibiting factor to achievement of the objectives. Program managers need to know how to move
toward that goal.  In addition to the above sequencing and critical path analysis, managers would
periodically (probably only at the annual planning sessions) examine which factors drive implementation
and which retard it. This would help reduce the effort put into actions which have limited or no impact.
14.    Monitoring & evaluation. For monitoring service performance, as well as public health and
fertility outcomes, indicators are the starting point for tracking project activities and impact. As given
in Annex 10, indicators have been defined for different types of objectives and their requisite decisions.
Indicators are also contained in the Components Summary of the Program for MPW and PWDs (see
Annex 6). Top managers should monitor policy and strategic indicators, whereas middle managers should
monitor operational indicators. Managers must respond to indicators which do not change or change in
a way which was not planned. The difficult part of the decision process is assessing what to do when
an indicator is significantly different than planned. The choices are:



139
Annex 9
Page 5 of 15
(i) do nothing - the indicator has not established a trend yet which demands correction;
(ii) ignore - the indicator does not measure a significant aspect of the program and all others
indicate positive progress;
(iii) take minor corrective action - usually by adjusting the quantity or quality of the existing
program of activities;
(iv) take major corrective action - add resources, increase quantity or quality of activities,
change the nature of activities, change the location, or some combination of these; and (v) drop
the activity or component of the program.
