PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Progress Indicators
Rationale for Program Indicators
1.     There is a need for a consistent set of indicators at the national, provincial, district and service
delivery levels to measure progress of the Population Welfare Program. These indicators should
correspond to the objectives and management processes of the Program. The timely measurement of this
set of agreed indicators will be important for MPW  and the donor organizations' program planners,
managers, and evaluators in order to define the overall and specific program status for any internal (GOP)
or external (donor organization) manager at any point in time.
2.     Traditional project approaches  typically  focus  on  indicators  for major  inputs  and
activities/processes. Due to the adoption of the program approach in this project, the indicators will not
only include inputs and activities, but will encompass policy, strategy, and broad component categories.
They will focus on (but not be completely limited to) outputs, outcomes, and impacts. The entire
pyramid of agreed indicators will have to be vertically and horizontally integrated and consistent, and
must be linked to the hierarchy of planning and program decision making (Figure 3, Annex 5).
3.     The primary objective of these indicators is to aid managers in monitoring progress and
achievements under the SAPP and the proposed PWPP, at regular intervals over the financial year.
Another objective is to ensure that managers are aware of both performance and operational problems.
For these indicators to be useful to managers, they must be valid, quantifiable, realistic, timely, and
flexible.
4.     These indicators have been defined using the concepts and the indicator ''menus' provided in the
draft guidelines proposed by UNFPA through a Program Advisory Notes (PAN) entitled "Managemenlt
Information Systems in Support of Family Planning and Maternal and Child Health Programs."  The
additions, modifications to PAN-suggested indicators, and deletions from the PAN's menu were made
on the basis of agreements made during the preparation and appraisal missions. An initial menu of
indicators, with benchmark values, is presented below (para. 9), while the full menu proposed by UNFPA
is included as an attachment to this Annex.
Types of Indicators
5.     Core decisions and indicators. Program implementation involves a wide variety of decisions
which in turn require information to support those decisions. The relevant information is generated
through data collection and analysis. While a program consists of many components, a central "core"
of decisions and indicators summarizes key points of program status and performance. These could
include:



151
Annex 10
Page 2 of 20
Decision Element                             Indicator(s)
a.     Reduce population growth              Change in population size; population growth
rate
b.     Reduce birth rate                     Change in CBR
c.     Increase contraceptive practice       Changes in CPR
d.     Increase contraceptive availability   Changes in numbers & distributions of outlets
(may include access measures also - changes in
average time & distances to services); changes in
performance per outlet; reduction in stock-outs;
e.     Increase contraceptive demand         Changes in the quality of services/care like
increases in follow-up visits leading to changes
in continuous users; Changes in IEC level of
effort; Changes in IEC impact.
6.     Programmatic and organizational indicators. Programmatic indicators relate to the policies,
strategies, and broad components of service delivery. In this case most of the clientele consists of
individuals or couples of reproductive age.  Organizational indicators refer to the policies, strategies,
and broad components which are intended to improve the internal functions and operations of the
organization so that it can more effectively deliver its program services. The latter relate to those types
of efforts known as institution building (or organizational development/management development) which
many international donor organizations currently have made a high policy priority in their assistance.
7.     Input, output, and process indicators. Every policy, strategy, broad component, output, activity
and input will have associated indicators of progress (process measures), final achievement (product or
impact measures), or both. The indicator, when measured, should be able to provide the "yes or no"
answer(s) to the questions:
*      are the requisite resources in place to implement the program's activities/services
(inputs)
*      are the activities/services being carried out/delivered (process)
*      are the results of individual activities or components as desired (output);
*      is the program overall and each component where we planned to be at this point (timing
& efficiency);
*      is the program achieving its objectives (outcome);
*      are the program's objectives the correct objectives and do they make any long-term
difference (impact)?



152
Annex 10
Page 3 of 20
If the answers to all of these is "yes," then implementation for the next year can proceed with no or little
modification to the plan. If not, then corrective actions and adjustments must be made (see Management
Annex and Cost-effectiveness Annex).
8.     In population programs the first four (a.b.c.& d.) are considered program-based performance
types of indicators, whereas the last two (e. & f.) are considered population-based effect type indicators.
Much of the data for the program-based indicators can be collected and analyzed as part of the MPW
information system. Many of the population-based indicators can be derived from data collected by the
Pakistan Integrated Household Survey (PIHS) as well as other sample surveys.
Program-Based          Input                   Process                 Timing      Output
Performance
Population-Based       Outcome                 Impact
Effects
These six categories of indicators must provide feedback to the iterative planning process. The continuing
process of policy dialogue and program adjustments is part of the normal process of good management
for a program of this size and duration.
Indicators in the short run
9.     The following menu of proposed indicators was derived through consultation with the Monitoring
and Statistics Wing of the Ministry of Population Welfare, the National Institute of Population Studies,
and the Population Council. It represents a minimum set of consistent indicators which can be generated
using the existing service statistics system and ongoing sample surveys and operations research activities.
Since managerial needs will change as the program evolves, the indicators menu will change in order to
serve managers' changing information needs. The following table also includes benchmark values for
selected indicators collected during the appraisal mission.

The Service Statistics System in the Long Run
10.    While a minimal set of indicators has been agreed upon for monitoring the program's progress
in the short or medium term, a key objective of the service statistics system is to provide useful and
timely information to managers. Efforts must be taken to increase the utility and timeliness of data
through streamlining the existing system of service statistics. Too much information is being collected
which is not being used. The number of forms to be filled by personnel at the service delivery points,
at the district level, and finally at the provincial level are too cumbersome and can be presented in a
format which more easily compiles the same information. The data should also be available in formats
which allow totals and the distribution across items to be readily interpreted (e.g. through bar graphs,
pie charts, and other graphics). Pointers will be sought from the MIS system experiment in the NWFP
and from the forms being used for monitoring by some NGO's. Technical assistance will be provided in
this area to simplify the system by mid-1995.
11.    Since the monitoring function should mirror the dynamic nature of the other two main managerial
functions (planning and operations/production), it should evolve to help managers define and solve
problems. One suggestion, which was accepted by the Ministry on a pilot basis, was to add an extra
form with a list of additional questions to be administered once every six months at the various service
delivery points, or when a particular problem emerges, to be continued until adequate information is
obtained. The one page form will be simple but can address special problems which tehsil or district
managers and their staff identify (stock-outs, drop-outs, etc.) in each province. The form can further
define and document the exact nature and magnitude of the problem, and can be attached to the regular
forms for a sub-sample of districts or service delivery points. A separate short form may be needed to
monitor the Village Family Planning Worker program.
12.     Another suggestion which was accepted is to disaggregate data from urban and rural service
delivery points to enable the evaluation of performance of these two sectors differentially. This will
require a minor change in the forms in use.
13.    Equally important is the need to have monitoring systems that are responsive to changes in
strategy and emphasis and are useful tools for managers. Ultimately, the indicators that evolve should be
used by managers at different levels to respond to any bottlenecks and problems being faced at the level
for which they are responsible. Greater involvement of the field functionaries responsible for collection
of the monitoring information in the process of designing, compilation and utilization of the figures is
essential. In particular, these figures need to be aggregated to assess performance starting at the tehsil
level. At present, lower level functionaires (FWW's and District Population Welfare Officers) view the
collection of information as something quite removed from their own sphere of interest. Not only must
they feel part of the process of monitoring, but they should recognize the utility of the data being
collected for their management duties and decisions. An important first step will be a series of intensive
three day workshops at the district level to orient the relevant officers/workers to the value of monitoring
in the management of their respective activities. They will be organized by the Ministry of Population
Welfare in conjunction with technical assistance from consultants.
14.    Up to now, the system of monitoring statistics has been geared towards the achievement of
targets. Unfortunately, past experience has shown that a target-driven program creates incentives to
distort or misrepresent performance. Performance can be more effectively assured through a changed
orientation which emphasizes accountability at all levels. For example, while the provincial headquarters



158
Annex 10
Page 9 of 20
will be responsible for providing adequate funds and contraceptive supplies promptly and regularly, as
well as handing down broad policy directives, the District Population Officers have to ensure the smooth
delivery of services in their areas.
15.    Ultimately, service providers such as the FWW's, the VFPW's etc, are accountable to the
communities they serve. A greater participatory approach towards monitoring is being developed. It is
important to reinforce mandatory face-to-face meetings between DPWO's and provincial headquarters
staff to review and use monitoring indicators for making and reviewing management decisions, which
may be more effective than a cursory compilation of forms. Six monthly meetings between DPWO's and
provincial staff to review progress will be a good first step. The provision of computers at the district
level and the use of graphs and charts would also simplify the process of recording and analyzing
information rapidly. Such steps have already been taken by the Departments of Health.
16.    Communities themselves have a monitoring function. Village committees and users' committees,
possibly organized around needs which communities have themselves identified, can help plan the
delivery of services, monitor progress, and provide a support system for village workers. More contact
between the lowest level workers and tehsil officers, as is the current practice for the village family
planning workers through bi-monthly meetings, should be encouraged through out the system, although
such efforts should be sensitive to local conditions (e.g. gender segregation). A concerted effort will be
necessary to establish qualitative systems of monitoring client satisfaction at the grassroots level by
involving clients and field-level service providers. To monitor the career development of female field
workers, particularly in an organization is divided along gender lines, the Ministry will need gender-
disaggregated staffing statistics on administrative and technical staff.
Supporting Research, Enquiries, Data Collection
17.    Several different sources of information will be utilized as inputs to the monitoring and evaluation
system. It will draw on the existing system of service statistics and supervisory reports, as well as sample
surveys.  Important sources of information outside the service statistics system  include:  (a) the
forthcoming rounds of the PIHS (Pakistan Integrated Household Survey), to be held annually to monitor
various components of SAP; (b) other planned surveys such as the Contraceptive Prevalence survey
scheduled for the second half of 1994; and (c) ongoing operations research studies which can address
special issues where gaps of information exist.  Issues to be addressed through sample surveys or
operations research include: drop-outs, client satisfaction, quality of care, efficacy of the IEC message,
evaluation of NGO efforts, preference for combined MCH versus family planning activities, shortage of
supplies, etc.. Two recent studies which are particularly useful are the FWC study carried out by the
Population Council and the Vicinity Study carried out by the Monitoring and Evaluation cell of the
Ministry of Population Welfare.
18.    Each district or province will identify its own set of specific problems which it needs to pursue
in greater detail. After such issues are identified, sample surveys can be conducted at the district level
or the service delivery level. Rapid assessment techniques will also be utilized. In fact, the identification
of "special" topics to be investigated ought to be part of the operational plans and can be a way of
ensuring that some attention has been devoted to seeking answers to problems of implementation and
delivery which have occurred in the recent past and to set in motion some corrective action. Small sample
surveys (comprising of 6-9 questions) at the district level will be carried out by an independent group at
six monthly intervals. They could investigate specific topics pertaining to client satisfaction (e.g., the



159
Annex 10
Page 10 of 20
number of contacts with providers, number of methods proposed by provider, side-effects etc.) and would
provide an independent assessment of service delivery points as well as provide feedback on reasons for
non-use or drop outs.  To reduce costs and workload, operations research should be driven by
performance problems, most of which should be defined by the regular performance monitoring system.
Operations research should only be undertaken when there are significant performance problems whose
nature cannot be defined through regular monitoring and managerial analysis.
19.    The important indicator of a reduction in the population growth rate will come from the results
of the forthcoming 1995 Census, presently planned for March with preliminary results expected in April
1995. Some of the major impact indicators of the population program have to be collected at the
national and provincial levels and can be best done through national sample surveys. The Federal Bureau
of Statistics is primarily responsible for the PIHS which will be conducted annually and has been granted
funding under the SAPP program. The CPS has been granted funding by the UNFPA and will take place
in 1994. A National Family and Fertility Survey has also been approved to be carried out in 1996 by
NIPS under ODA funding. A major fertility survey is planned in 1998 to make a full assessment at the
end of the project by collecting full birth histories. For comparability, all the proposed surveys need to
be integrated by utilizing the same sampling frame, although care must be taken not to draw the same
sample unless for a panel survey. They will have the same core of members on their technical advisory
boards to ensure complementarity and comparability to enable the compilation of time series of indicators.
20.    The Population Council continues to carry out useful operations research studies on FWC's, IUD
follow up, etc.. Its role in assisting the Ministry of Population Welfare to strengthen its system of
monitoring and evaluation should continue. Moreover, the Council should also expand its training
function and develop local and national capability in undertaking operations research.
21.    NIPS can be utilized to pursue a research program which will feed into the monitoring and
evaluation of the Program. Their recent studies on cost benefit analysis, on non-users and NGO's are
steps in the right direction, and should be utilized more fully in designing and running the Program.

Attachment
Recommended Indicators following UNFPA Guidelines
Suggested Framework of Initial Indicators
22.    The following matrices are a set of recommended indicators following the UNFPA guidelines on
program indicator definition. The following framework does make two additions: (i) it adds the decision
column so that the indicators are clearly linked to management decisions, and (ii) it codes the type of
indicator from the six types.

Changing Indicators During the Program's Implementation
23.     Flexibility in the monitoring and evaluation system  is desirable.  Indicators should evolve to
reflect the changing needs of the program. While the above set of indicators may be valid at the
beginning of the program, it may need to be modified as some of the program components change.
Planners and managers will inevitably change some of the values of the indicators, but they also may
elect to change the type of indicators used. In order to measure the most relevant indicators for the
program, they should also change along with the priority program components.
24.     No program  can or should be collecting information to measure every possible indicator. If an
aggregative (or macro level) indicator indicates sub-optimal performance, program management will have
to disaggregate and examine the component indicators to identify the critical component. If in so doing
an indicator which had not been used previously is identified as critical, then a special study to gather
additional information may have to be undertaken in the short term.  It may be possible to analyze the
existing service statistics or other management information in a different manner to produce the relevant
information, but those cases will likely be few.  In the long term, particularly for program-based



165
Annex 10
Page 16 of 20
indicators, the needed indicator information may be integrated into and collected/analyzed as part of the
normal information system. Many of the population-based indicators can only be collected through
periodic surveys and census.
25.    In order to assist national and donor organization program managers in the process of indicator
selection and modification the following menu is offered as a guide. This menu could be used during the
annual reviews particularly when there is a significant change in the program or major problems in
program implementation. Decisions about corrective actions may require that program performance
related to these actions be measured with new or additional indicators.
Inputs
I.    Indicators to measure the policy environment
A.    National public policy on population growth; its priority as a national concern; and its
relationship to the development process and other sectors.
B.    Existence of a policy plan for MPW  used by top management as the basis for their
management actions and decisions and as the basis of monitoring
C.    Number and completeness of appropriately disseminated policy analyses
D.    Number of policy discussions or awareness raising events with national, provincial,
and/or local leaders
E.    Existence of a strategic plan for expanding the national population welfare program
F.    Integration of demographic data into development planning of other sectors
G.    Number of statements of leaders in support of population welfare
H.    Number of coordinating mechanisms and the number of practical actions of each between
population welfare, line departments, and other sectors
I.     Level and relative priority of population welfare within government administration
J.     Restrictions on advertising of contraceptives in the mass media
K.    Absence of unwarranted restrictions on providers
L.    Absence on unwarranted restrictions on users
M.    Public sector resources devoted to population welfare as a percentage of total government
budget and of total GDP
N.    Quality of program leadership as indication of government priority
0.    Extent of commercial sector participation
II.    Indicators to measure resource adequacy
A.    Adequacy of numbers, types and distributions of staffing
B.    Adequacy of total financing, allocation among components, and speed and ease of
expenditure
C.    Adequacy of numbers and distribution of facilities, particularly for service delivery
D.    Adequacy of numbers, types, and distributions of supplies particularly contraceptives
E.    Adequacy of numbers, types, and distributions of equipment (office and service delivery)



166
Annex 10
Page 17 of 20
Process
III.   Indicators to measure service delivery operations
A.    Management
1.     Using the above policy and strategic plans as a basis, the definition of a clear
division of managerial responsibility, authority, and accountability which is
generally divided by top management having responsibility for policy and
strategic management and middle and lower management responsible for
operations management. [A clear definition and balance between centralization
and decentralization.]
2.     Clearly defined organizational structure
3.     Awareness by the appropriate managers of current financial allocations,
proportions, and percentage of changes
4.     Access (to the information system) by appropriate managers to respective
requisite information on program functions and progress
5.     Capacity to predict needs and track distribution of commodities
B.     Training
1.     Existence of overall pre-service and in-service staff development plan
2.     Annual number of trainees by type completing courses as compared to total
potential trainee population and relationship of this number to total number
expected according to overall plan
3.     Number/percentage of courses where appropriate training methods were used for
the jobs
4.     Number/percentage of courses that achieve learning objectives
5.     Number/percentage of courses that contribute to achievement of program
performance
6.     Lasting impact of training - follow-up evaluations indications of need for and
ideal timing of re-training for knowledge and skills forgotten or not performed
correctly from first training
C.     Commodities and logistics
1.     Pipeline wastage
2.     Percentage of storage capacity meeting acceptable standards
3.     Frequency and types of locations of stock-outs
4.     Percentage of service delivery points (SDPs) stocked according to plan
5.     Percentage of essential personnel at each level trained in contraceptive logistics
6.     Composite indicators of distributed commodities (mostly contraceptives) against
totals planned by province

Annex 10
Page 18 of 20
D.    Information, Education, and Communication (IEC)
1.    Number of communication programs, materials, or events produced by type
2.     Number of communication programs or materials disseminated, by type and
distribution patterns
3.     Percentage of target audience exposed to program messages, based on respondent
recall
4.     Percentage of audience exposed to a specific message who report liking it
E.    Research and Evaluation
1.     Presence of an active research and evaluation unit(s)
2.     Extent of use of an information system based firstly on service statistics
3.     Conduct and quality of periodic household and/or special purpose surveys and
studies
4.     Conduct and quality of operations research
5.     Conduct and quality of policy, strategic, cost-effectiveness, process efficiency,
and program impact evaluations
6.     Use of research and evaluation results for program decision making
7.     Dissemination  of research and evaluation results throughout the MPW
organization and to related population sector organizations
Outputs
IV.    Indicators to Measure Population Welfare Service Outputs
A.    Accessibility
1.    Number of SDP located within a fixed distance or travel time of client
populations (service density)
2.     Changes in the real and perceived costs of one month's supply of contraceptives
as a percentage of monthly wages
3.     Changes in restrictive program policies on contraceptive choice
4.     Percentage of the population who know at least one source of contraceptive
service outlet/supply source
5.     Percentage of non-use related to psycho-social barriers
B.    Quality of Care
I.     Number of contraceptive methods available at a specific SDP
2.     Percentage of counseling session with new acceptors in which provider discusses
all methods
3.     Percentage of client visits during which provider demonstrates skill at clinical
procedures, including asepsis
4.     Percentage of clients reporting "sufficient time" with provider

2      Percentage of target audience who acquire the skill to complete a certain task as

3.     Number/percentage of target audience who discuss message(s) with others

Outcome

F.    Changes in user characteristics
G.    Changes in continuation rates among current acceptors

C.    Levels and changes in "ever used" clients
D.    Source of supply (by method)
E.    Failure rates
Impact

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Technical Assistance
Technical Assistance: A Program Approach
1.      Coordination of donor technical assistance is necessary to ensure consistency with and solid
support for the program approach for the entire project. The increased managerial demands of
implementing a program approach underline the importance of technical assistance for management
and institutional development, over and above technical assistance for specific project components.
Thus, current, committed, and likely proposed technical assistance from other donors was reviewed
and appraised; the Government and IDA jointly then attempted to identify any current and future gaps
in technical assistance. At present, such gaps appear to be relatively few.
2.      Under the program  approach to technical assistance, IDA will finance the balance of the
technical assistance required for the entire population program, after existing and planned donor
assistance has been considered. A summary of overall technical assistance requirements in presented
in Table Al 1.1, with details in Tables A1l.2 to Al1.4.
Table A1 .1

Annex 1 1
Page 2 of 12
3.     As shown in Table A1l.l, separate provisions have been made for the Village Family
Planning Worker Program and for overall program support, which are described in detail below.
Although the technical assistance requirements were determined jointly with Government and other
donors based on an assessment of program needs, they are likely to be overprogrammed, particularly
for the earlier years. Uncertainty of donor plans for the later years gives IDA a larger share of TA in
the out-years, which is likely to decline as other donor TA programs are finalized.
Support for the Village Workers Program
4.     The major new initiative of the PWP, the Village-Based Family Planning Worker Program,
will have substantial technical assistance requirements for training, operations research, establishing
favorable employment conditions, and building community support for the village-based workers.
5.     Training. Local and expatriate consultant expertise would be used intermittently to build up
and supplement the master trainers group in the Directorate of Clinical Training. The technical
assistance proposed is specifically for support of the VFPW program and would be additional to other
TA proposed for the Directorate of Clinical Training. The TA would support materials development,
teaching of interpersonal communication, planning and assessment of training methods, and
curriculum and materials revision.
6.     Operations researclh. Additional support to the village workers program includes ongoing
operations research, which is a part of the overall program support. A part will be financed by Asian
Development Bank, and the balance will be supported by IDA. The surveys to be undertaken under
operations research will require hiring local staff to undertake interviews and data analysis.
7.    Employvmnent conditions for Village Workers. This component would examine current
employment conditions of village workers, and propose ways to improve staff performance and
retention through provision of adequate and timely remuneration and employment conditions.
8.     Cotmnusnitv support for village-based services. This component would explore issues and
experience related to community support for village based service delivery and conduct a pilot study
of community mobilization in support of the village-based worker program. It would include: a study
of perceptions of government officers (PWDs and others) and community influentials in relation to the
VFPW strategy itself and to community support of the strategy; presentation of experience of
community involvement in village service delivery from other countries (such as Jiggasha project in
Bangladesh); and a pilot study of community mobilization in support of VFPWs.
9.     Cost-effectiveness of village-based services. This component, to be undertaken by an
independent agency appointed by the Government, would evaluate the cost-effectiveness of the
Village-Based Family Planning Worker Program and the Village Health Worker Program. The
evaluation would encompass broader public health objectives in addition to family planning.
Provisions have been made for both prospective and ex-post evaluations once both service delivery
schemes are on the ground.



172
Annex II
Page 3 of 12
Management of Technical Assistance
10.    Effective management of technical assistance under the program approach is critical. The
aims are to ensure consistency, continuity, and coverage, while avoiding overloading key staff. The
continuing coordination of technical assistance efforts will be a priority so that frequent changes in
systems and procedures, poor use of MPW staff time, and conflicting program changes may be
avoided. The current mechanism of donor coordination meetings will help minimize these problems.
11.   In addition to overall coordination of technical assistance, operational review of technical and
managerial inputs will be needed. An agency with an established international reputation for
operations research in family planning, which is acceptable to GOP, will serve as an independent
arbiter between GOP and IDA to monitor progress in the implementation of the program. Terms of
reference for technical assistance for overall program support are attached.



173
Annex 1 1
Page 4 of 12
Attachment
Terms of Reference:
Technical Assistance for Overall Program Support
Rationale
1.     Achievement of the Population Welfare Program's Eighth Plan objectives will require an increase
in the number of IEC and MCH/family planning services (and therefore coverage) and a significant
improvement in quality of both types of services. These changes in capacity and performance imply
major increases in organizational resources of all types, but more importantly this implies improvements
in existing capability.  Existing managers, staff and the systems they use to plan, operate and monitor
the above services will be upgraded to deliver the levels of performance necessary to meet the objectives.
2.     In addition, this program has extensive and varied international support. Most of the current
international support has been designed with full consideration of the other donor agencies existing or
planned assistance. Nevertheless, operational coordination will be quite difficult. The Government
officially will have the responsibility to coordinate donor inputs and assistance, but this is a major and
complex task requiring understanding of the needs of both parties. As part of this donor coordination,
there will be a need to periodically assess and evaluate the implementation and impact of the program.
This needs to be done independently.
Objective
3.     This technical assistance is to achieve the following:
a.    to provide technical, program, organizational/managerial advice and operational support
to the Government of Pakistan, particularly the Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW)',
in order to facilitate the program's implementation and achieve the Government's overall
population program objectives; and
b.     to provide program and organizational/managerial analysis and advice to the World Bank
and to other population program donors.
Major Tasks
4.     Assist MPW  in improving organizational performance through organizational and managerial
reform. This may include inter alia systems development or improvements in policy planning; strategic
planning; operational/work planning; human resource planning and management; technical planning and
management; IEC planning and management; procurement/logistics planning and management; financial
planning and management; transport planning and operations; facility planning and management; and
monitoring and evaluation operations.
I/Understood to include the provincial Departments of Population Welfare throughout.



174
Annex 11
Page 5 of 12
5.     Conduct operational research on various aspects of the program.  In particular, new  or
problematic program components should be the focus during the first 12 to 18 months of the project. The
results of the analyses from these studies should form the basis for program design and
organizational/managerial advice (for immediate corrections) as well as feeding into the formal planning
process (for more major/longer-term changes).
6.     The two main areas which will require considerable operations research and other assistance are
the two major areas of expansion: (a) the new Village Based Family Planning Worker service cadre and
delivery system; and (b) making family planning services an integral part of public sector health services,
particularly in the provincial health infrastructure. The VFPW assistance would include helping prepare
detailed implementation plans for each expansion phase at province level and below, and assistance with
field monitoring, surveillance, and research to help feedback implementation problems to managers. The
same type of intensive assistance should be provided for providing family planning within the public
health system; however, in addition, the consultants should facilitate the building of inter-organizational
relationships.
7.     Assist MPW  in the planning and re-design of their information systems, particularly service
statistics systems (formats and procedures) as well as  their other regularly collected monitoring
information. Assist MPW in the analysis and reporting of their service statistics and other regularly
collected monitoring information. This should include, not only written reports, but also preparation of
presentations to MPW, DOH, NGOs, and other program managers.
8.     After the major expansion of IEC and service delivery infrastructure has been achieved, assistance
should be provided to improve the quality of services. This would include defining specific quality
assurance procedures with MPW and NGO staff; assist in the design of pilot activities involving clients
and communities in the definition of service quality issues and needs; and up-dating the clinical and non-
clinical training curricula to include these procedures.
9.     All of the above assistance should be delivered with the overriding aim of institutional and
manager/staff development. Therefore, all the above assistance should be conceived and delivered with
learning and performance improvement as the underlying objective. Thus, the consultants will:
a. provide formal off-the-job training in topics defined by training needs assessments:
i.     organize training for similar level groups and mixed level groups depending on
the topic; and
ii.    organize formal training workshops; there will be both learning and a product
which the participants have produced during the training which will be
immnediately useful to them in their jobs;
b. on-the-job training for the same groups including:
i.     counselling and coaching on operational tasks;
ii.    collaborative re-design of systems and procedures;
iii.   informal job analysis based on agreed procedures agreed; and
iv.    instruction and preparation on the use of job aids.



175
Annex 11
Page 6 of 12
These training activities should be organized with both managers and technical/program staff participation
and include mixes of Federal and Provincial staff at all levels - Provincial, Divisional, District, and
Tehsil. Managers, program research/statistical staff [both at the National Institute for Population Studies
and within MPW] as well as those working with the VFPW operations should be given priority.
Preservice and in-service training of technical staff has reasonably good coverage and quality for some
time. While this should continue to be emphasized, it is in the maintenance phase, whereas training for
managers and others has to be built up or significantly improved.
10.   In order to provide objective assessments of the program's achievements, conduct independent
surveys as well as analyses of service statistics. The nature of the surveys should be agreed with the
Government and IDA, but the detailed design and implementation of the survey(s) should be done
independently by the consulting organization. The organization would provide the analysis, conclusions,
and recommendations to the Government, IDA, and the other donors ideally two months before the
annual program planning meetings. This would pave the way for establishing a monitoring and evaluation
system (including data collection) independent of the Ministry's service statistics system, which would
then be extended to cover other public health interventions.
1l.    Provide support for project supervision by submitting twice-yearly reports consistent with IDA's
supervision plan (Annex 18), on inputs and outputs, including policy and institutional issues and agreed
project performance indicators.
12.    Conduct at least one major program evaluation toward the end of the project period. This should
first examine the program achievement macro indicators such as changes in fertility and health/fertility-
related behavior and causality. It should also examine overall organizational operations including:
changes in service quantities and qualities; differences in organizational performance for common services
provided, and effectiveness of organizational inter-relationships (for example, were supplies actually
provided by one organization to another).
13.    Provide coordination for other technical assistance. As this technical assistance should be more
comprehensive and provide on-going, on-the-ground support, it should assist the MPW in coordinating
other technical assistance provided by IDA and other donors.  This would include briefing new
consultants and assisting them during their detailed work with understanding issues, operations, and
potential constraints on change. It would involve providing guidance to such consultants for the sake of
overall work program consistency so that recommendations of consultants hired by different donors would
not have conflict, or arrive at conclusions based on conflicting data. This would include keeping a
database/reference center for all technical assistance provided during the project.
Implementation Arrangements
14.   This TA would be implemented over four years, beginning in July 1995, with MPW  as the
executing agency. The first year would coincide with the Asian Development Bank funded TA contract
which focuses on organization and management development assistance similar to those specified above.
In keeping with the program approach for the entire program, specific tasks would be agreed in annual
tripartite planning exercises [MPW, IDA, and the consulting organization] which should be done as part
of, or immediately after, the overall annual program planning exercise. There would be a comprehensive
midterm review of the TA.



176
Annex I 1
Page 7 of 12
15.    The contract will finance about 15 person years of assistance in the first year; 40-45 person years
in the second year; declining to about 36 and 30 person years in the last two years respectively. The
first year total would also be about 45 person years of assistance, but with 30 person years financed by
ADB. In general, this assistance should provide the equivalent of 3 international person years per annum
and the balance as national inputs. While the expertise requirements will vary over time and be agreed
annually as part of the tripartite agreement, it is expected that two of the international person years will
be provided by full-time residential staff and the balance through short-term consultants. Most of the
national inputs will be provided by full-time staff, including support personnel and field interviewers.
Qualifications of Consulting Organization
16.    The providing organization must have a long and highly regarded reputation in international
population program technical assistance.
17.   Ideally, the organization would have recent experience in the Pakistan population program or
similar programs in the South Asia Region.
18.    The organization would have experience in population program  planning; management
development; organizational development; operations research; information systems design and operation
including service statistics; evaluation studies design and implementation; consulting; training
design/implementation/evaluation; and technical assistance management.
19.    The organization will need to engage a significant number of national staff to conduct the surveys;
therefore, they must have experience in large scale administrative support (ideally in Pakistan). In
addition, there will be two international resident staff, who will require administrative support, as well
as support for short term technical visitors.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
AMlanagement Systems, Practices and Developments
I.    The process of strategic planning begun under SAPP will be maintained under the present project.
Under this process, sectoral policies, strategies and resource allocations will be defined, revised as
necessary, and agreed. The day-to-day planning process will be actively linked to the planning of
components, which in turn will feed into work planning.
2.     By the time this project is underway, most of thefinatncial management processes should have been
streamlined so that the central release, expenditure, accounting, and auditing procedures place a minimum
of managers' time. However, efforts will be continued during the life of the project to reinforce this. The
project itself should create a very little additional financial management work for most program managers.
3.     Efforts at improving human resource planning and management were begun as part of the project's
preparation. Designing and implementing a quality human resource planning sub-system de novo is usually
takes  a  decade.   Coordinating  changes  in  the  two  other management sub-systems--staff
productionldevelopment and personnel--with the planning sub-system is typically an ongoing effort in large
organizations. Managers at various levels need to be involved in this effort in different ways. Since social
change programs depend on people changing the behavior of other people, human resources are critical
to the program's success. Therefore, human resource management must be the top priority for all
managers.
4.     Through a project launch workshop, it is expected that managers will agree on a work planning
methodology. This may be similar to the approach that MPW currently uses, although it could be
enhanced by computer-based project management software as recommended by IDA for large, complicated
projects. Managers will be required to prepare annual work plans, as they now do, and use them as the
main guide in their operational management. However, work plans should be broken down to lower levels
of management, so that each manager and his/her team have their respective work plans. Ideally, these
work plans should be developed through a participatory planning process involving all members of eaci
team (see below for team development and management).
5.     Senior managers will participate in annual reviews and program revision/replanning jointly with
IDA staff. It is expected that the mechanisms for monitoring the population program will be maintained
whether or not there is a second phase for the SAP, including the Social Sector Coordinating Committee
(SSCC) and its equivalent in the provinces. This will provide the opportunity for improvement in macro
management, since senior GOP managers from  other ministries will also be reviewing MPW's
performance.
6.     As part of the annual review and work planning exercise, an analysis of previously agreed
indicators of project performance, based on monitoring and evaluation data from the preceding year, will
be presented (see Annex 10). These indicators may be adjusted for the coming year, but once set for a
given year they will be adhered to in terms of collection and analysis. Quantitative annual values need to

Accountability
7.     Oversight management of the  entire
program  will be done by MPW, with line
departments, NGOs, and  the private  sector
managing  their  respective  components  and
services. As part of its coordinating role, MPW                      Figure 1
must deliver financial and/or supply support to
other organizations requiring such support
promptly and effectively.
8.     MPW  has already begun to devolve responsibility and authority, particularly for financial and
personnel matters, to operational and service levels. This is particularly important between federal and
provincial levels and will continue, PWD will find it extremely difficult to achieve its fertility decline
objectives without this.
9.     As Fig. 1 shows, managers need to have both authority and responsibility so that they can perform
their tasks and functions. Only then can they reasonably be held accountable for their own and their team's
performance. Devolution of authority closer to actual service delivery will allow the program to operate
with the geographic flexibility that a national social change effort of this nature requires. Without adequate
authority, persons who are nominally in positions titled 'manager' are not managers in reality.
10.    Decentralization of authority inevitably involves a continual 'balancing act.' Senior federal or
provincial managers would maintain control over policy, strategy and centrally-retained operations. While
many changes are expected during the initial SAP period, additional fine tuning of the decision-making
systems will be needed under the project.  Decentralization needs to take the form of integrated
management 'packages' comprising the three elements noted above. These start with the relevant civil
service regulations but must include feasible and acceptable options for management of the population
welfare program. The process of dividing and devolving authority and responsibility should be driven by

11.    The contraceptive supply system should be significantly improved during the SAP period. While
parts of the system have worked well, the erratic availability of supplies at service delivery points indicates
uneven performance. During the project period, the revised supply system needs to be fine-tuned based
on an assessment of the system's needs at the start of the project.
12.    The MPW/PWDs task is a large social change effort. Thus, managers should have a downward
focus within the organization and an outward focus toward the public. Such a downward/outward focus
is not usual in large public sector organizations; a change in the orientation of the MPW/PWD is therefore
needed. This change should be initiated by senior management, who should maintain direct contact with
the public and carefully review surveys of public opinion and other indications of public sentiment. Both
senior and middle managers need to be aware of the views of the public so that program interventions can
be adjusted accordingly. The interface between clients and staff is the most critical relationship in the
entire program. As Fig. 2 indicates, it is the responsibility of managers to ensure that all the support
streams work effectively so that field staff can perform at their best in the staff-client relationship.
13.    With the introduction of the SAP and the additional support provided under this project, the total
resource package, and the linked political support, should be adequate for the program to achieve its
objectives. A major risk to the success of the program arises from management deficiencies. With the
assistance of the Asian Development Bank (ADB), there will be a major effort in management development
during the SAP assistance period. This program will help to improve the performance of managers at all
levels and will facilitate many of the organizational changes and system improvements mentioned above.
If the ADB assistance is not sufficient in either scope or duration, UK ODA is prepared to provide further
assistance in this critical area. The management assistance effort should help managers in MPW and other
organizations associated with the program to improve their performance in the following areas:
a. setting priorities for themselves and their teams on a yearly, monthly, and daily basis;
b. making decisions based on the information system--which needs some enhancements so that the
outputs can be easily and directly used in decision making; and



185
Annex 12
Page 4 of 11
c. understanding the dynamics of organizational changes (structures, functional distributions,
relationships), their impact on the way tasks are divided and, ultimately, on performance.
Management Improvement
14.    Recent management studies have demonstrated that 'successful' managers do very different things
from 'effective' managers. These studies defined 'successful' managers as those who climbed up the
organizational ladder more rapidly than others, whereas 'effective' managers were those who, working
with the people under them, were able to achieve their work unit's objectives. Given that the objective of
the PWD and associated organizations is to create and support social change, it is clear that a critical mass
of 'effective' managers, proficient in team building and team work, will be required.
15.    Managerial functions will need to evolve over the SAP/project period.  The following are
indications, beyond the official job descriptions, for elements which should become part of management
responsibilities for each type of manager.
a.     Tehsil.
i.     Provide 'full-life' models of job requirements, conditions, and acceptable
performance, based on the realities of working conditions:
(1)    Define/list all the decisions he/she can make;
(2)    Define options/alternatives for each decision;
(3)    Define results/consequences of each type of decision alternative;
(4)    Define/edit all steps and procedures for the various management sub-
systems which he/she must apply;
(5)    Revise job description separately; with other staff in a tehsil team; and as
part of the district team;
(6)    Prepare case studies and possibly a video of good/bad performance;
ii.    Prepare and provide work planning and monitoring tools (enhancements of the
existing MIS system so that monitoring easily and directly supports actual
decisions)
iii.    Make these officers DDOs (Category IV) and ensure that they have an impressed
account of at least Rs. 500;
iv.    Give them full control over tehsil-level vehicles, including scheduling, use, POL
and maintenance;
v.     Expand their authority over staff to the maximum allowable under civil service
rules;
vi.    Use TA/DA as staff rewards; try to increase communities' involvement in the
reward system for staff (various options); allow VFPWs to keep contraceptive sale

receipts (the performance value of the incentive is high and the total 'costs' of
collection are far higher than the value);
vii.    Use 'come-and-get' supply system for obtaining supplies from districts;
b.     District Level:
i.     Provide 'full-life' models of job requirements, conditions, and acceptable

(1)    Define/list all the decisions he/she can make;

e.     Federal Level:
xvii.  Ensure that senior managers travel an average of at least 8 days a month together
with provincial, divisional, and district officers to build ownership and
commitment of provincial, divisional, urban, and community leaders towards the

xviii.  Decentralize all relevant management consulting and training to the provinces by:

(2)    devolving management of the PWTIs in Lahore and Karachi to their

(3)    closing the PWTI in Multan;
xix.   Develop enhanced policy and strategic planning, analysis, and monitoring
capacity;
xx.    Become more directly involved in management training.
16.    Wherever possible, female staff should be supervised by female managers. This is particularly
needed at the lower levels of the organization. Since the program will continue to target women, female
managers will be more effective. They will tend to have more suitable concerns and commitment, promote
more appropriate types of activities, and achieve better levels of performance.
17.    Commnlunities need to be directly involved in program management. This was attempted during the
early Bank-supported project, although implementation was uneven. Experience indicated that the seed
funds which were put at the disposal of the management committees were not needed. A standard formula
for community involvement should not be attempted; communities should be allowed to work out their own
mechanisms, although they could be assisted by providing a list of options.
18.    Performance. The existing systems are designed to sanction the worst performer and catch the
most corrupt. However, this approach is unlikely to produce the desired results. Any organization must
be run by the law of averages and must accept that there is a bell-shaped curve of ability and potential
performance among staff. There is a tendency to focus on the poor performers and use them as an excuse
for overall inaction or poor performance. The 80/20 rule applies 20% of the staff do 80% of the work,
or at least the best work; those 20% will do 80% of their work in 20% of their time; and so on. Managers
should focus on the best and the average performers, encouraging them to move the whole curve of
performance higher. The lowest performing quarter to third of the staff have to be ignored in most
instances unless they are or become totally disruptive to others' performance. The best strategy for
breaking the 80/20 rule seems to be teamwork management and team performance measures.
19.    Managers also need to focus on priorities and major problems so that their time, which is the most
important resource in the entire organization, is not wasted on solving problems which have little or no
impact on overall performance. Problem priority matrices such as the example shown below often help



189
Annex 12
Page 8 of 11
in this continual sorting process. Managers will be encouraged to ask, 'does this problem make any
difference to the performance of my unit? and 'what is the most important thing I can do today to improve
our performance?
20.    The management cycle - planning, implementation, monitoring and evaluation - must be complete
at every level of management. This does not imply that a manager at the lowest level has complete control
over all the resources he/she needs to carry out his/her unit's responsibilities. It does mean that he/she
should have control over all resources except where significant savings can be gained through higher level
decision making (e.g. contraceptives procurement) or where equity and/or standardization are overriding
policy concerns (e.g. some types of public sector staff recruitment). If the cycle is not complete, managers
will continue to exist only in name, not in function.
21.   Particularly in this type of social change program, top-to-bottom management relationships need
to operate in both directions. Road construction or similar activities can be accomplished by a directive,
linear programming type of management; social change cannot. It is critical that higher level managers
know what is happening at the periphery, both geographically and socially. This implies that managers
must be highly mobile and must have open and complete communication with other managers and staff.
They must be performance problem solvers, not performance problem creators.
22.   This program's ultimate aim is to get a significant number of eligible couples to make desired
decisions about fertility and family planning and turn those decisions into action. Supportive decision
making should be applied at the interface between the program and the public. This should be built on the
Socratic approach (always ask; don't tell--communicating positions through questioning). In order for staff
to understand, feel comfortable with, and use this approach, it should be mirrored within and throughout
the organization.
23.    Much of accepted management tends to become 'file work' for the purpose of documenting
decisions. However, this paper trail tends to become an end in itself. When the span of control of a given
manager is limited and he/she is working with staff as a team, the amount of written instructions should
be minimal. Most of what is required should be available in the form of work plan and monitoring
information.

24.    Change in management practices and behaviors is always difficult for all concerned--higher level
as well as lower level managers. External partners/facilitators can greatly assist this change process.
Organizational change always has elements of trial and error. It is by definition a risky business, and
managers, particularly senior managers, need someone to transfer these risks to so that:
a.     some of the resistance to change among lower level managers and staff will be directed at
these external change agents, rather than the senior managers;
b.     the external consultants rather than senior management can take the blame for change
interventions if they are deemed, after a fair trial, to be inappropriate for the organization.
25.    Management has two types of 'raw material '. managers' abilities and information. It needs a set
of 'tools' to process these 'raw materials' into good decisions which will translate into actions on the part
of the organization that will achieve the desired results. Currently, the 'tools' are understood to be
generally administrative in nature. That is, the purpose of most of the 'tools' is to record a decision, not
to help a manager make the decision. New management system tools are needed at all levels-- policy
planning, strategic planning, program planning, work planning, implementation, monitoring and
evaluation.
26.    The other half of the MIS has to be designed, developed and implemented. The MIS system has
been designed from the data perspective, not on the basis of management decisions. The design and
development process should proceed along the following lines:
a.     definition of needed decisions at each level;
b.     decision criteria for each;
c.     information system outputs for each decision,
d.     analysis processes/procedures and database structure;
e.     data input requirements.
This should result in information outputs directly supporting data-based decision making, which will greatly
simplify the process for managers. If they have to 'add up the numbers' and derive the implications for
decisions, most managers will not do it.
27.    Staff rewards at all levels need to be maximized. Incentives for performance have to be made
available at all levels. Simple things like TA/DA are seen as rewards. Control over the award of
discretionary rewards tends to be concentrated at the top. While the civil service system cannot be
changed, various discretionary rewards can be linked to performance and more evenly distributed.
28.    Organizational and  management development need  to  become  totally  integrated  and
institutionalized functions. The Implementation Plan calls for a top-to-bottom functional and structural
analysis (federal to village level). If this is to be worth the effort, the results must be in terms of



192
Annex 12
Page 11 of 11
organizational development.  For an organization the size of MPWlDPWs, it will be a full-time
responsibility. There should be at least one person at federal level and one in each province with this
assignment (as in the Family Health model in the Department of Health. In the provinces, there should
be one or several PWTI staff currently providing management training who act as both consultants and
trainers. In addition, there need to be organizational development task forces, comprising managers
supported by full-time internal consultants/ trainers, who will implement or facilitate the changes. These
internal consultants will be supported by external consultants on a regular basis, particularly for the next
three to five years.
29.    Management training needs to be as job-specific as technical training. It will need to reflect
anticipated changes in structures, functions, and responsibilities that will result from current discussions
and the continuing organizational functional/structural analysis. It is important that management job
descriptions be defined in terms of the decisions to be made at each level and that subsequent courses be
built around practical, job-derived exercises which involve practice in making those decisions. If
management trainers are also intemal management consultants to the organization, they should be able to
generate job-based cases, which should comprise 80% of the training.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Cost-Effectiveness of Service Delivery
Overview of Cost-Effectiveness Analysis
1.    Cost effectiveness analysis, through service delivery distributions and cost matrices, is
designed to be a program management tool. It can aid in planning, monitoring, and evaluating
different types of service delivery interventions with similar objectives. However, while objectives
may be similar, in that most types of family planning services aim to reduce fertility, the actual
services offered at different outlets may not be strictly comparable.
2.     The matrices display all the programmatic interventions under any operational manager's span
of control. The matrix may include: (a) types of interventions; (b) relationships between operating
units; (c) average coverage of each unit; (d) expected workload of each unit; (e) expected impact per
intervention or for the program as a whole (acceptors or IEC coverage); (f) total costs of each
intervention; (g) unit cost per acceptor of each intervention; (h) relative costs of each intervention; (i)
overall average costs per acceptor within the manager's jurisdiction; and (j) total program and
administrative costs for the planning period (usually one year).
3.     As a management tool, cost-effectiveness matrices give managers an overview of: (a) program
performance and possible problems; (b) options for optimal resource re-allocation; (c) relative
contribution of each intervention (such as whether a component has reached a point of diminishing
returns and should be closed); (d) whether a component is achieving its objectives; and (e) whether
the objectives are correctly defined.
4.     The same matrix format can be used at all levels of management--from tehsil to federal. At
each level of management and each location, the program components may change according to the
actual situation. For example, there may be no Reproductive Health Centre within a tehsil officer's
area, so that line would not appear on his/her form. All program components should appear no
matter how small the activity.
5.     These frameworks are not intended for target setting, but rather as an input to good planning
and operational management. Thus, numbers entered should be revised as managers obtain data on
actual performance. Absolute precision is less important than obtaining relative indicators of various
units' performance. Updating these forms could be one of the products of monthly or quarterly mini-
reviews/planning workshops. These forms can also be modified to suit managers' need for
information, such as percentage distributions to show relative costs across components.
Explanation of the Figures as Currently Entered
6.     The matrices were constructed using cost estimates in the PC-Is and projections of the
program's planned outcomes, defined as the product of what is expected/assumed in terms of present
and future service capacity multiplied with expected/assumed present and future levels of average



194
Annex 13
Page 2 of 5
performance. Note that these examples show only the first and last years of the program; a historical
record form should have all program years showing planned and actual figures.
7.     The first column has the numbers of all the current and planned facilities or individual service
providers. In all cases these numbers were drawn directly from the PC-Is. Note that the units in this
column are not comparable across categories. Some are service units with a corresponding
complement of personnel (such as static service units, like FWCs), while others are individuals
(TBAs, RMPs). Some units (such as FWCs) are directly supported and controlled by MPW, while
others (such as PLDs) may be marginally supported and not controlled in any way by MPW.
However, from a management perspective, these are the basic performance units for each component.
Data in the "Coverage/Facility or Provider" column were also obtained from the PC-I.
8.     The "Total Population Coverage" column is obtained by multiplying the first column by the
second column.
9.     The "Total Continuing Acceptors" column is derived by multiplying "Total Population
Coverage" by the percentage of expected continuing acceptors in the population. This was calculated
separately for each province, since it is expected that this would vary across provinces.  Percentages
for the beginning and end years also varied (ranging from just over 1 % in the beginning year for
Balochistan to over 4% in the final year in Punjab). The same beginning and ending year percentage
was applied across all components except for RHCs where the percentage was much lower, based on
assumed catchment areas and recent average performance. Note that both the total population
covered and the number of total continuing acceptors sum up to a figure 1. I to 3 times the actual
population/expected acceptors. This is due to assumed overlaps in coverage and population-to-
acceptor rates which are probably too high for some of the components. These figures need to be
revised during implementation.
10.    The next two columns, "Continuing Acceptors/Provider/Year" and "Continuing
Acceptors/Provider/Month" provide averages for each year and month, respectively. This provides
some validation for the coverage and acceptor rate assumptions.
11.    The figures in the "Total Cost/Component" column are taken directly from the PC-Is. These
would be adjusted annually with ADP-approved allocations.
12.    The "Cost/Continuing Acceptors" column is obtained by dividing "Total Cost/Component"
by the "Total Continuing Acceptors" for that year. This allows managers to assess relative
differences in cost per continuing acceptor (a measure of cost-effectiveness). This does not imply that
components which are less cost-effective should be closed down. Often they provide other functions
which are essential to the program's operation. Moreover, costs for those indirect program functions
may be also charged to that component instead of being distributed across other units. For example,
FWCs provide both supervision and act as a limited supply base in addition to actual family planning
service delivery.
13.    After the costs of all the direct service delivery components have been calculated, the program
overheads are added at the bottom of each page to arrive at a better estimate of total program
costs/per acceptor.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Program Phasing
Overview
1.     The proposed expansion of the population program involves increasing the quantity and
improving the quality of services, and directly involving more organizations. The program will also
introduce or significantly expand a number of untried or experimental/small scale interventions.
Thus, predicting trends in performance and resource requirement growth is somewhat problematic.
2.     The original program and financial plans called for a very rapid expansion of services and
resource needs in the first few years and then a slowing of growth from between one-sixth to one-
fourth the rate of early growth. The proposed pattern of growth resembled an "S-shaped" growth
curve. This pattern is both common and desirable for the type of program rapid expansion which will
be necessary if the proposed objectives are to be met'.
3.     The original Government plans estimated resource requirements for individual program
intervention streams and then aggregated them to produce separate plans for each province and for the
Federal program (for national, Federal Areas, and cross-cutting interventions). While these plans are
necessary and produced a wealth of planning, management, and financial information, they are not
organized or formatted in a manner which allows for easy time series analysis and adjustment,
analysis of cross-cutting interventions, or analysis across geographic areas.
4.     During project preparation, summary frameworks were developed to integrate the
quantification of service delivery capacities, expected workloads, outputs, and costs. These
summaries allowed for easier and more valid prediction of operational and financial phasing. The
summaries also make it easier to adjust yearly targets and estimates of resources required as the
program is implemented.
5.     These summary frameworks were originally developed as a program management tool. The
framework also show cost-effectiveness of different intervention streams relative to each other; within
each intervention relative to changes over time; and comparisons of the same type of intervention
implemented in different geographic areas. As such these "tools," developed in spreadsheet format,
were designed to be an aid in both planning, operational management, monitoring, as well as a
information source for evaluation.
6.     The same methodolgy was used to revise the prediction for resource requirements based on
the Government's revised estimates (in consultation with IDA staff) of future service delivery
I "Life Cycles: Forecasting the Rise and Fall of Almost Anything' by Theodore Modis The Futurist: A Journal of Forecasts, Trends and Ideas about the
Future Septerr.ber-October, 1994 Volume 28 No 5



199
Annex 14
Page 2 of 13
capacity. The revisions made varied considerably across types of interventions. In some cases,
MPW had revised their estimates of the number of service delivery points. In most such cases,
further reviews were minor. Modifications were made in the facility service provider numbers and
then the output and financing requirements were calculated at the same rates as the original plans
indicated. In other cases, usually non-facility based service delivery, revisions were made on the
basis of FY 1993/94 actual performance and agreed estimates for FY 1994/95. These patterns, along
with comparisons with the original PC-Is, were used as the basis for estimating requirements for
1995/96 and beyond. The further into the future, the closer the revised estimates to the original PC-1
requirements.
7.     Using a combination of revised estimates of delivery capacity and implementation/utilization
patterns produced a more realistic phasing for the overall program. As seen in Figure 1, the
proposed changes in phasing are not radical, but reflect a more realistic program development pattern.
In both cases, the program expands quickly in the early years and then plateaus. The revised
phasing, while still showing major growth in the second year, predicts growth will be slower in that
year than in the original estimates. More rapid growth than originally planned is projected in the
third and fourth years; the original planned growth in the fourth year; was less than 5 percent.
8.     This structure provides a convenient framework for tracking performance and modifying
predictions for future performance and resource needs. The method is likely to be used and up-dated
during the annual planning exercises. The structure allows planners, managers, and evaluators to
aggregate and disaggregate the entire national program to any level of geographic or program
intervention unit(s)2. Because it interrelates delivery capacity, expected performance, and financial
requirements, it is a more realistic and reliable tool than analyzing and predicting any one of these
independently. For example, Figure 2 shows Provincial and Federal disaggregated trends. These are
realistic in that the Punjab and the central Federal national programs (as opposed to the Federal
Areas) should produce the most growth. All the other Provinces and the Federal Areas indicate a
more modest straight-line growth with a gentle upward slope. The greatest program effort will be in
Punjab, which accounts for most of the population and where the program can probably expand the
most rapidly. The Federal programs also have the managerial capacity to launch relatively quickly
and expand significantly many of the central programs and organizational support functions, like IEC
and staff training. This structure allows the analysis of projections predictions from several
perspectives and to test for validity, internal consistency, and logic.
Specific Explanation of Calculations
9.     The physical aspects of service delivery capability were adjusted based on projections from
figures for 1993/94 and projections for 1994/95. These were derived from actual data for the third
quarter of 1993/94 collected during the appraisal mission, were subsequently revised when additional
information from the Provinces became available. Estimated trends from 1993/94 and 1994/95 were
used to derive growth/expansion assumptions for 1995/96 (see Tables 1 & 2). Then the PC-1
projections for the later years were reviewed and accepted if the trend "fit" a reasonable pattern of
1 Currently it is not disaggregated below the level of Province, following the structure of the PC-Is for population, but further disaggregation to lower
levels can easily be done, using the same methodology by planners and managers of districts & telisils.



200
Annex 14
Page 3 of 13
growth from 1994/5 and 1995/6. If the PC-1 levels for later years were not judged feasible, the
estimates were revised downward for the last two years (never upward). (See Tables 3 to 7 which
display the first and final year of the program by province/area as an example; the intermediate years
are available.
10.    The trends in service delivery capacity were used to predict the change in family planning
acceptors, using the revised formula for acceptors for each province/area provided by MPW (May
1994). Actual figures for the third quarter of 1993/94 were used to obtain predicted financial changes
for the end of 1993/94 and for 1994/95 and 1995/96. These were related to the physical capacity
estimates as a feasibility check. The last two years' estimates were obtained in the same manner as
the physical service delivery expansion by cross-checking PC-Is and assessing trend feasibility. The
results of the review suggest that some of the later stage program costs, particularly for provincial
line departments, and registered medical practitioners, may be under-estimated. In some cases,
changes in cost-effectiveness were used as a guide to estimate trends. Note that some interventions
initially become less cost-effective but then improve. As a whole, cost-effectiveness for all
interventions in all geographic areas improves over the five years.
