PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Nongovernmental Organizations in the PWPP
Background
1.     Over the past decade, the Government has increasingly recognized the complementary role
nongovernmental organizations (NGOs) play in facilitating social and economic development. In the
last few years, there has been a growing awareness of, and political commitment to, the need to
create and strengthen community organizations through intermediary NGOs. Such community
organizations can work in partnership with government agencies to improve the quality, efficiency
and effectiveness of services.
2.    NGOs have played a key role in the development and implementation of the national
population program. For example, in 1953 the Family Planning Association of Pakistan (FPAP) led
the way by introducing family planning services to Pakistan, and much of the Government's program
for the Seventh and Eighth Plans was modelled on NGO programs. Government policy since the
mid-1980s has actively encouraged NGOs to provide family planning services to complement its own
limited service delivery network. By the end of the Seventh Plan period, some 126 NGOs were
operating over 400 service delivery outlets. Moreover, NGOs have proven their ability to experiment
with innovative service delivery approaches and develop effective models for application by the
govermnent and other NGOs. The tendency of NGOs to work more closely with communities than
government, and their ability to tailor services to local need, has contributed to the success of NGO
projects. For example, in pilot community-based contraceptive distribution projects, NGOs have been
able to raise contraceptive prevalence in project areas from between 6-16% to 25-45 % over a two
year period.
3.    Despite the number of successful NGO projects, the overall performance of the NGO family
planning program has been mixed. The major constraints have included: (a) limited technical and
managerial capacity of both NGOs and the NGO Coordinating Council (NGOCC), which was created
by the government to promote and support population NGO activities; (b) financial dependence on
external donor financing which has been unstable and discontinuous; (c) NGOCC's narrow and rigid
focus on funding projects which replicated government service delivery models and until recently,
discouraged innovation and flexibility; and (d) poor coordination, networking and information-
sharing. The result has been a NGO family planning program of varying quality and effectiveness,
which has failed to draw on the strengths of NGOs.
4.    The NGO program is at present in transition. Firstly, the government has recently established
the National Trust for Population Welfare (NATPOW) to replace the NGOCC. The new Trust is an
autonomous NGO designed to address several of the systemic weaknesses of NGOCC. In particular,
it is expected to broaden the parameters of population welfare, to move away from a single focus on
service delivery to a more holistic and integrated approach to population and development. Such a
change in emphasis will enable broader participation in the NGO program. Furthermore, the Trust is



212
Annex 15
Page 2 of 7
intended to pay particular attention to building the capacity of NGOs, and comnmunity based
organizations (CBOs), and fostering greater NGO coordination. Secondly, external donor support for
NGOs has almost dried up, and is unlikely to pick up until the new Trust is operational, with the
result that many NGO family planning projects have closed down or been scaled back.
5.       To sustain effective NGO family planning projects and assist in the institution building of
the new Trust, IDA has agreed to provide technical assistance and financial support to the program
during 1994/95 under the Social Action Program Project (SAPP). Technical assistance will be
engaged to evaluate which of the projects funded by NGOCC merit continued support in 1994/95
based on performance over the past two years and availability of field, supervisory and management
staff equipped to implement the project in 1994/95 - and thereafter to provide technical back-up to
them, prior to the Trust having the capacity to take over this function. Secondly, technical assistance
will be engaged to assist the Trust in developing a strategic plan, including organizational goals and
objectives; the organization's structure and management systems, including financial; the
organization's rules of business and project appraisal, monitoring and evaluation procedures; and
recruiting and training staff. In the absence of government and donor finance for the approved
1994/95 program, projects which provide community based services, and/or involve the community in
the management, monitoring or implementation of services, or demonstrate how linkages can be
fostered between communities, NGOs and government services will be eligible for support from the
SAPP Participatory Development Program.
PWPP: NGO Program
6.     To support the Government's policy of enhancing the involvement of the private sector in the
population program, IDA aims to strengthen and support NGOs to enable them to increase the
acceptability, availability, quality and demand for family planning, through a US$5 million fund for
NGO activities. The developments outlined in para. 4 above, constitute the necessary up-front actions
for the NGO program proposed under the PWPP. The IDA fund will be managed by the Trust. The
specific objectives of the proposed NGO program reflect those of the Government and those
anticipated for the Trust, especially the commitment to broaden the scope of NGO participation in the
population sector, and more broadly, to create institutional capacity at the community level to enable
communities to participate in the delivery and management of social services. The Government's
objectives in four key areas are:
a.     Reproductive health services
i.    to develop the capacity of NGOs and CBOs to promote and deliver quality
reproductive health services;
ii.    to expand NGO community based service delivery;
iii.   to experiment with innovative methods of delivery, cost-recovery and
integration of reproductive health with other social services;
iv.    to increase the participation of communities in the planning, delivery,
monitoring and management of reproductive health services; and



213
Annex 15
Page 3 of 7
v.    to improve links between communities, NGOs and government services.
b.     Demand generation
i.    to increase demand for basic social services; and
ii.    to increase the involvement of the private sector in IEC activities.
c.     To institutionalize population as an integral part of development
i.    to broaden awareness of population and the social and economic factors that
influence desired family size, amongst NGOs and CBOs; and
ii.    to assist and support NGOs with the integration of population
concerns/activities into their program planning and operations.
d.    Institutional development
i.    develop in-country NGO technical and managerial capacity to enable (a) to
(c);
ii.    to support community-based institution building to enable (a) to (c);
iii.   to support the development of the National Trust for Population Welfare; and
iv.    facilitate NGO networks, information-sharing and coordination.
Eligibility and Criteria for Appraising Project Proposals
7.     NGOs in Pakistan fall into a range of types according to approach, priorities, maturity and
size. The PWPP NGO Fund will support different types of NGOs -- development and welfare-
oriented; sector specific, integrated community development, capacity-building; well-established and
young organizations; national and small-scale - based on the proposal's merit, and the capacity of
the organization to implement the proposed project. There are three modalities for supporting the
different types of NGOs:
a.    large, well-established NGOs with a proven track record in implementing population,
health or community development programs may apply directly to the Trust;
b.    small, less developed NGOs are eligible to receive support through the Trust either
directly or preferably via NGO grant-making organizations with the capability of
administering funds and providing technical and managerial support to the
implementing NGO; i.e., grant-making organizations - such as the Trust for
Voluntary Organizations, South Asia Partnership - Pakistan - will prepare a program
proposal for consideration by the Trust to support a number of NGO projects; and



214
Annex 15
Page 4 of 7
c.    institution building NGOs, such as the Small Projects Office, the National Rural
Support Program, may apply direct to the management agency.
8.     All NGO recipients of fund finance must be NGOs registered in Pakistan. They must be non-
profit making, non-sectarian and without political affiliations. The maximum funding period is three
years. Details regarding disbursement and administration of the fund are found below.
9.     The fund will be used to support NGO projects and activities, including training and national
and international technical assistance, which meet one or more of the specific objectives outlined
above. Prior to submission of project proposals, NGOs should seek confirmation that they meet the
eligibility criteria, and clarify which funding modality applies. Appraisal and selection of project
proposals will be based on the following criteria:
a.     clear objectives and sound strategy;
b.     geographical location, with priority given to less developed regions, rural areas and
low-income urban communities;
C.     extent of community or beneficiary demand;
d.     extent of community participation in project planning, implementation, monitoring,
management, and financing;
e.    links with government services;
f.    innovative and experimental approaches with scope for wider application if successful;
g.     project impact on disadvantaged groups;
h.     cost-effectiveness and value-for-money;
i.    institutional development;
j.     sustainability of the project;
k.    technical and management capability; and
I.     capital costs of less than a specified percentage (to be determined) of project costs.
Disbursement Mechanisms
10.    NATPOW will act as an intermediary between IDA and the local/community level NGOs
which are the intended beneficiaries. Thus, there are two separate financing relationships that need to
be established in order to ensure proper financial management of the project. The first is the
disbursement mechanism between IDA and NATPOW; the second is the subsequent relationship
between NATPOW and the NGOs. These two systems need to be linked and coordinated by a



215
Annex 15
Page 5 of 7
mechanism which ensures that a smooth unimpeded flow of funds from IDA to the NGOs by way of
NATPOW, with a minimum of bureaucratic obstacles.
11.   The most practical funding mechanism in this case is the establishment of a Special Account.
NATPOW would have access to the funds in the Special Account to fund approved NGO recipients
falling within the scope of its activities framework, and the drawdown of funds would be replenished
by IDA upon receipt of full justification of the appropriate use of funds. This process would ensure
an adequate flow of funds to NATPOW for operational purposes. All disbursement procedures will
operate directly between the NATPOW administration and IDA.
12.    Since recipient NGOs may differ among each other in terms of orientation, size, structure,
and amount of resources available, a single mechanism may not be suitable for the financial
arrangements for all NGOs. The appropriate mechanism will depend on an organization's track
record, the activity being funded, and the organization's needs and capabilities. The premium should
be on allowing flexibility while retaining transparency and accountability of funds use at all times.
13.   A prerequisite to NATPOW management of the funds, is that NATPOW has an acceptable
financial management system operating. NATPOW will be responsible for financial monitoring of
NGO activities financed by the fund. The fund will be audited on a regular basis according to
standard government and IDA procedures.
14.   The mechanism likely to be the most appropriate, in the light of the above, is as follows.
NATPOW supplies all or part of the approved funding for an approved project as a grant. For larger
grants, releases may be tranched over periods of time or at intervals pending review of work done up
to the date of the next scheduled tranche. The payment may be ex-post, that is, being released
following submission of SOEs or more commonly ex-ante in the form of advance payments.
15. Advances are the most effective - even if unsecured as may be the case - if they are small and
reasonable, part of a contractual arrangement, based on actual calculation of funds needed to initiate
project activities, and clear with regard to the purposes for which such advances may be used. These
are especially helpful to small NGOs which encounter lack of adequate financial liquidity to initiate
activities. This form of disbursement may be the easiest, as it is direct without outside
intermediaries, dependent primarily on approval of an application, and then monitoring of
compliance. Other conditions which will likely be specified in an agreement are that only 20 percent
of a contract amount may be advanced, and subsequent payments can be made in tranches limited to
30 percent of the total size of the project.
16.   Operational rules must also reflect realistically the implementation capacity of the newly
established NATPOW as well as the NGOs which will be providing services. Since it will be a small
new organization with no operating history of its own, the NATPOW faces a risk of encountering
capacity problems at the outset. One potential difficulty is having to process an overwhelming
number of small claims, and treating them equally - as far as time spent on them is concerned - to
large applications. In this case, a possible remedy for the NATPOW is to establish a minimum of,
say, US$50,000 for consideration of NGO applications, as with the US$100,000 threshold for SAP
Project's PDP. This will allow it to focus administrative resources on major NGO undertakings
which require its full attention and supervision. It will also promote decentralization by way of a



216
Annex 15
Page 6 of 7
form of sub-contracting, as smaller NGOs will be permitted to pool their applications under an
umbrella organization in order to attain the US$50,000 prospective threshold.
17. Applicants for grants equivalent to US$50,000 or more would be required to meet the following
criteria: (a) demonstrated effectiveness in organizing and delivering community-based services on a
substantial scale and in diverse settings; (b) need for, and ability to make use of, additional funding;
(c) potential for replicability of the program on a larger scale; (d) capacity for working in partnership
with government social sector institutions; and (e) willingness to test and analyze the effectiveness of
different innovations.
18.    Such an approach is not without its risks, though. The prospective threshold of US$50,000
represents a large sum in Pakistan, and may well exclude many worthy applicants who have
significant needs which nevertheless come nowhere near to this amount. Domestic devaluations may
further aggravate this problem. The pooling option introduces a new layer of administration and
bureaucracy into the picture, potentially raising the operating costs and obstacles to be overcome by
the NGOs in the field. It may permanently exclude small but worthy NGOs in remote areas, with no
possibility of pooling with others (the proper focus should be increasing awareness of the existence of
the NATPOW as well as access, especially in remote areas where government and donor projects
have little effect or are missing). Another danger is that a threshlold may result in interest being
supply-driven, with NGOs inflating cost estimates, or else attracting sub-par partners within the
umbrella in order to meet the US$50,000 minimum.
19.    Despite the dangers, the sub-contracting option possesses merits, at least initially, as
NATPOW will be small, without regional offices, and probably lacking in the necessary
administrative capacity to deal effectively with many small organizations.
20.    One issue which seems to be particularly difficult is planniing a disbursement schedule since,
at present there is little basis to estimate the interest that exists in the field, the number of NGOs that
will be involved, or their capacity to absorb funds. Similarly, there is no way to estimate the real
institutional capacity of the NATPOW.
Procurement
21.    Local Competitive Bidding will be used for procurement where possible, and local shopping
as necessary. It is not possible to specify any more at this time as there is disparity of conditions and
needs in each region. Therefore, flexibility will be necessary; a 'framework agreement' will be made
establishing guidelines to ensure accountability.
22.    There will be semi-annual procurement audits, an annual technical audit to examine the
quality of the infrastructure being created, and an annual procurenment review. Standard IDA
monitoring and evaluation conditions, in cooperation with the Government's guidelines, would ensure
proper use of the funds as well as the continued input of the Government without the possibility of its
exercising undue influence.



217
Annex 15
Page 7 of 7
Management of the Program
23.   The Government has authorized the new National Trust for Population Welfare to manage the
fund. The new Trust will have a mandate which encompasses the objectives above, some of the
technical and managerial capacity required to manage the proposed program, though this is likely to
need strengthening, and to be already funding and supporting NGO population projects which are part
of the proposed NGO program.
24.   At the start of the project, it is expected that the Trust will prepare a management proposal
for agreement with IDA. The proposal will identify the objectives, responsibilities, tasks, and inputs
of the Trust in managing the PWPP NGO fund. On the basis of the management proposal, a
management fee will be agreed. This will include provision for technical assistance to assist in
appraisal and monitoring of projects considered and funded through the Trust. The Trust will submit
to IDA the first five grant proposals under the proposed Credit, and thereafter, all proposals for
grants for the equivalent of US$100,000 or more. NATPOW would approve all other grants on a
regular basis, and would keep records on the description and appraisal of the grant activity, including
a description of the expenditures proposed to be financed from the proceeds of the Credit. NATPOW
would retain this information and make it available for the project's regular reviews.
Monitoring and Evaluation
25.   Projects will be monitored by the Trust quarterly, with six-monthly progress reports prepared
by the implementing NGO. Programs administered by grant-making organizations will be monitored
on a quarterly basis by them, with six-monthly progress reports prepared by the implementing NGO.
The management agent will monitor progress jointly with the grant-making body every six months.
The PWPP NGO program and management of the fund will be monitored by Government and IDA
during PWPP supervision missions. A mid-term review will be held in 1997. An evaluation of the
PWPP program will be undertaken by Government and IDA at the end of the project.



218
Annex 16
Page I of 2
PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Procurement
Procurement Requirements
1.    Compared with other programs in the social sectors, the procurement requirements of the
population program are modest, confined to a very limited number of items typically required only in
small quantities and for use over a very wide geographical area. Both in the past, and for the future
program, the major item of procurement has always been contraceptives. Other key items are
vehicles, minor medical equipment and simple medicines. Historically, donors have provided a high
proportion of these requirements directly to the program with the Governmllenlt being mainly
responsible for logistics and distribution. Thus the federal Ministry of Population Welfare (MPW)
and the provincial Departments of Population Welfare (PWDs) direct involvement in and experience
of larger procurement packages, especially through internationial bidding, is limited.
Present Procurement Arrangements
2.     Currently, procurement is mainly undertaken by MPW; some limited procurement, especially
for day-to-day consumable items, is done by provincial PWDs. Within MPW. with its headquarters
in Islamabad, the Director General (Monitoring & Statistics) is overall responsible for procurement
warehousing and distribution of contraceptives, major equipment and vehicles. The MPW's Central
Warehouse, Karachi, is responsible for storage and distribution of contraceptives, including
monitoring, to program managers throughout the country. The procuremenlt ot technical assistance
(consultant services and foreign training) is undertaken within MPW jointly by the Director (Foreign
Assistance) and the Director General (Programmes). The procuremiient of civil works, furniture,
office equipment and stationery is done by MPW's Joint Secretary (Administration). The Director
General (Technical) in MPW  is responsible for procurement and maintenialice of clillical equipment
and clinical research. The procurement of medicines, largely a provincial responsibility, is
undertaken by the Directors (Technical) in the provincial PWDs while other procurciiiemet items in the
provinces are handled by the Directors (Monitoring, Evaluation and Statistics), except tor Balochistan
where it is carried out by the PWD's Administration and Finanicial Managemiienit Wing.
Local Procurement
3.     The federal and provincial units of MPW and the provincial PWDs responsible for
procurement are very conversant with Government procurement rules and regulations through local
competitive bidding. They have been implementing these rules for many years and IDA has
determined that, with attention to a few shortcomings generic to the implementation of the
Government's own rules, that these procedures are in compliance with IDA guidelilles. Both under
the Social Action Program Project and this project, staff training will be provided to address these



219
Annex 16
Page 2 of 2
shortcomings. With the small volume and limited nature of the procurement envisaged, any such
difficulties are expected to be very minor.
International Procurement
4.     Conversely, the limited experience of the MPW and provincial PWDs with international
competitive bidding (ICB) could present difficulties. The main potential problem area is the
procurement of contraceptives. During program and project preparation, it was agreed by all donors
and the Government, at the Government's suggestion, that UNFPA would be responsible for
procurement of all major consignments of contraceptives irrespective of funding source. This would
ensure efficient and cost-effective procurement and minimize the chance of stock-outs. To support
this initiative, UNFPA will provide long term technical assistance to MPW and the PWDs, to assist
with planning and undertaking procurement, and for staff and organizational development. Thus, the
direct burden of ICB procurement on MPW will be much reduced. For vehicles, the only other
goods which would be procured internationally, the requirements are well defined and draft
procurement documents have been approved. Each province should procure its own vehicles.
However, since the amounts are small and the provincial capacity limited, it would be more efficient
for MPW to coordinate the requirements annually, and undertake the procurement on behalf of the
provinces and the federal level jointly. One major technical assistance contract is anticipated (for
program monitoring, advice and operations research). MPW will be responsible for procurement.
Preparation of documentation and selection of consultants is underway. Major procedural problems
are not anticipated as the Ministry has some experience in this area.
Improved Procurement Organization
5.     Procurement responsibilities are fragmented within MPW, and to a lesser extent in the PWDs.
It is intended during the four years of the project to rationalize these functions in MPW into one
procurement unit or section. The existing staff should be sufficient. This change would facilitate all
types of procurement, and encourage greater exchange of procurement expertise within the Ministry.
With supporting technical assistance and training, this unit would work towards taking responsibility
for all procurement, including for contraceptives. Likewise, although the needs are not so great, the
provincial PWDs would be encouraged to set up consolidated procurement units.

PAKISTAN
POPULATION WELFARE PROGRAM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Project Supervision
1.    IDA's Resident Mission in Pakistan will monitor program and project progress throughout
particularly concentrating on the more detailed aspects of project implementation. For greater cost-
effectiveness in supervision, IDA will tap the expertise of the independent agency providing overall
support and technical assistance to the program (see Annex 11, Technical Assistance) and the resident
specialist on population who will be part of the Multi-Donor Support Unit for SAPP. This main technical
assistance agency would provide a twice-yearly supervision report, covering inputs and outputs, including
policy and institutional issues, and the agreed project performance indicators.
2.     Formal program and project supervision will be based on three missions per year: a main
mission in March/April, and two other missions in June/July and December/January.
3.    IDA would begin its supervision cycle with a main mission in March or April of each year to
review the draft Operational Plans being prepared for the coming fiscal year. It would coincide or be
synchronized with the similar mission for SAPP supervision. It would be the major supervision mission
of the annual cycle and would involve a review of MPW's and each PWD's budget, plan and
implementation reform proposals, as well as a review of progress over the previous three quarters (i.e.
since the beginning of the fiscal year). The Government will convene a donors meeting to coincide with
this mission.
4.     A second supervision mission would be a brief review of the Operational Plans (budgets and
reform measures) for population. It would take place around the start of the fiscal year (late June/July)
after the new fiscal year's allocations have been determined. If there are no major outstanding issues,
it may be possible to deal with these matters by correspondence rather than a formal mission.
5.     A third supervision mission, in December/January, would focus on technical issues, review mid-
year progress, and contribute IDA's views on issues to be addressed in preparation for the coming year's
Operational Plans. This mission would also check on the overall progress.
6.     Other donors in the population sector, such as ADB, ODA, UNFPA, the EU, and KfW would
join supervision missions as appropriate.
7.     During the first supervision mission in March/April 1995, a project launch workshop will be
organized. The workshop will reinforce earlier workshops and ensure that all officials concerned are
fully familiar with the details of the project and, additionally, will help the executing agencies to: (a)
produce a detailed work plan for the first year of implementation; (b) better understand IDA and
cooperating donor procurement, disbursement, and other procedures; and (c) review plans for monitoring
and evaluation.



222
Annex 18
Page 2 of 3
8.     A mid-term  review, carried out in the third year, will evaluate the program  and project
thoroughly, assess to what extent objectives are being met, identify changes needed to improve
effectiveness, and modify as necessary the project design and detailed content.
9.      A supervision plan is given below.
